🏡 Start Here
Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit
The Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit is a living workspace for organizing, connecting, and applying knowledge across regenerative web3, local coordination, public goods, ecosystem stewardship, knowledge commoning, and implementation learning.
It is designed for people who are not only trying to understand this field, but also trying to work inside it: community organizers, builders, researchers, knowledge gardeners, funders, local node stewards, ecosystem mappers, protocol designers, civic technologists, and people deeply exploring how regenerative systems can be coordinated in practice.
The Toolkit brings together several kinds of work:
* explaining concepts and frameworks
* mapping real projects, tools, organizations, people, events, and ecosystems
* preserving source lineage and attribution
* organizing reusable design options
* helping communities move from ideas into implementation
* capturing signals, failures, tensions, and lessons from practice
* supporting adaptive updates to the commons over time
At its simplest, the Toolkit helps people ask:
* What knowledge already exists?
* What people, projects, tools, communities, and resources are relevant?
* How do these pieces relate?
* What concepts or lineages are shaping the field?
* What options can communities use when designing their own systems?
* What needs to be made explicit before moving from ideas into action?
* What can be learned from real implementations?
* How can that learning update the commons?
What this workspace is for
This document is a shared structural workspace.
It is not a final specification, public pitch, product roadmap, or canonical architecture. It is the current working iteration of a larger knowledge-gardening process. Its purpose is to hold enough organized context that a group of serious collaborators can explore, critique, extend, reorganize, and improve the Toolkit together.
The document should be dense enough to support deep work, but structured enough that people can enter through different doors.
Some people may use it to understand the architecture.
Some may use it to contribute resources.
Some may use it to map adjacent ecosystems.
Some may use it to develop an ontology.
Some may use it to design a deployment process.
Some may use it to identify missing concepts, tools, cases, or questions.
Some may use it to connect this project with other knowledge commons, regenerative networks, civic infrastructure efforts, or web3 public goods systems.
The document is meant to be worked with, not merely read.
What the Toolkit is becoming
The Toolkit is currently imagined as a modular knowledge commons and coordination framework with several connected parts:
A structured knowledge commons
A place for concepts, explainers, frameworks, comparisons, guides, glossary entries, learning paths, open questions, and case-linked knowledge.
A Resource Graph and Ecosystem Atlas
A way to map real things in the world: projects, organizations, people, tools, protocols, events, maps, datasets, papers, source materials, communities, ecosystems, and implementation examples.
A Concept and Idea Ecology
A way to map not only entities, but also intellectual lineages, frameworks, paradigms, tensions, design principles, cultural references, and conceptual clusters shaping the field.
An Option Library
A structured design space for reusable choices such as governance / coordination patterns, funding mechanisms, documentation systems, discovery flows, contribution models, review systems, infrastructure patterns, and implementation approaches.
A Deployment and Structural Integrity layer
A way to help communities make structure explicit before moving from knowledge into action. This includes roles, decision paths, consent conditions, information asymmetries, power and control, adverse signals, stewardship obligations, review paths, and regenerative obligations.
An Implementation Memory layer
A place to capture what happens when ideas meet reality: pilots, local nodes, funding rounds, events, campaigns, knowledge gardens, resource maps, coordination processes, field projects, failures, adaptations, and lessons.
An Evolution layer
A way for signals, feedback, tensions, and implementation learning to update the commons over time.
A substrate-portable infrastructure view
A way to compare possible tools, schemas, publishing systems, graph structures, AI workflows, civic tech platforms, web3 protocols, and coordination substrates without prematurely locking into one stack.
Who this is for
Knowledge gardeners and researchers
People curating resources, writing explainers, mapping ecosystems, organizing concepts, preserving source lineage, building ontologies, creating synthesis notes, and maintaining shared memory.
Community organizers and local node stewards
People coordinating local groups, chapters, hubs, bioregional initiatives, public goods programs, regenerative projects, workshops, campaigns, and community infrastructure.
Builders and protocol designers
People developing tools, interfaces, schemas, graph systems, contribution flows, AI-assisted workflows, governance/coordination systems, funding mechanisms, attestations, or knowledge infrastructure.
Funders, reviewers, and ecosystem stewards
People are trying to understand what exists, identify promising work, evaluate proposals, support public goods, steward shared resources, or learn from implementation.
Advanced explorers
People who may not yet know where they fit, but are deeply interested in regenerative systems, web3, commons, civic innovation, ecological coordination, collective intelligence, localism, public goods, or knowledge commoning.
How to move through this document
This workspace is organized as a set of major tabs.
The early sections explain the posture and architecture.
The middle sections describe the layers, systems, maps, concepts, and options.
The later sections focus on deployment, implementation learning, infrastructure, Swarm context, and open questions.
A recommended reading path:
1. Start Here — understand what this workspace is.
2. Working Posture — understand how to interpret the current iteration.
3. Toolkit Architecture — understand the overall structure.
4. Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas — understand how the project maps reality.
5. Concept & Idea Ecology — understand how the project maps ideas and lineages.
6. Deployment & Structural Integrity — understand how the project moves toward practice.
7. Implementation Memory & Evolution — understand how the commons learns.
For contributors, the most useful entry points may be:
* add or classify a resource
* identify an important concept or framework
* add an ecosystem map lead
* improve a definition
* connect related ideas
* identify a failure mode
* add a case example
* propose an option
* improve the deployment checklist
* flag a structural risk
* add a question that should remain open
The core movement
The Toolkit is not meant to be a static archive.
It is designed around a recurring movement:
Knowledge → Resources → Options → Deployment → Implementation → Learning → Updated Knowledge
Or more simply:
Discover → Understand → Compose → Practice → Learn → Evolve
The purpose of the Toolkit is not only to collect information. It is to help knowledge become more findable, more connected, more attributable, more useful, and more capable of learning from practice.
Working thesis
Regenerative web3 and local coordination ecosystems are rich but fragmented.
There are many projects, tools, frameworks, communities, protocols, maps, funding mechanisms, ecological initiatives, governance experiments, and knowledge resources. But they are scattered across documents, chats, websites, GitHub repositories, forums, social media, maps, events, and private community spaces.
This fragmentation creates repeated work, lost context, weak attribution, poor discoverability, shallow synthesis, and difficulty moving from ideas into action.
The Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit exists to help turn that fragmentation into a more legible, interconnected, adaptive commons.
It does not need to become the center of the ecosystem.
It needs to help the ecosystem see, connect, compose, and learn from itself.


🦠 Working Posture
Current iteration
This document is the current working iteration of the Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit.
It is not a final architecture, fixed specification, public launch document, product roadmap, or canonical source of truth. It is a structured workspace for gathering context, organizing thinking, testing relationships, and supporting collaboration among knowledge gardeners, builders, researchers, community organizers, and ecosystem stewards.
The latest version is simply the latest version.
The goal is not to defend any section as final. The goal is to create enough structure that people can work with the material, improve it, add to it, challenge it, reorganize it, and connect it with other efforts.
Design posture
The Toolkit should remain:
* broad enough to hold a complex ecosystem
* structured enough to prevent collapse into a link dump
* modular enough to evolve without requiring every part to be rebuilt
* source-aware enough to preserve attribution and lineage
* practical enough to support real implementation
* critical enough to detect failure modes, risks, and hidden assumptions
* adaptive enough to learn from use
* generous enough to integrate with other communities, networks, and knowledge systems
The Toolkit should not try to become the single center of regenerative web3, local coordination, or knowledge commoning. It should help map, connect, interpret, and compose what already exists while also making room for new patterns to emerge.
How to treat this document
This document should be treated as a living workspace.
Sections may contain polished language, draft structure, open questions, candidate categories, unresolved distinctions, and speculative architecture. These should not be read as equal in maturity. Some sections are ready to use. Some are scaffolds. Some are placeholders for future gardening. Some are invitations for critique.
Readers and contributors should feel free to:
* add missing resources
* suggest better categories
* rename sections
* challenge assumptions
* propose alternate structures
* identify unnecessary complexity
* mark weak or unclear areas
* add concepts and lineages
* add implementation cases
* improve definitions
* connect related ideas
* flag risks and failure modes
* add source lineage
* recommend tools, frameworks, communities, and examples
The document should support active gardening.
Working assumptions
The current architecture is based on several assumptions.
Knowledge needs structure
Regenerative web3 and local coordination knowledge is too fragmented to remain only in chats, event notes, isolated docs, resource lists, or personal memory. Shared structure makes knowledge easier to find, connect, compare, and reuse.
Structure should not become rigidity
A useful knowledge commons needs coherence, but not premature closure. Categories should help people navigate and contribute, not freeze the field into a final map.
Resources are not enough
A list of links does not become a commons by itself. Resources need context, relationships, provenance, review status, and pathways into use.
Concepts and ideas need mapping too
Ecosystem mapping should not only include organizations, tools, projects, and people. It should also map concepts, lineages, paradigms, frameworks, practices, tensions, and open questions.
Design options need conditions
Governance models, funding mechanisms, documentation systems, impact tools, role structures, AI workflows, and infrastructure choices should not be treated as interchangeable menu items. Each option has conditions, risks, dependencies, failure modes, and contextual limits.
Implementation should update knowledge
A knowledge commons should learn from real projects, not only from theory or research. Field experience, failures, adaptations, tensions, and local context should feed back into the commons.
Source lineage is part of regenerative practice
The Toolkit should credit and preserve the context of the people, communities, organizations, and knowledge systems it learns from. Aggregation without attribution becomes extraction.
AI can help, but cannot replace stewardship
AI can assist with synthesis, classification, drafting, search, comparison, and pattern detection. But AI-generated or AI-assisted content needs human review, source lineage, and clear stewardship.
Structural integrity matters
When knowledge systems influence coordination, funding, community discovery, public claims, local implementation, or ecological work, structure matters. Roles, consent, power, decision paths, information asymmetries, adverse signals, and obligations should be made visible where relevant.
What should be avoided
The Toolkit should avoid becoming:
A flat link dump
A resource list without relationships, context, source lineage, or review status will become hard to use and maintain.
A polished but unreviewed authority layer
Well-written content can appear more trustworthy than it is. The document should avoid presenting draft synthesis, AI-assisted content, or weakly sourced claims as settled knowledge.
A centralized source of truth
The Toolkit should support a federated knowledge commons, not replace the knowledge systems of other communities.
A platform fantasy
The current document should not assume that every need requires new software. Some needs may be solved through better documentation, shared schemas, contribution practices, lightweight templates, existing tools, or social processes.
A purely conceptual map
The project should stay connected to real tools, people, communities, implementations, and field conditions.
An extractive aggregator
The Toolkit should not absorb other communities’ work without attribution, context, permission where needed, and visible return paths.
An overbuilt architecture
Complexity is useful only when it helps people think, contribute, coordinate, or act. If a distinction does not improve use, stewardship, interoperability, or learning, it should be questioned.
A deployment shortcut
Reading about a governance model, funding mechanism, impact claim, or local coordination pattern is not the same as being ready to use it. Deployment requires context, roles, consent, review, and structural clarity.
Maturity language
Because this is a working document, content may exist in different states.
Suggested lightweight states:
State
	Meaning
	Raw
	Added for visibility, not yet organized.
	To-place
	Useful but not yet located in the right section.
	Draft
	Developed enough to discuss, not fully reviewed.
	Candidate
	Likely useful, but needs validation, source review, or testing.
	Reviewed
	Checked by at least one relevant human reviewer.
	Field-informed
	Informed by implementation or real-world use.
	Pattern-generating
	Has produced reusable lessons across contexts.
	Deprecated
	Kept for memory, but no longer recommended or current.
	Open question
	Intentionally unresolved.
	Source system
	A living external knowledge environment, map, wiki, repository, directory, or curated body of work.
	These states are not meant to create bureaucracy. They help contributors understand how much trust to place in a section, resource, option, or claim.
Contribution posture
A useful contribution can be small.
Examples:
* adding one missing resource
* naming one important concept
* correcting one definition
* adding source lineage
* linking two related sections
* identifying a better category
* adding an implementation example
* naming an anti-pattern
* adding a question
* flagging a risk
* explaining why a section is confusing
* pointing to an existing tool or community doing similar work
The Toolkit should make it easy for people to contribute at different depths.
Not every contributor needs to understand the full architecture.
Not every contribution needs to become a polished page.
Not every resource needs to be fully classified immediately.
Not every open question needs to be resolved.
The commons improves through many small acts of gardening.
Relationship to adjacent communities
The Toolkit should be porous.
It should learn from and connect with adjacent networks, knowledge gardens, civic infrastructure efforts, commons projects, regenerative communities, web3 public goods ecosystems, localist movements, AI sensemaking experiments, ecological monitoring systems, and public knowledge initiatives.
Some adjacent communities may provide concepts.
Some may provide tools.
Some may provide source systems.
Some may provide implementation cases.
Some may provide critiques.
Some may provide lineage.
Some may provide infrastructure.
Some may reveal that parts of this Toolkit are unnecessary.
The goal is not to absorb these communities. The goal is to map relationships, preserve attribution, and make collaboration more legible.
Relationship to the Knowledge Commoning Swarm
The Knowledge Commoning Swarm is an important collaboration context for this iteration.
This document includes a dedicated Swarm section. Swarm-specific notes should mostly live there, rather than being repeated throughout every layer.
The Toolkit enters that context as one domain-specific working architecture: a way to explore how regenerative web3, local coordination, public goods, ecosystem stewardship, and implementation learning might be organized as a knowledge commons.
The Swarm may help identify:
* existing components worth reusing
* better language
* missing source systems
* shared schemas
* clearer component categories
* useful patterns
* unnecessary complexity
* infrastructure already solving parts of the problem
* collaborators and knowledge gardeners
* ways to improve this architecture
The Toolkit should remain open to that learning without reshaping itself entirely around any single event or group.
Working principle
This document should help the ecosystem become more legible to itself.
That means mapping what exists, explaining what matters, preserving where knowledge came from, showing how ideas relate, naming practical options, making structural risks visible, and learning from implementation.
The goal is not finality.
The goal is better commoning.

Misc GPT recs - Matt May 6th


1. Add a Frame Language audit layer
The article is directly relevant because it says vocabulary mismatch is the canary: when intent and available language diverge, the vocabulary decides the structure by default. Your Toolkit should therefore add a lightweight Frame Language Audit before or inside the Ontology / Deployment layers.
For each important term, ask:
What structure does this term secretly import?
What possibilities does it make visible?
What possibilities does it remove?
Does this term match the regenerative intent of the Toolkit?
Is it bridge language for accessibility, or structural language for design?
Where should the more precise term be used?
Priority terms to audit: governance, accountability, public goods, community, ecosystem, impact, stakeholder, contributor, steward, local node, regenerative, evidence, claim, implementation, resource, commons, participation, coordination, legitimacy.
Your existing bridge-language table is a great start. The next move is to make it procedural: every major template should include a “language risk” or “frame inheritance” field.
2. Define the minimum viable operating kernel
The architecture has eight layers and many cross-cutting systems. That is conceptually useful, but early contributors need a tiny kernel.
I would reduce v0.1 operations to five objects:
Resource — something found.
Concept — something explained.
Option — something reusable.
Deployment — something used in context.
Signal — something learned or flagged.
Everything else can be metadata, view, or template until the system proves it needs more. This protects the Toolkit from becoming structurally elegant but socially hard to use.
3. Convert templates into actual decision rules
The templates are good, but many are still open-ended. The next iteration should define routing rules like:
A resource becomes a Source System when it is a living external knowledge environment, not a single artifact.
A resource becomes an Encyclopedia candidate when it explains a recurring concept used across multiple entries.
An option becomes Deployment-ready only when conditions, risks, roles, and failure modes are filled.
A case becomes Pattern-generating only when it has repeated signals or transferable conditions.
A claim becomes high-risk when it involves ecological, financial, governance, identity, or public representation claims.
The doc already says “a case is not automatically a pattern” and “a signal is not automatically a conclusion.” Now those distinctions need explicit thresholds.
4. Make “named response” real
The Toolkit correctly says detection is not enough and that structural gaps require a named response. This is one of the most important CSIS-aligned ideas in the document.
But the next iteration should specify response types:
Disclose — proceed, but make the limitation visible.
Review — route to a named reviewer or review type.
Pause — do not publish or deploy until resolved.
Narrow scope — reduce visibility, claims, or affected parties.
Deprecate — keep for memory but mark as not recommended.
Escalate — route to steward group, community review, ecological review, legal review, or conflict path.
Convert to open question — preserve uncertainty without pretending resolution.
This turns structural integrity from a value into an operating rule.
5. Add stewardship roles before adding more content
The document names many functions, but the people/role architecture is still implicit. For v0.1, you probably need only a few roles:
Knowledge gardener — adds and classifies resources.
Source steward — checks provenance and attribution.
Concept steward — improves definitions and bridge/structural language.
Structural reviewer — checks deployment risks, power, consent, adverse signal paths.
Implementation scribe — captures cases, retrospectives, signals, and pattern candidates.
Maintainer — keeps the substrate clean, prunes, merges, archives.
Without roles, the Toolkit risks becoming dependent on hidden labor, which the document itself identifies as a structural weakness.
6. Separate “commons language” from “interface language”
Some of the language is powerful but dense: structural floors, generative capacities, adverse signals, obligation direction, source systems, implementation memory, evolution routing, etc.
That language is correct for the inner architecture, but the public interface needs gentler entry points. Keep two layers of language:
Interface language: Find resources, understand concepts, compare options, plan a deployment, capture learning.
Structural language: Source lineage, maturity status, obligation direction, adverse signal path, structural exposure, named response.
This prevents the project from becoming inaccessible while preserving precision where it matters.
7. Make implementation memory the forcing function
Right now, the Resource Graph feels like the obvious first build. But the deeper differentiator is Implementation Memory. Many ecosystems already have maps, wikis, lists, and directories. Fewer have a disciplined way to learn from attempts, failures, adaptations, and field signals.
The doc says Implementation Memory should capture what was attempted, who participated, what context mattered, what worked, what failed, what confused people, what created friction, what assumptions changed, and what should update the Toolkit. That should become a primary interface, not a later layer.
A strong move would be to start with 3–5 implementation cases from your own ecosystem: Regen Rio, GreenGoods, Gardens/GG23, Cookie Jar Research Raid, GreenPill Brasil local coordination. These will test whether the categories are real.
8. Add “claim-evidence discipline” earlier
The Toolkit is likely to touch public goods, ecological impact, funding, local coordination, and web3 attestations. These domains are full of overclaiming risk.
The deployment checklist already asks what claims are being made, what evidence supports them, who reviewed them, what uncertainty remains, and what should not be claimed publicly. This should be promoted into a core pattern: every resource, option, and implementation case should separate:
claim
evidence
interpretation
uncertainty
review state
public-use boundary
That single discipline would make the Toolkit much more trustworthy.
My overall assessment
I would score this iteration as:
Conceptual architecture: 8.5/10
 Very strong. The layers, distinctions, and posture are coherent.
CSIS / structural integrity alignment: 7.5/10
 Strongly aligned in spirit and language, but not yet procedural enough to be assessed. The doc wisely says it is CSIS-informed, not CSIS-conformant.
Contributor usability: 5.5/10
 The doc is rich, but still heavy. It needs a smaller operating kernel, clearer first actions, and contributor-facing pathways.
Implementation readiness: 5/10
 Good templates exist, but there are not yet enough named roles, thresholds, routing rules, stewardship loops, or tested cases.
Strategic uniqueness: 8/10
 The combination of Resource Graph + Concept Ecology + Option Library + Deployment Integrity + Implementation Memory is distinctive. The Toolkit becomes much more interesting if it leads with learning from practice, not just ecosystem mapping.
The next best iteration
The next version should not primarily be “more nested.” It should be more decidable.
I would make the next pass around this question:
Can a new contributor add one article, classify it, preserve lineage, route it, flag risks, and know what happens next in under 10 minutes?
That matches the doc’s own success condition for the Minimum Viable Resource Graph.
The practical next sprint should produce:
1. A one-page contributor guide.
2. A five-object minimum ontology: Resource, Concept, Option, Deployment, Signal.
3. A Frame Language audit table for the top 20 terms.
4. A Resource Graph v0.1 spreadsheet with 25–50 entries.
5. A Source System Registry v0.1.
6. Three full Option entries.
7. Three Implementation Memory cases.
8. One Structural Exposure Card example.
9. A routing decision tree.
10. A changelog / evolution log.
The deepest improvement is this: make the language itself part of the infrastructure. The article’s warning is that vocabulary quietly builds the structure before builders notice. Your Toolkit already senses that. The next iteration should make vocabulary review a formal practice, not just a conceptual section.

🍃 First Working Pass / Next Actions
Purpose
This section turns the master workspace into a working plan.
The Toolkit now has enough structure to support collaborative gardening. The next step is not to finalize everything. The next step is to decide what needs attention first, who can garden which areas, and what outputs would make the Toolkit more useful.
This tab helps contributors move from reading to action.
It should be updated regularly as sections improve, questions are answered, resources are classified, and implementation signals emerge.
________________


1. Immediate Working Priorities
Priority 1 — Clean and structure the Resource Graph
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas is the largest and most important working area.
Next actions:
* move resource entries into a structured table
* identify source systems vs individual resources
* classify entries by type
* classify entries by domain
* classify entries by function
* add Toolkit relevance
* add source lineage
* mark status: raw, candidate, source system, reviewed, to-review, etc.
* identify duplicate or outdated entries
* identify resources that should become concept pages, option entries, implementation cases, or infrastructure candidates
Suggested output:
Resource Graph v0.1 table
Minimum fields:
Field
	Purpose
	Name
	Resource or entity name
	Link
	URL or citation
	Type
	Tool, org, person, source system, framework, event, etc.
	Domain
	Commons, ReFi, civic tech, ecology, AI, funding, etc.
	Function
	Publish, map, fund, verify, coordinate, sensemake, etc.
	Related concepts
	Key ideas connected to the entry
	Toolkit relevance
	Which tabs or layers it informs
	Source lineage
	Where it came from / how to credit
	Status
	Raw, candidate, source system, reviewed, to-review
	Notes
	Caveats, risks, open questions
	________________

* note which source systems should be revisited regularly
Candidate source-system types:
* wikis
* public forums
* GitHub repositories
* resource libraries
* curated maps
* event archives
* knowledge gardens
* standards repositories
* community directories
* learning hubs
* research databases

Source System Registry v0.1

	Link
	URL
	Type
	Wiki, map, repo, forum, garden, directory, etc.
	Steward

2. Suggested Gardening Roles
These are working roles, not formal positions.
Resource Gardener
Focus:
* adding resources
* cleaning entries
* adding links
* classifying by type/domain/function
* identifying source systems
* removing duplicates
* flagging outdated material
Useful outputs:
* Resource Graph table
* source-system registry
* loose-leads cleanup
Concept Gardener
Focus:
* improving definitions
* creating concept stubs

* identifying lineages

* source-system cards

Option Librarian

* option-to-tool maps
* option risk notes

* improving deployment templates

* developing structural exposure cards

* identifying failures and adaptations
* routing lessons back into the Toolkit

* retrospective notes
* pattern candidates
* anti-pattern entries
Infrastructure Gardener

* comparing tools

* high-risk flags
* reviewer notes

This is how the master workspace becomes useful as a living commons.


⚒️ Toolkit Architecture
Overview
The Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit is organized as a layered knowledge-to-action system.
Its purpose is to help people move from fragmented knowledge into structured understanding, from understanding into practical composition, from composition into implementation, and from implementation back into shared learning.
The architecture has three main movements:
1. Map the field
 Identify concepts, resources, people, projects, tools, organizations, practices, ideas, frameworks, and relationships.
2. Compose from what exists
 Turn mapped knowledge and resources into reusable options, design patterns, deployment scaffolds, and coordination structures.
3. Learn from practice
 Capture signals, failures, adaptations, evidence, and lessons from real implementations so the commons can evolve.
The architecture should be treated as a working model, not a fixed system. Its value is in helping people organize complexity, see relationships, ask better questions, and improve the commons over time.
Core architecture
The Toolkit currently has eight core layers:
   1. Ontology Layer
   2. Encyclopedia Layer
   3. Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
   4. Concept & Idea Ecology
   5. Option Library
   6. Deployment & Structural Integrity
   7. Implementation Memory
   8. Evolution Layer
These layers are supported by several cross-cutting systems:
   * Provenance & Attribution
   * Review & Maturity
   * Privacy, Consent & Identity
   * Standards & Interoperability
   * Discovery & Affinity
   * Infrastructure & Substrate
   * Interface & Navigation
   * Synthesis & Sensemaking
   * Regenerative Obligation
The layers describe what the Toolkit does.
The cross-cutting systems describe what the Toolkit must preserve across everything it does.
The knowledge-to-action loop
The Toolkit is designed around a recurring loop:
Ontology → Knowledge → Resources → Ideas → Options → Deployment → Implementation → Evolution → Updated Knowledge
A simpler version:
Discover → Understand → Connect → Compose → Practice → Learn → Evolve
This loop does not mean every user must move through every step.
Some people may only explore concepts.
Some may only add resources.
Some may only map an ecosystem.
Some may only compare tools.
Some may only use deployment templates.
Some may only contribute implementation reflections.
Some may only help with review, synthesis, or stewardship.
The loop exists so that the system does not become a static archive. It gives the commons a way to learn.
Layer 1 — Ontology Layer
The Ontology Layer defines the shared semantic structure of the Toolkit.
It helps clarify what kinds of things exist in the system and how they relate.
Examples:
   * concept
   * framework
   * tool
   * protocol
   * organization
   * person
   * community
   * project
   * event
   * source system
   * option
   * pattern
   * implementation
   * signal
   * evidence
   * claim
   * place
   * role
   * obligation
   * open question
The Ontology Layer helps prevent semantic drift.
It distinguishes between:
   * a type
   * a tag
   * a specific entity
   * a relationship
   * a classification
   * a claim
   * a resource
   * a pattern
This matters because a knowledge commons can become confusing when specific entities are treated as categories, tags are treated as types, tools are treated as concepts, or resource lists are treated as knowledge.
The Ontology Layer is listed as a layer, but it also acts as a semantic kernel across the whole Toolkit.
Layer 2 — Encyclopedia Layer
The Encyclopedia Layer holds structured explanatory knowledge.
It includes:
   * concept pages
   * framework pages
   * glossary entries
   * comparisons
   * explainers
   * guides
   * learning paths
   * open questions
   * anti-pattern pages
   * case-linked knowledge
The Encyclopedia helps people understand the field.
It answers questions like:
   * What is a knowledge commons?
   * What is regenerative web3?
   * What is a resource graph?
   * What is an attestation?
   * What is MRV?
   * What is a local node?
   * What is structural integrity?
   * What is source lineage?
   * What is the difference between governance and coordination?
   * What is the difference between an option and a deployment?
The Encyclopedia should remain broad, but visibly uneven. Some pages may be polished. Some may be drafts. Some may be open questions. Some may be AI-assisted. Some may need review. That is acceptable as long as maturity is visible.
Layer 3 — Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas grounds the Toolkit in reality.
It maps real entities and artifacts:
   * organizations
   * networks
   * people
   * projects
   * tools
   * protocols
   * events
   * maps
   * datasets
   * articles
   * papers
   * books
   * communities
   * source systems
   * implementation examples
   * local and bioregional references
This layer asks:
   * What exists?
   * Who made it?
   * What does it do?
   * What concepts does it relate to?
   * What function does it serve?
   * What ecosystem is it part of?
   * What other resources does it connect to?
   * What would make it reusable?
   * What should be credited?
   * What needs review?
   * What risks or limits should be visible?
This layer should not be a flat resource list. It should become an ecosystem atlas with multiple views: entities, functions, concepts, relationships, source lineage, places, tools, communities, events, options, and implementation evidence.
Layer 4 — Concept & Idea Ecology
The Concept & Idea Ecology maps the intellectual and conceptual field.
This layer exists because ecosystems are not only made of organizations and tools. They are also made of ideas, language, lineages, tensions, paradigms, metaphors, design principles, and assumptions.
It includes:
   * intellectual lineages
   * conceptual clusters
   * movement language
   * paradigms
   * frameworks
   * tensions
   * open questions
   * bridge language
   * structural language
   * regenerative design principles
   * civic innovation ideas
   * commons theory
   * web3 public goods concepts
   * localism and bioregional concepts
   * collective intelligence concepts
   * ecological and systems concepts
This layer helps the Toolkit ask:
   * What ideas are shaping the field?
   * Where did those ideas come from?
   * Which concepts are being used differently by different communities?
   * Which terms are bridge language?
   * Which terms need structural precision?
   * Which lineages should be credited?
   * Which concepts are useful but contested?
   * Which ideas are missing from our map?
The Concept & Idea Ecology prevents ecosystem mapping from becoming only institutional mapping.
Layer 5 — Option Library
The Option Library organizes reusable design choices.
Options are not the same as tools.
Options are not the same as concepts.
Options are not the same as full deployments.
An option is a reusable pattern, mechanism, structure, or design choice that a community might select, adapt, compare, or reject.
Examples:
   * source lineage field
   * review queue
   * public resource map
   * requests and offers board
   * local node model
   * contribution pathway
   * community profile
   * consent-based decision process
   * quadratic funding
   * conviction voting
   * milestone-based grants
   * public impact reporting
   * human-in-the-loop AI review
   * ecological evidence checklist
   * knowledge garden publishing flow
   * opt-in discovery profile
   * structural exposure card
Each option should eventually include:
   * what problem it addresses
   * when it is useful
   * when it is not useful
   * required conditions
   * related tools
   * related concepts
   * related examples
   * risks
   * failure modes
   * consent concerns
   * power or control concerns
   * maintenance burden
   * review status
   * source lineage
The Option Library makes design space visible without pretending every option is appropriate in every context.
Layer 6 — Deployment & Structural Integrity
The Deployment & Structural Integrity layer helps communities move from ideas into action.
This layer asks what must be explicit before something becomes operational.
It includes:
   * purpose
   * scope
   * roles
   * decision paths
   * coordination structure
   * source of truth
   * information requirements
   * information asymmetries
   * consent conditions
   * power and control points
   * stewardship obligations
   * adverse signal channels
   * review pathways
   * implementation boundaries
   * maintenance responsibilities
   * escalation paths
   * structural risks
   * regenerative obligations
This layer is strongly informed by structural integrity thinking, including CSIS. CSIS should be treated as a serious reference for detecting weak structures, but the Toolkit should avoid claiming conformance unless explicit criteria and review processes are defined.
The key distinction:
Detection is not enough.
If a structural gap is detected, the Toolkit should help define what happens next:
   * Who sees it?
   * Who is responsible for response?
   * Is the deployment paused, revised, disclosed, or allowed to proceed?
   * What remains unresolved?
   * What must be revisited later?
Deployment is where attractive ideas become accountable to context.
Layer 7 — Implementation Memory
Implementation Memory captures what happens in practice.
This layer includes:
   * local nodes
   * funding rounds
   * knowledge gardens
   * resource maps
   * ecosystem mapping projects
   * hackathons
   * research sprints
   * governance / coordination processes
   * public goods programs
   * regenerative field projects
   * workshops
   * community campaigns
   * civic partnerships
   * experiments
   * failures
   * adaptations
   * retrospectives
Implementation Memory should preserve more than success stories.
It should capture:
   * what was attempted
   * who participated
   * what context mattered
   * what worked
   * what failed
   * what confused people
   * what created friction
   * what signals emerged
   * what assumptions changed
   * what should be repeated
   * what should not be repeated
   * what remains unresolved
This layer is how the Toolkit stays grounded in reality.
Layer 8 — Evolution Layer
The Evolution Layer defines how the Toolkit learns and updates.
It receives signals from:
   * contributors
   * users
   * implementations
   * ecosystem changes
   * new resources
   * structural reviews
   * failed assumptions
   * open questions
   * conflicts
   * field experience
   * infrastructure experiments
It helps decide:
   * What needs updating?
   * What should be reclassified?
   * What should be deprecated?
   * What should become a pattern?
   * What should remain an open question?
   * What needs human review?
   * What reveals structural risk?
   * What reveals regenerative or extractive drift?
   * What should be added to the commons?
Evolution is not a mechanical feedback pipeline. It requires synthesis, interpretation, stewardship, and memory.
A signal does not automatically become an update.
A complaint does not automatically become a rule.
A success does not automatically become a pattern.
A failure does not automatically become a rejection.
The Evolution Layer helps the commons learn without losing coherence.
Cross-cutting systems
The cross-cutting systems operate across all layers.
Provenance & Attribution
Preserves where knowledge came from, who created it, how it is being used, and how sources should be credited.
Review & Maturity
Makes the state of content, resources, options, templates, claims, and implementation learnings visible.
Privacy, Consent & Identity
Protects people and communities when profiles, locations, interests, needs, offers, contribution histories, or implementation cases are represented.
Standards & Interoperability
Supports metadata, schemas, relation types, export formats, source lineage fields, and crosswalks with other systems.
Discovery & Affinity
Helps people find relevant topics, resources, communities, collaborators, requests, offers, opportunities, and learning paths.
Infrastructure & Substrate
Compares possible technical components and support systems without prematurely locking into one stack.
Interface & Navigation
Defines how people actually move through the Toolkit.
Synthesis & Sensemaking
Turns messy inputs, notes, signals, conversations, resources, and implementation experiences into interpreted learning.
Regenerative Obligation
Keeps attention on obligations toward contributors, source communities, local communities, cost-bearing parties, ecosystems, future maintainers, and affected places.
Interface between layers
The Toolkit depends on clear interfaces between layers.
Ontology ↔ Encyclopedia
The Ontology provides shared types and relationships.
The Encyclopedia gives those structures human-readable explanation.
Encyclopedia ↔ Resource Graph
The Encyclopedia explains concepts.
The Resource Graph links those concepts to real people, projects, tools, organizations, and source materials.
Resource Graph ↔ Concept & Idea Ecology
The Resource Graph maps entities.
The Concept & Idea Ecology maps ideas, lineages, tensions, and paradigms.
Resource Graph ↔ Option Library
The Resource Graph identifies tools, projects, protocols, and examples.
The Option Library turns reusable design choices into comparable options.
Option Library ↔ Deployment
The Option Library shows what can be selected.
Deployment asks what must be true for those choices to work in context.
Deployment ↔ Implementation Memory
Deployment defines structure.
Implementation Memory records what happened when the structure met reality.
Implementation Memory ↔ Evolution
Implementation generates signals.
Evolution interprets those signals and routes updates back into the Toolkit.
Evolution ↔ All layers
Evolution updates concepts, resources, options, templates, classifications, infrastructure notes, review states, and open questions.
Minimum architectural rules
The Toolkit should preserve several distinctions.
A resource is not an explanation
A link, project, tool, or article may support knowledge, but it is not the same as an Encyclopedia entry.
A concept is not a tool
A concept explains meaning. A tool performs a function.
A tool is not an option
A tool may instantiate or support an option, but an option is the reusable design choice.
An option is not a deployment
A deployment is a context-specific composition with roles, obligations, decisions, consent, review, and operating conditions.
A tag is not a type
Tags help filter and group. Types define what kind of thing something is.
A case is not automatically a pattern
A case becomes pattern-generating only when lessons are transferable, repeated, or clearly bounded by context.
A signal is not automatically a conclusion
Signals require interpretation before they update the commons.
AI output is not automatically knowledge
AI-assisted content needs source lineage, review, and visible maturity status.
Aggregation is not commoning
A commons requires stewardship, attribution, context, contribution pathways, review, and shared memory.
Structural floors and generative capacities
The Toolkit should distinguish between two kinds of design needs.
Structural floors
Structural floors are minimum conditions that reduce confusion, hidden power, exploitation, and coordination failure.
Examples:
   * clear definitions
   * visible roles
   * source of truth
   * decision paths
   * consent boundaries
   * source lineage
   * review pathways
   * information asymmetry awareness
   * power and control visibility
   * adverse signal channels
   * fixed / configurable / experimental boundaries
Generative capacities
Generative capacities allow a system to learn, adapt, repair, and remain alive.
Examples:
   * shared sensemaking
   * trust-building
   * conflict transformation
   * contributor care
   * implementation learning
   * regenerative obligation
   * local adaptation
   * memory
   * pattern harvesting
   * capacity awareness
   * evolutionary updates
A system with only structural floors can become rigid.
A system with only generative capacities can become vague or dependent on hidden labor.
The Toolkit should help hold both.
Architecture summary
The Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit is a living architecture for organizing knowledge, mapping ecosystems, composing options, supporting implementation, and learning from practice.
Its core pattern is:
Map what exists.
Explain what matters.
Connect what relates.
Compare what can be used.
Make structure explicit.
Learn from what happens.
Update the commons.


🌳 Core Layers
Purpose of the core layers
The core layers describe the main functional architecture of the Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit.
They help separate different kinds of work so the Toolkit does not collapse into a flat wiki, link list, project directory, or abstract framework.
Each layer has a distinct role:
Layer
	Primary function
	Ontology
	Defines shared types, relationships, and semantic structure.
	Encyclopedia
	Explains concepts, frameworks, guides, comparisons, and open questions.
	Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
	Maps real entities, resources, projects, tools, people, organizations, events, and ecosystems.
	Concept & Idea Ecology
	Maps concepts, lineages, paradigms, tensions, frameworks, and movement language.
	Option Library
	Organizes reusable design choices, patterns, mechanisms, and components.
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Makes structure explicit before action.
	Implementation Memory
	Captures what happens in practice.
	Evolution
	Routes learning back into the commons.
	A layer should not absorb another layer’s function unless the interface is clear.
________________


1. Ontology Layer
Function
The Ontology Layer provides the shared semantic structure of the Toolkit.
It defines what kinds of things exist, how they relate, and what distinctions need to remain coherent across the system.
The Ontology helps contributors avoid confusion between:
   * types
   * tags
   * entities
   * concepts
   * resources
   * options
   * patterns
   * tools
   * deployments
   * claims
   * signals
   * evidence
   * implementation cases
The Ontology should remain small enough to use, but expressive enough to support the complexity of the field.
What belongs here
The Ontology Layer includes:
   * entity types
   * relationship types
   * classification fields
   * metadata fields
   * type / tag distinctions
   * entity / concept distinctions
   * graph-compatible structures
   * interoperability constraints
   * mappings to external schemas or vocabularies
   * definitions of core objects used across the Toolkit
Example object types
Possible core object types:
   * Concept
   * Framework
   * Practice
   * Pattern
   * Tool
   * Protocol
   * Standard
   * Template
   * Resource
   * Source System
   * Organization
   * Network
   * Community
   * Person
   * Project
   * Event
   * Place
   * Option
   * Deployment
   * Implementation
   * Claim
   * Evidence
   * Signal
   * Open Question
   * Failure Mode
These should be treated as working types, not final categories.
Design constraints
Keep the core small
The ontology should avoid too many root types. If something can be represented as a tag, subtype, relationship, or metadata field, it may not need to become a root type.
Preserve type / tag distinction
A type defines what kind of thing something is.
A tag helps classify, filter, or group something.
Example:
   * Type: Tool
   * Tags: ReFi, mapping, open-source, governance, local coordination
Preserve entity / concept distinction
A specific organization, tool, or person should not usually become a category.
Example:
   * Entity: Bloom Network
   * Concepts: local regeneration, community weaving, place-based coordination, regenerative culture
Support interoperability
The ontology should be able to map to external systems where useful, such as metadata standards, knowledge graphs, civic data schemas, DAO standards, open knowledge systems, and other knowledge commons architectures.
Support evolution
The ontology should be updated when use reveals unclear distinctions, missing relationships, overloaded terms, or unnecessary categories.
Useful relationship types
General relationships:
   * is a
   * part of
   * related to
   * depends on
   * supports
   * uses
   * references
   * cites
   * adapts
   * derived from
   * conflicts with
Knowledge relationships:
   * defines
   * explains
   * compares
   * challenges
   * has evidence
   * has open question
   * supports claim
   * contradicts claim
Ecosystem relationships:
   * created by
   * maintained by
   * stewarded by
   * used by
   * active in
   * partnered with
   * located in
   * relevant to
Option and deployment relationships:
   * enables
   * requires
   * constrains
   * compatible with
   * incompatible with
   * implemented by
   * configured as
   * reviewed by
Evolution relationships:
   * generates signal
   * flags
   * updates
   * improves
   * deprecates
   * tests
   * resolves
   * remains unresolved
Discovery relationships:
   * interested in
   * skilled in
   * needs
   * offers
   * requests
   * available for
   * wants to learn
   * wants to contribute
   * consented for contact
   * consented for matching
Lightweight ontology entry template
Field
	Description
	Name
	Term or object type.
	Definition
	Plain-language meaning.
	Object type
	Concept, tool, option, implementation, etc.
	Parent type
	Broader type, if any.
	Related types
	Adjacent types.
	Allowed relationships
	Relationships this object can have.
	Examples
	Concrete instances.
	Common confusions
	What this is often mistaken for.
	Source lineage
	Where the term or structure comes from.
	Maturity status
	Raw, draft, reviewed, deprecated, etc.
	Working goal
The Ontology Layer should make the Toolkit easier to connect, search, classify, and evolve without making contribution overly burdensome.
It should provide enough shared structure for interoperability, but not so much structure that people stop contributing.
________________


2. Encyclopedia Layer
Function
The Encyclopedia Layer is the Toolkit’s structured knowledge commons.
It explains concepts, frameworks, domains, comparisons, guides, glossary entries, learning paths, open questions, and case-linked knowledge.
Its role is to help people move from fragmented exposure to shared understanding.
What belongs here
The Encyclopedia includes:
   * concept pages
   * framework pages
   * comparison pages
   * glossary entries
   * domain overviews
   * guide pages
   * learning paths
   * open-question pages
   * failure and anti-pattern pages
   * case-linked knowledge pages
What does not primarily belong here
The Encyclopedia should not absorb every kind of information.
Item
	Primary home
	Raw link or resource
	Resource Graph
	Specific tool profile
	Resource Graph
	Reusable design choice
	Option Library
	Deployment checklist
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Specific project case
	Implementation Memory
	Formal schema definition
	Ontology
	Feedback from use
	Evolution
	The Encyclopedia can link to these things, but its main job is explanation and synthesis.
Page types
Concept page
Explains a single concept.
Examples:
   * Knowledge commons
   * Regenerative web3
   * Source lineage
   * Local node
   * Information asymmetry
   * Public goods
   * Attestations
   * MRV
Suggested fields:
   * definition
   * why it matters
   * common confusions
   * related concepts
   * related resources
   * related options
   * related cases
   * source lineage
   * maturity status
Framework page
Explains a structured model or framework.
Examples:
   * CSIS
   * Cynefin
   * Ostrom’s commons principles
   * Doughnut Economics
   * Regenerative Development
   * Viable System Model
   * Pattern Language
   * Theory of Change
Suggested fields:
   * what the framework is
   * where it came from
   * what it helps interpret
   * where it is useful
   * limits and critiques
   * related Toolkit layers
   * related cases
   * source lineage
Comparison page
Clarifies differences between adjacent ideas.
Examples:
   * Taxonomy vs ontology
   * Governance vs coordination
   * Transparency vs multi-directional visibility
   * Accountability vs stewardship obligation
   * Resource Graph vs Encyclopedia
   * Option vs component vs deployment
   * Quadratic Funding vs RetroPGF
Suggested fields:
   * what is being compared
   * key differences
   * overlaps
   * when each term is useful
   * common misunderstandings
   * related options or cases
Guide page
Helps people use part of the Toolkit or understand a process.
Examples:
   * How to add a resource
   * How to preserve source lineage
   * How to classify an option
   * How to use the Toolkit as a local node
   * How to review AI-assisted content
   * How to move from resource discovery to deployment
Failure or anti-pattern page
Preserves recurring risks and breakdowns.
Examples:
   * Extractive aggregation
   * Governance theater
   * Documentation decay
   * Local node drift
   * Hidden multisig control
   * Token incentive distortion
   * AI-assisted flattening
   * Measurement theater
Suggested fields:
   * pattern name
   * symptoms
   * structural causes
   * examples
   * related concepts
   * related deployment checks
   * mitigation patterns
Open-question page
Preserves uncertainty without forcing premature closure.
Examples:
   * What should a knowledge commons assembly process ask?
   * How can discovery be useful without becoming surveillance?
   * What does CSIS alignment mean for a knowledge commons?
   * How should AI-assisted knowledge be reviewed?
   * What makes implementation evidence transferable?
Encyclopedia maturity states
Possible states:
   * stub
   * draft
   * AI-assisted draft
   * reviewed
   * source-linked
   * field-informed
   * pattern-generating
   * open question
   * deprecated
A polished page should not automatically be treated as authoritative.
Working goal
The Encyclopedia should become the Toolkit’s shared explanatory memory: broad, navigable, source-aware, and able to improve through review and implementation learning.
________________


3. Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
Function
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas maps what exists.
It grounds the Toolkit in real entities, artifacts, communities, and systems.
It should help people see the field across multiple lenses:
   * who is doing work
   * what tools exist
   * which concepts are connected
   * what ecosystems overlap
   * where knowledge is curated
   * what resources can be reused
   * what examples can be learned from
   * what remains unmapped
   * what deserves review
What belongs here
The Resource Graph includes:
   * organizations
   * networks
   * communities
   * people
   * tools
   * protocols
   * platforms
   * maps
   * datasets
   * articles
   * papers
   * books
   * events
   * projects
   * source systems
   * local nodes
   * implementation cases
   * infrastructure candidates
   * open repositories
   * curated libraries
   * knowledge gardens
Resource Graph vs Ecosystem Atlas
The Resource Graph is the structured registry.
It stores entries and relationships.
The Ecosystem Atlas is the interpretive map.
It lets people view the graph through different lenses:
   * entity view
   * function view
   * concept view
   * place view
   * source lineage view
   * relationship view
   * tool / infrastructure view
   * implementation view
   * risk / maturity view
Core questions
For each resource, ask:
   * What is it?
   * Who made it?
   * What does it do?
   * What ecosystem is it part of?
   * What concepts does it relate to?
   * What function does it serve?
   * What other resources does it connect to?
   * What layer of the Toolkit does it inform?
   * What should be credited?
   * What is its review or maturity status?
   * What risks, limits, or open questions should be visible?
Lightweight resource entry template
Field
	Description
	Name
	Resource, entity, project, person, organization, tool, etc.
	URL / reference
	Link or citation.
	Type
	Tool, org, person, event, framework, map, paper, source system, etc.
	Short description
	What it is.
	Domain
	ReFi, commons, AI, localism, ecology, governance, funding, etc.
	Function
	What it helps do.
	Related concepts
	Concepts or frameworks connected to it.
	Related entities
	People, orgs, tools, communities, events, or projects.
	Toolkit relevance
	Which Toolkit layers it informs.
	Source lineage
	Where it came from or how we found it.
	Maturity / review status
	Raw, candidate, reviewed, source system, etc.
	Notes / risks
	Limits, caveats, questions, or failure modes.
	Working goal
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas should make the field more legible without pretending to fully contain it.
It should support discovery, attribution, interoperability, ecosystem mapping, option selection, implementation learning, and future collaboration.
________________


4. Concept & Idea Ecology
Function
The Concept & Idea Ecology maps the ideas shaping the field.
It exists because regenerative web3, knowledge commoning, civic innovation, commons theory, ecological restoration, AI sensemaking, and local coordination are not only technical ecosystems. They are also conceptual ecosystems.
Ideas travel through people, organizations, books, events, communities, tools, practices, and metaphors. The Toolkit should make those idea flows more visible.
What belongs here
This layer includes:
   * intellectual lineages
   * conceptual clusters
   * frameworks
   * paradigms
   * movement language
   * design principles
   * tensions
   * unresolved debates
   * metaphors
   * bridge language
   * structural language
   * practice traditions
   * epistemic assumptions
Example idea clusters
Knowledge commoning
   * knowledge commons
   * knowledge gardening
   * shared memory
   * source lineage
   * living documents
   * component indexes
   * resource graphs
   * curation
   * synthesis
   * review and maturity
   * open questions
Commons and P2P
   * commoning
   * peer production
   * commons governance
   * enclosure
   * mutual aid
   * platform cooperativism
   * cooperative ownership
   * open knowledge
   * commons transition
Regenerative design
   * regeneration
   * living systems
   * place-sourced development
   * potential
   * reciprocity
   * nested wholes
   * developmental practice
   * regenerative economics
   * ecological limits
   * community vitality
Web3 public goods
   * public goods funding
   * credible neutrality
   * attestations
   * hypercerts
   * RetroPGF
   * quadratic funding
   * onchain coordination
   * decentralized identity
   * token engineering
   * ReFi
   * impact claims
Civic innovation
   * civic infrastructure
   * public participation
   * open government
   * deliberation
   * participatory democracy
   * digital public infrastructure
   * civic imagination
   * public interest technology
Localism and bioregionalism
   * local nodes
   * chapters
   * bioregions
   * watersheds
   * local resilience
   * mutual support
   * community wealth
   * place-based coordination
   * translocal learning
Structural integrity
   * information asymmetry
   * adverse signals
   * consent legibility
   * obligation direction
   * cost-bearing parties
   * power and control
   * structural floors
   * generative capacities
   * detection with named response
Collective intelligence and AI
   * sensemaking
   * distributed cognition
   * human-in-the-loop AI
   * epistemic commons
   * AI-assisted synthesis
   * deliberation
   * pattern detection
   * semantic drift
   * automation bias
Ecology and MRV
   * ecological evidence
   * biodiversity
   * soil
   * water
   * carbon
   * restoration
   * community science
   * remote sensing
   * monitoring
   * verification
   * ecological complexity
Relationship to sources
Concepts should preserve lineage where possible.
A concept may come through:
   * a book
   * a community
   * a framework
   * a person
   * a movement
   * a tool
   * a research field
   * a local practice
   * a knowledge garden
   * an implementation case
   * an event or convening
The Toolkit should credit curated lineages where they are inherited or inspired by another group’s work.
For example, if OpenCivics, Bloom, SuperBenefit, Crypto Altruists, P2P Foundation, ReFi DAO, GreenPill, Metagov, or another knowledge system helps surface an idea or lineage, that should be visible.
Working goal
The Concept & Idea Ecology should help people see not only what exists, but what ideas are moving through the field and how those ideas shape design choices.
It should make the Toolkit more intellectually honest, more generative, and more useful for deep synthesis.
