5. Option Library
Function
The Option Library organizes reusable design choices.
It helps communities and builders compare possible patterns before moving into deployment.
An option may be:
   * a funding mechanism
   * a decision process
   * a documentation pattern
   * a discovery pattern
   * a contribution model
   * a review process
   * an identity pattern
   * a mapping approach
   * a governance / coordination structure
   * an implementation pattern
   * an infrastructure pattern
   * a measurement approach
The Option Library should not say every option is good. It should make the design space visible.
Option vs tool vs deployment
Option
A reusable design choice.
Example: milestone-based funding.
Tool
A specific component that may support an option.
Example: Karma GAP.
Deployment
A context-specific composition of options, tools, roles, and operating conditions.
Example: a local regenerative funding round using milestone-based funding, community review, public reporting, attestations, and a shared treasury process.
Option entry template
Field
	Description
	Option name
	Name of reusable design choice.
	Description
	What it is.
	Problem addressed
	What need or tension it responds to.
	Useful when
	Conditions where it may fit.
	Not useful when
	Conditions where it may fail or overcomplicate.
	Related tools
	Specific tools or platforms that may support it.
	Related concepts
	Concepts or frameworks needed to understand it.
	Related cases
	Implementations or examples.
	Required roles
	Roles needed to operate it.
	Required structure
	What must be explicit.
	Risks / failure modes
	What can go wrong.
	Consent / power notes
	Representation, control, or participation concerns.
	Maturity status
	Raw, candidate, reviewed, field-informed, etc.
	Source lineage
	Where it comes from.
	Example option categories
Knowledge commons options
   * public wiki
   * digital garden
   * component index
   * source lineage field
   * glossary
   * open-question pages
   * review queue
   * contribution guide
   * knowledge graph
   * resource ingestion workflow
Ecosystem mapping options
   * organization directory
   * source system registry
   * concept map
   * event map
   * people / steward map
   * tool map
   * local node map
   * project map
   * bioregional map
   * relationship graph
Governance / coordination options
   * consent-based decision-making
   * council model
   * working group model
   * role-based stewardship
   * multisig execution
   * proposal flow
   * delegated review
   * participatory budgeting
   * dispute process
   * escalation pathway
Funding options
   * quadratic funding
   * retroactive funding
   * direct grants
   * milestone-based grants
   * conviction voting
   * streaming payments
   * donation pools
   * matching pools
   * local vouchers
   * community currencies
   * impact attestations
Discovery and participation options
   * opt-in profile
   * requests / offers board
   * skill map
   * interest map
   * local discovery flow
   * contributor pathway
   * mentor matching
   * project opportunity board
   * event discovery
   * privacy-preserving matching
Implementation learning options
   * retrospective template
   * after-action review
   * signal log
   * adverse signal channel
   * failure report
   * pattern harvest
   * implementation case page
   * adaptation record
   * unresolved tension log
   * change proposal
Working goal
The Option Library should help communities compose from visible choices rather than reinventing structure from scratch.
It should make options comparable, contextual, and accountable to their conditions.
________________


6. Deployment & Structural Integrity
Function
Deployment translates selected ideas, options, and components into operational structure.
It asks what must be explicit before something becomes active in the world.
This layer is especially important when the Toolkit informs:
   * funding rounds
   * local nodes
   * public maps
   * community directories
   * ecological claims
   * governance / coordination processes
   * AI-assisted discovery
   * public knowledge systems
   * implementation cases
   * resource allocation
   * real-world community work
What belongs here
Deployment includes:
   * purpose and scope
   * participant roles
   * decision paths
   * source of truth
   * consent conditions
   * information asymmetries
   * power and control points
   * obligation direction
   * review pathways
   * adverse signal channels
   * escalation paths
   * implementation boundaries
   * maintenance responsibilities
   * structural exposure notes
   * regenerative obligations
Core deployment questions
Before action, ask:
   * What is being deployed?
   * Who is involved?
   * Who may be affected?
   * Who may bear costs?
   * What decisions need to be made?
   * Who can make those decisions?
   * What information is required?
   * Who has information others lack?
   * What consent is needed?
   * Where is power or control located?
   * What can go wrong?
   * Where do adverse signals go?
   * Who responds?
   * What remains unresolved?
   * What must be reviewed later?
Structural integrity posture
The Toolkit should be informed by CSIS and adjacent structural integrity work.
The current posture:
CSIS-informed, not CSIS-conformant by default.
CSIS can help detect structural risks such as:
   * vague definitions
   * hidden power
   * unclear consent
   * information asymmetry
   * weak adverse signal paths
   * extractive aggregation
   * lack of review
   * contributor capacity strain
   * role confusion
   * overclaiming conformance
   * weak conflict transformation
   * unclear response when something fails
The Toolkit’s responsibility is to define what happens when a structural gap is detected.
Working goal
Deployment should help communities avoid moving from attractive ideas into fragile or harmful structures.
It should make context, obligation, consent, power, and response pathways visible.
________________


7. Implementation Memory
Function
Implementation Memory captures what happens when ideas meet reality.
It turns practice into learning material for the commons.
What belongs here
Implementation Memory includes:
   * local node cases
   * funding round cases
   * community projects
   * ecosystem maps
   * knowledge gardens
   * workshops
   * public goods programs
   * research sprints
   * hackathon outputs
   * regenerative field projects
   * governance / coordination processes
   * civic partnerships
   * tool experiments
   * failure cases
   * adaptation records
   * retrospectives
What to capture
For each implementation, capture:
   * what was attempted
   * why it mattered
   * who participated
   * what context shaped it
   * what tools or options were used
   * what worked
   * what failed
   * what confused people
   * what created friction
   * what signals emerged
   * what changed during implementation
   * what should be repeated
   * what should not be repeated
   * what remains unresolved
   * what could update the Toolkit
Evidence types
Implementation evidence may include:
   * notes
   * meeting records
   * public reports
   * participant reflections
   * screenshots
   * datasets
   * attestations
   * funding records
   * proposals
   * retrospectives
   * field observations
   * metrics
   * stories
   * conflict signals
   * failure notes
   * adaptation logs
Evidence quality should be visible. Not all evidence is equal, and not all evidence needs to be quantitative.
Working goal
Implementation Memory should prevent the Toolkit from becoming only theoretical.
It should preserve practical learning, including failure, friction, ambiguity, and adaptation.
________________


8. Evolution Layer
Function
The Evolution Layer defines how the Toolkit updates itself.
It receives signals from the rest of the system and routes them into review, synthesis, revision, or memory.
Signal types
Signals may include:
   * missing concept
   * confusing definition
   * outdated resource
   * broken link
   * weak category
   * repeated question
   * new tool
   * implementation failure
   * implementation success
   * adverse feedback
   * contributor burnout
   * unclear consent
   * hidden power concern
   * AI error
   * ecological claim concern
   * funding failure
   * new pattern
   * deprecated practice
   * unresolved tension
Evolution process
A simple process:
   1. Receive signal
   2. Classify signal
   3. Identify affected layer
   4. Interpret meaning
   5. Decide response
   6. Update or preserve as unresolved
   7. Record source and rationale
   8. Communicate change if needed
Possible responses
   * update an Encyclopedia page
   * add a resource
   * reclassify an entry
   * revise an option
   * add a failure mode
   * flag a structural risk
   * add an open question
   * change maturity status
   * deprecate a resource or pattern
   * add implementation evidence
   * revise ontology terms
   * update deployment templates
   * preserve unresolved tension
Working goal
Evolution should help the commons learn without becoming reactive, chaotic, or over-controlled.
The goal is not to resolve every signal immediately. The goal is to keep signals visible, interpretable, and connected to possible action.
________________


Layer summary
Layer
	What it answers
	Ontology
	What kinds of things exist, and how do they relate?
	Encyclopedia
	What does this mean, and how should we understand it?
	Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
	What exists in the world, and how is it connected?
	Concept & Idea Ecology
	What ideas, lineages, and tensions shape the field?
	Option Library
	What reusable choices are available?
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	What must be explicit before action?
	Implementation Memory
	What happened in practice?
	Evolution
	What should the commons learn or update?
	Core layer principle
Each layer should make the others more useful.
The Ontology makes knowledge classifiable.
The Encyclopedia makes ideas understandable.
The Resource Graph makes knowledge reality-grounded.
The Concept & Idea Ecology makes lineages visible.
The Option Library makes design space comparable.
Deployment makes action structurally legible.
Implementation Memory makes practice learnable.
Evolution makes the commons adaptive.


🌿 Cross-Cutting Systems
Purpose of cross-cutting systems
The cross-cutting systems describe functions and obligations that apply across the whole Toolkit.
They are not separate layers in the same sense as the Ontology, Encyclopedia, Resource Graph, Option Library, Deployment, Implementation Memory, or Evolution layers. Instead, they are shared concerns that shape how every layer should work.
The cross-cutting systems help the Toolkit remain:
   * source-aware
   * interoperable
   * reviewable
   * navigable
   * privacy-conscious
   * structurally legible
   * adaptive
   * regenerative
Without these systems, the Toolkit risks becoming a large archive with weak attribution, unclear trust levels, poor navigation, and limited capacity to learn from use.
Cross-cutting systems overview
System
	Function
	Provenance & Attribution
	Preserves where knowledge came from and how it should be credited.
	Review & Maturity
	Makes the status and trust level of content visible.
	Privacy, Consent & Identity
	Protects people and communities represented in the system.
	Standards & Interoperability
	Keeps data, metadata, schemas, and relationships portable.
	Discovery & Affinity
	Helps people find relevant topics, resources, communities, and collaborators.
	Infrastructure & Substrate
	Compares possible technical foundations and tools.
	Interface & Navigation
	Shapes how people actually move through the Toolkit.
	Synthesis & Sensemaking
	Turns messy inputs into interpreted learning.
	Regenerative Obligation
	Preserves obligations toward sources, contributors, communities, places, and future users.
	________________


1. Provenance & Attribution
Function
The Provenance & Attribution system preserves where knowledge comes from.
It tracks sources, contributors, references, adaptations, inspirations, implementation evidence, and review history.
This system is essential because the Toolkit aggregates and synthesizes knowledge from many communities, networks, authors, researchers, builders, and local contexts.
A knowledge commons should not erase the people and systems that made the knowledge possible.
What provenance should capture
For substantial claims, resources, concepts, frameworks, patterns, and implementation learnings, capture:
   * original source
   * creator / author / steward
   * contributor who added it
   * direct citation or URL
   * whether it is quoted, summarized, adapted, synthesized, or inspired
   * related source system
   * date accessed or reviewed
   * review status
   * context of use
   * known limitations
   * correction path
   * return path where appropriate
Use types
A resource may be used in different ways.
Use type
	Meaning
	Direct reference
	The Toolkit points to the source without modifying it.
	Summary
	The Toolkit summarizes source content.
	Adaptation
	The Toolkit changes or repurposes source material.
	Synthesis
	The Toolkit combines multiple sources into new framing.
	Inspiration
	The source shaped thinking but is not directly reused.
	Implementation evidence
	The source records something that happened in practice.
	Lineage reference
	The source helps trace the history of an idea or framework.
	Source systems
Some sources are not individual articles or tools. They are living knowledge environments.
Examples:
   * wikis
   * resource libraries
   * public forums
   * GitHub repositories
   * curated maps
   * knowledge gardens
   * community directories
   * standards repositories
   * event archives
   * research databases
   * local knowledge archives
These should be treated as source systems, not merely mined for individual links.
A source system entry should capture:
   * what it curates
   * who stewards it
   * what kinds of resources it contains
   * how it should be credited
   * what assumptions or worldview shape it
   * how current it appears to be
   * whether reuse or extraction has conditions
   * how to link back or return value
Working goal
Provenance should make the Toolkit more trustworthy, more reciprocal, and less extractive.
________________


2. Review & Maturity
Function
The Review & Maturity system makes trust levels visible.
A large knowledge commons will contain uneven material. Some entries will be polished. Some will be raw. Some will be AI-assisted. Some will be field-tested. Some will be speculative. Some will be outdated.
That is acceptable if the state is visible.
Suggested maturity states
State
	Meaning
	Raw
	Added for visibility; not yet organized.
	To-place
	Useful but not yet placed in the right section.
	Stub
	Minimal entry; needs expansion.
	Draft
	Developed enough to discuss; not fully reviewed.
	AI-assisted draft
	Created or substantially shaped with AI; needs human review.
	Source-linked
	Has visible references or source lineage.
	Candidate
	Likely useful; needs validation or review.
	Reviewed
	Checked by relevant human reviewers.
	Field-informed
	Informed by real implementation or practice.
	Pattern-generating
	Has produced reusable learning across contexts.
	Open question
	Intentionally unresolved.
	Deprecated
	Retained for memory but no longer current or recommended.
	Review types
Different content needs different kinds of review.
Review type
	Useful for
	Source review
	Checking citations, links, and provenance.
	Domain review
	Checking content accuracy in a field.
	Structural review
	Checking roles, obligations, risks, and coordination assumptions.
	Community review
	Checking representation of a group or community.
	Place-based review
	Checking local, cultural, ecological, or bioregional context.
	Legal / tax review
	Checking jurisdiction-sensitive claims.
	Ecological review
	Checking environmental, MRV, biodiversity, or restoration claims.
	AI review
	Checking AI-assisted synthesis, classification, or recommendations.
	Implementation review
	Checking whether practice-generated lessons are represented accurately.
	Higher-risk content
Some content should require stronger review before being treated as reliable.
Examples:
   * legal guidance
   * tax guidance
   * ecological impact claims
   * MRV guidance
   * claims about specific communities
   * representations of people or local groups
   * funding recommendations
   * governance / coordination recommendations
   * safety and security guidance
   * AI-assisted recommendations
   * deployment templates
   * structural integrity assessments
Working goal
The Review & Maturity system should let the Toolkit stay broad without creating false authority.
________________


3. Privacy, Consent & Identity
Function
The Privacy, Consent & Identity system protects people and communities represented in the Toolkit.
This matters whenever the Toolkit includes or may later include:
   * people profiles
   * community profiles
   * local maps
   * project directories
   * requests and offers
   * contributor histories
   * interests and skills
   * location information
   * contact preferences
   * discovery and matchmaking
   * implementation case studies
   * AI-assisted recommendations
The Toolkit should distinguish between information that is public, commons-visible, restricted, private, or intentionally unlisted.
Consent types
Consent should be specific.
A person or community may consent to one use but not another.
Consent type
	Meaning
	Consent to be listed
	Entry can appear in a directory or map.
	Consent to be described
	Summary or profile can be written.
	Consent to be contacted
	Others can reach out.
	Consent to be recommended
	System can suggest the person/community to others.
	Consent to be matched
	System can include them in active matching flows.
	Consent to use location
	Location can be used at agreed precision.
	Consent to use contribution history
	Past contributions can inform discovery or reputation.
	Consent for AI-assisted routing
	AI can use profile information to recommend connections.
	Consent to publish case material
	Implementation details can be shared publicly.
	Visibility levels
Possible visibility states:
   * private
   * visible to self only
   * visible to a working group
   * visible to a community
   * visible to trusted partners
   * public
   * public but non-indexed
   * public summary only
   * anonymized
   * aggregated
Location sensitivity
Location can be useful for local and bioregional coordination, but it can also create risk.
The Toolkit should support different precision levels:
   * country
   * region
   * state / province
   * city
   * neighborhood
   * watershed
   * bioregion
   * exact location
   * approximate location
   * private location with matchable radius
Exact location should not be assumed necessary.
Community representation
Representing a community is different from linking to a public website.
Community profiles should consider:
   * who can speak for the community
   * whether the community wants to be listed
   * what information is safe to share
   * what contact path is appropriate
   * whether the community is open to new participants
   * what cultural or local context matters
   * what should not be mapped publicly
Working goal
Discovery should be useful without becoming surveillance.
________________


4. Standards & Interoperability
Function
The Standards & Interoperability system helps the Toolkit connect with other tools, schemas, knowledge commons, graph systems, publishing systems, civic data efforts, web3 protocols, and resource directories.
The Toolkit should avoid becoming trapped in one platform or one internal schema.
What interoperability should support
   * exportable metadata
   * importable resource entries
   * stable identifiers
   * relationship types
   * source lineage fields
   * maturity fields
   * review status fields
   * consent status fields
   * ontology crosswalks
   * citation formats
   * open licensing
   * data portability
   * graph compatibility
   * publishing compatibility
   * AI-readable structure
   * human-readable structure
Candidate standards and references
Possible references include:
   * Schema.org
   * Dublin Core
   * W3C PROV
   * RDF / linked data
   * DCAT
   * Open Referral
   * Open Civic Data
   * Creative Commons licensing
   * FAIR principles
   * CARE principles
   * DAO metadata standards
   * grant metadata standards
   * EAS schemas
   * Hypercert metadata
   * GitHub / Markdown frontmatter
   * JSON-LD
   * CSV / tabular export
   * graph formats
These references should be evaluated by usefulness, not included for complexity’s sake.
Interoperability questions
For each important object type, ask:
   * Can this be exported?
   * Can another group understand it?
   * Can it map to a known schema?
   * Can its source lineage travel with it?
   * Can review and maturity status travel with it?
   * Can consent boundaries travel with it?
   * Can it be represented in both human-readable and machine-readable forms?
   * Can it move from document to database to graph to interface?
Working goal
The Toolkit should remain substrate-portable and interoperable across future infrastructure choices.
________________


5. Discovery & Affinity
Function
The Discovery & Affinity system helps people find what is relevant.
It is different from the Resource Graph.
The Resource Graph maps what exists.
Discovery & Affinity helps people navigate that map based on interest, context, role, need, offer, place, skill, project, or learning path.
Discovery modes
Possible discovery modes:
   * topic discovery
   * resource discovery
   * concept discovery
   * project discovery
   * organization discovery
   * tool discovery
   * person discovery
   * community discovery
   * event discovery
   * local discovery
   * bioregional discovery
   * request / offer discovery
   * implementation case discovery
   * learning path discovery
   * option discovery
Affinity signals
Affinity may be based on:
   * interests
   * skills
   * needs
   * offers
   * location
   * bioregion
   * community membership
   * project participation
   * contribution history
   * learning goals
   * active requests
   * active opportunities
   * shared frameworks
   * shared practices
   * complementary capacities
Personal or community affinity should only be used with appropriate consent.
Discovery surfaces
Possible surfaces:
   * search
   * filters
   * graph views
   * maps
   * topic pages
   * suggested related resources
   * “people working on this”
   * “tools that support this”
   * “projects using this”
   * “concepts connected to this”
   * “open questions in this area”
   * “requests and offers”
   * “local groups near this area”
   * “implementation cases using this option”
Working goal
Discovery should help people find meaningful paths through complexity without flattening the field or violating consent.
________________


6. Infrastructure & Substrate
Function
The Infrastructure & Substrate system compares possible technical foundations for the Toolkit.
It should support exploration without premature lock-in.
Possible substrates include:
   * Google Docs
   * Markdown
   * GitHub
   * static site publishing
   * Astro / Starlight
   * Notion
   * Obsidian
   * graph databases
   * semantic search
   * AI-assisted curation
   * KOI-like ingestion systems
   * Geo-like governed knowledge spaces
   * civic tech platforms
   * deliberation tools
   * community coordination platforms
   * web3 attestations
   * public goods funding infrastructure
   * APIs and data export formats
Evaluation criteria
When considering infrastructure, ask:
   * What function does it serve?
   * What problem does it solve?
   * What data does it require?
   * Who controls it?
   * Can we export from it?
   * Does it preserve source lineage?
   * Does it support review and maturity?
   * Does it support consent boundaries?
   * Does it create lock-in?
   * Does it support collaboration?
   * Does it support AI-assisted workflows safely?
   * Does it scale down to small groups?
   * Does it scale up to larger ecosystems?
   * Does it increase or reduce maintenance burden?
Working goal
Infrastructure should serve knowledge commoning, not define it too early.
________________


7. Interface & Navigation
Function
The Interface & Navigation system determines how people actually experience the Toolkit.
A strong architecture is not enough if people cannot find their way through it.
Entry points
Different people need different entry points:
   * by topic
   * by role
   * by question
   * by resource
   * by concept
   * by tool
   * by community
   * by place
   * by ecosystem
   * by implementation case
   * by option
   * by open question
   * by learning path
   * by contribution pathway
   * by deployment need
Navigation patterns
Useful navigation patterns may include:
   * tabbed workspace
   * table of contents
   * role-based entry points
   * topic maps
   * graph views
   * related concepts
   * related resources
   * related options
   * related cases
   * maturity filters
   * function filters
   * source-system filters
   * place filters
   * “start here” pages
   * “next steps” sections
   * guided pathways
Contributor navigation
Contributors also need clear paths:
   * add a resource
   * classify a resource
   * add a concept
   * improve a definition
   * add source lineage
   * review a page
   * suggest a relationship
   * add an implementation case
   * flag a risk
   * propose an option
   * update a maturity state
   * add an open question
Working goal
Navigation should help people enter at the right depth without needing to understand the entire architecture first.
________________


8. Synthesis & Sensemaking
Function
The Synthesis & Sensemaking system turns messy inputs into interpreted learning.
Inputs may include:
   * meeting notes
   * resource submissions
   * event outputs
   * workshop notes
   * research notes
   * AI summaries
   * implementation reflections
   * conflicting perspectives
   * open questions
   * failure reports
   * adverse signals
   * chat threads
   * field notes
   * ecosystem scans
Synthesis is not just summarization.
It involves interpretation, comparison, pattern recognition, uncertainty preservation, source tracing, and deciding what should update the commons.
Sensemaking questions
When interpreting messy input, ask:
   * What is being said?
   * What is new?
   * What is repeated?
   * What is uncertain?
   * What is contested?
   * What source does this come from?
   * What layer does this affect?
   * What concepts does this relate to?
   * What options does this inform?
   * What implementation learning does this generate?
   * What should be preserved as an open question?
   * What should be updated?
   * What should not be over-interpreted?
Types of synthesis
Possible synthesis outputs:
   * summary note
   * comparison page
   * open-question page
   * resource entry
   * concept entry
   * option entry
   * implementation case
   * failure mode
   * pattern harvest
   * structural risk note
   * glossary update
   * relationship map
   * source-system card
   * research backlog item
   * deployment checklist update
Working goal
Synthesis should help the commons learn from complexity without erasing nuance.
________________


9. Regenerative Obligation
Function
Regenerative Obligation names the Toolkit’s responsibility toward the people, communities, sources, places, ecosystems, and future users affected by its work.
This system asks:
   * Who made this knowledge possible?
   * Who is represented?
   * Who could be affected?
   * Who may bear costs?
   * Who should be credited?
   * Who should be able to correct or contest representation?
   * What local context matters?
   * What should not be extracted?
   * What return paths are possible?
   * What obligations continue after publication or deployment?
Obligation directions
The Toolkit may hold obligations toward:
   * source creators
   * source communities
   * contributors
   * reviewers
   * local communities
   * people represented in maps or cases
   * funders
   * users
   * implementers
   * future maintainers
   * affected ecosystems
   * knowledge holders not present in the room
   * places and bioregions
Regenerative risks
Watch for:
   * extractive aggregation
   * attribution loss
   * local knowledge extraction
   * representation without consent
   * source flattening
   * overclaiming impact
   * unpaid invisible labor
   * contributor burnout
   * imported solutions
   * ecological abstraction
   * AI-assisted erasure of nuance
   * value flowing away from source communities
Working goal
The Toolkit should not only organize regenerative knowledge. It should practice regenerative knowledge stewardship.
________________


Cross-cutting summary
The cross-cutting systems keep the Toolkit from becoming only a content architecture.
They help ensure that the commons preserves:
   * source lineage
   * trust signals
   * consent
   * privacy
   * interoperability
   * discoverability
   * navigability
   * sensemaking capacity
   * structural integrity
   * regenerative obligation
Together, they make the Toolkit more than a map.
They make it a living commons infrastructure.


🌐 Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
Note: This section introduces an upgraded operating model for the Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas. Some other sections may still use earlier language. Future passes should harmonize the Ontology, Provenance, Review & Maturity, Option Library, Deployment, and Interface sections with this version.
gov
Purpose
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas maps the real-world people, projects, organizations, tools, protocols, events, maps, datasets, source systems, publications, communities, and implementation examples that shape the Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit.
This section is not a link list.
It is the Toolkit’s reality layer: the place where concepts, frameworks, options, and design patterns are connected back to actual people, tools, communities, infrastructures, source systems, and cases.
The Resource Graph helps answer:
   * What exists?
   * Who made it?
   * Who stewards it?
   * What does it help people do?
   * What concepts does it carry?
   * What source systems should be credited?
   * What tools, standards, or frameworks can be reused?
   * What communities are adjacent or aligned?
   * What examples can be learned from?
   * What remains unmapped, unclear, outdated, sensitive, or under-reviewed?
   * What should become an Encyclopedia page, Option Library entry, Deployment template, or Implementation Memory case?
The goal is not to collect everything.
The goal is to make the ecosystem more legible, connected, attributable, reviewable, and useful for action.
________________


Core distinction: Resource Graph vs Ecosystem Atlas
The Resource Graph is the structured registry.
It stores entries, metadata, relationships, source lineage, review status, and routing information.
The Ecosystem Atlas is the interpretive map.
It helps people view the graph through different lenses.
View
	What it helps people see
	Entity view
	People, organizations, projects, tools, protocols, events, datasets
	Function view
	What resources help people do: publish, fund, verify, coordinate, map, sensemake
	Concept view
	Which concepts, lineages, and frameworks are moving through the field
	Source system view
	Which living knowledge environments should be credited and revisited
	Tool / infrastructure view
	What platforms, protocols, schemas, and substrates may support the Toolkit
	Option view
	Which resources support reusable design choices
	Implementation view
	Where tools, concepts, or options have been used in practice
	Place view
	Local nodes, bioregions, communities, ecological contexts, and place-based projects
	Risk / maturity view
	What is reviewed, raw, outdated, sensitive, high-risk, or deprecated
	Relationship view
	How resources connect, depend on, cite, support, or inform one another
	The Resource Graph stores the data.
The Ecosystem Atlas tells the story of the field.
________________


What belongs here
The Resource Graph may include:
   * organizations
   * networks
   * communities
   * people
   * tools
   * protocols
   * platforms
   * maps
   * datasets
   * articles
   * papers
   * books
   * events
   * projects
   * local nodes
   * source systems
   * standards
   * repositories
   * knowledge gardens
   * implementation cases
   * infrastructure candidates
   * failure cases
   * open questions
   * loose leads
Inclusion does not mean endorsement.
Inclusion means the resource may be useful for understanding, mapping, composing, reviewing, or improving the Toolkit.
________________


What does not primarily belong here
The Resource Graph can link to many kinds of knowledge, but it should not absorb every function of the Toolkit.
Item
	Primary home
	Concept explanation
	Encyclopedia / Concept & Idea Ecology
	Reusable design choice
	Option Library
	Operational checklist
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Retrospective or case analysis
	Implementation Memory
	Formal schema definition
	Ontology
	Feedback from use
	Evolution
	Unresolved research question
	Open Questions & Research Backlog
	The Resource Graph points to these layers. It does not replace them.
________________


1. Resource Graph v0.1 Schema
The first version of the Resource Graph should be a structured table.
The table below is a first working registry. It is intentionally simplified for readability inside the master doc. A future spreadsheet, database, or graph view can use more detailed fields.
________________


Minimum fields for the working registry
Field
	Description
	Category
	Broad resource family or ecosystem area
	Resource
	Name of the resource, source system, tool, organization, project, or case
	Type
	What kind of thing it is
	Function
	What it helps people do
	Toolkit Relevance / Notes
	Why it matters, how it may route, and what risks or review needs are visible
	________________


Expanded fields for future structured data
When this table moves into a spreadsheet, database, or graph system, it should eventually include:
Field
	Description
	Name
	Resource, entity, project, tool, person, organization, source system, etc.
	Link / reference
	URL, citation, repo, document, event page, or source reference
	Type
	What kind of thing it is
	Short description
	Plain-language description
	Domain
	The ecosystem or topic area it belongs to
	Function
	What it helps people do
	Related concepts
	Concepts, frameworks, or ideas connected to it
	Related entities
	People, orgs, tools, communities, projects, or events connected to it
	Toolkit relevance
	Which Toolkit layer or section it informs
	Source lineage
	Where it came from, who surfaced it, or how it should be credited
	Use type
	Direct reference, summary, adaptation, synthesis, inspiration, implementation evidence, lineage reference
	Status
	Raw, candidate, source-linked, reviewed, source system, field-informed, deprecated, etc.
	Review need
	Source review, domain review, structural review, ecological review, legal review, community review, AI review
	Notes / risks
	Caveats, limits, sensitivities, unresolved questions, failure modes
	________________


2. Source System Registry
Some resources are not just links.
They are living knowledge environments.
A source system may be a wiki, map, GitHub repo, public forum, resource library, knowledge garden, research database, community directory, event archive, standards repository, or curated body of work.
Source systems should not be treated as extractable link pools.
They should be treated as stewarded knowledge environments with context, worldview, maintenance rhythms, attribution needs, and possible return paths.
________________


Source System Card
Field
	Description
	Name
	Source system name
	Link
	URL or reference
	Type
	Wiki, map, repo, forum, garden, directory, archive, database, library
	Steward
	Person, group, community, org, or unknown
	What it curates
	Main knowledge domain or resource type
	Why it matters
	Relevance to the Toolkit
	Related domains
	Domains it informs
	Related concepts
	Concepts, frameworks, or lineages it carries
	How to credit
	Attribution note
	Use type
	Direct reference, summary, synthesis, adaptation, inspiration
	Reuse conditions
	License, norms, permission needs, unclear
	Currentness
	Active, partially active, archived, unclear
	Review notes
	Caveats, gaps, risks, worldview, maintenance limits
	Return path
	How attention, corrections, collaboration, or value can flow back
	Toolkit route
	Resource Graph, Encyclopedia, Option Library, Implementation Memory, etc.
	________________


3. Relationship Grammar
The Resource Graph becomes valuable when relationships are explicit.
The first relationship grammar should be small. Too many relationship types will create confusion and slow contribution.
________________


Core relationship types
Relationship
	Use when
	is a
	Classifying an entity as a type
	part of
	One thing belongs within another
	related to
	General relationship when more precision is not yet known
	created by
	Identifying originator or creator
	maintained by
	Identifying steward or maintainer
	stewarded by
	Identifying care / governance responsibility
	cites
	One resource cites another
	references
	One resource points to another
	adapts
	One resource modifies or builds from another
	inspired by
	Influence without direct reuse
	supports
	A tool, resource, or framework supports a function
	enables
	One thing makes another possible
	used by
	A tool, framework, or resource is used by an entity or case
	implemented by
	An option or concept is implemented in a case
	informs
	A resource shapes a concept, option, or Toolkit section
	has concept
	A resource carries or connects to a concept
	has option
	A resource supports a reusable design choice
	has evidence
	A claim or case has supporting evidence
	has risk
	A resource has an identified caveat or failure mode
	requires review
	A resource needs a review type before stronger use
	updates
	A signal or case updates another part of the Toolkit
	deprecates
	A signal or review makes an entry no longer recommended
	conflicts with
	Two resources, claims, or frameworks disagree
	complements
	Two resources work well together
	________________


Example relationships
Subject
	Relationship
	Object
	Hypercerts
	supports
	impact claims
	EAS
	supports
	attestations
	Karma GAP
	supports
	grant progress reporting
	Allo
	supports
	allocation mechanisms
	OpenTEAM
	supports
	ecological data infrastructure
	Hylo
	supports
	place-based coordination
	CSIS
	informs
	structural integrity

	regenerative community practice

	informs
	knowledge gardening

	nonprofit-facing web3 adoption

	commons theory

	governance / coordination research
	farmOS
	supports
	field record systems
	Pol.is
	supports
	deliberation synthesis
	DAOstar OpenGrants
	supports
	grant metadata interoperability
	Open Referral
	informs
	local service directory standards
	Restor
	supports
	restoration project mapping
	These relationships are starting points for mapping, review, and refinement.
________________


4. Routing Logic
Every resource should eventually have a route.
The Resource Graph should not become a pile of “interesting things.” Each entry should point somewhere.
________________


Routing questions
Ask:
   * Should this remain a Resource Graph entry?
   * Should it become an Encyclopedia concept page?
   * Should it become a Concept & Idea Ecology lineage note?
   * Should it become an Option Library entry?
   * Should it become an Implementation Memory case?
   * Should it become an Infrastructure candidate?
   * Should it become a Deployment checklist reference?
   * Should it become an Open Question?
   * Should it become a Failure Mode or Anti-pattern entry?
   * Should it be archived, deprecated, or removed?
________________


Routing table
If the resource is…
	Route it to…
	A tool or platform
	Resource Graph + Option Library if it supports a reusable design choice
	A concept or framework
	Encyclopedia + Concept & Idea Ecology
	A living knowledge environment
	Source System Registry
	A real-world example
	Implementation Memory
	A funding mechanism
	Option Library + Deployment
	A standard or schema
	Ontology + Standards & Interoperability
	A governance method
	Option Library + Deployment
	A community map
	Resource Graph + Privacy / Consent review
	An ecological dataset
	Resource Graph + Ecology / MRV review
	A cautionary case
	Failure Case Library + Implementation Memory
	A speculative lead
	Loose Leads / To-review
	A repeated unresolved issue
	Open Questions & Research Backlog
	Routing helps the Resource Graph feed the rest of the Toolkit.
________________


5. Review and Maturity Status
The Resource Graph should stay open, but trust levels must be visible.
A raw entry can be useful.
A reviewed entry can be relied on more strongly.
A high-risk entry should be handled with care.
A deprecated entry may still be useful for memory.
________________


Entry status
Status
	Meaning
	Raw
	Added for visibility; not yet classified
	To-place
	Useful but not yet routed
	Candidate
	Likely useful; needs validation or review
	Source-linked
	Has visible source lineage
	Source system
	Living external knowledge environment
	Reviewed
	Checked by relevant human reviewer
	Field-informed
	Informed by real use or implementation
	Pattern-generating
	Has produced reusable learning across contexts
	High-risk
	Requires stronger review before practical use
	Deprecated
	Retained for memory but no longer current or recommended
	Archived
	Kept for historical reference
	Open question
	Useful uncertainty, not yet resolved
	________________


Review types
Review type
	Use for
	Source review
	Links, citations, provenance, attribution
	Domain review
	Accuracy in a specific field
	Structural review
	Roles, obligations, risks, power, decision paths
	Community review
	Representation of people, groups, or networks
	Place-based review
	Local, cultural, ecological, or bioregional context
	Legal / tax review
	Jurisdiction-sensitive claims
	Ecological review
	MRV, biodiversity, carbon, restoration, environmental claims
	AI review
	AI-assisted synthesis, classification, or recommendation
	Implementation review
	Accuracy of practical lessons or case claims
	Privacy / consent review
	People, locations, profiles, matching, sensitive mapping
	Review status should travel with the resource.
A polished description should not make an entry appear more trustworthy than it is.
________________


6. High-Risk Resource Triggers
Some resources should not be treated as ordinary references.
Mark stronger review needs when an entry involves:
   * people profiles
   * community profiles
   * local maps
   * exact locations
   * vulnerable communities
   * Indigenous or local knowledge
   * ecological impact claims
   * biodiversity, carbon, soil, water, or restoration claims
   * funding recommendations
   * legal or tax guidance
   * governance recommendations
   * public rankings or scoring
   * AI-assisted matching
   * contribution histories
   * identity, reputation, or credentials
   * sensitive political, social, or ecological conflict
   * funder-facing impact claims
High-risk does not mean “do not include.”
It means: include with care, visible status, consent notes, and review pathway.
________________


7. Resource Graph Views
The same dataset should support multiple views.
These views can be built later from the same structured table.
________________


View 1 — Source Systems
Purpose: identify living knowledge environments and attribution obligations.
Useful filters:
   * type = source system
   * currentness
   * domain
   * steward
   * return path
   * review status
________________


View 2 — Tools by Function
Purpose: help builders and organizers find tools.
Useful filters:
   * function
   * domain
   * related option
   * maintenance burden
   * interoperability notes
   * privacy risk
________________


View 3 — Concepts by Resource
Purpose: show which ideas are moving through the field.
Useful filters:
   * related concepts
   * lineage references
   * domain
   * source system
   * maturity
________________


View 4 — Options by Supporting Tools
Purpose: connect design choices to practical components.
Option
	Supporting tools
	Review needs
	Impact attestations
	EAS, Hypercerts, Green Goods
	Evidence quality, privacy, claim boundaries
	Grant progress reporting
	Karma GAP, OpenGrants, public docs
	Reporting burden, metric theater, accountability
	Place-based coordination
	Hylo, OpenStreetMap, local directories
	Consent, location sensitivity, community review
	Knowledge garden publishing
	GitHub, Markdown, Quartz, Astro, Starlight
	Maintenance burden, contributor accessibility
	Public goods funding
	Gitcoin, Allo, Octant, Giveth, Open Collective
	Gaming, accountability, eligibility, legitimacy

	farmOS, Restor, iNaturalist, GBIF, Global Forest Watch
	Ecological review, data quality, local context
	________________


View 5 — Implementation Cases
Purpose: ground the Toolkit in real practice.

   * implementation type
   * tools used
   * options used
   * signals generated
   * field-informed status
   * Toolkit updates
________________


View 6 — Risk and Review
Purpose: make trust levels visible.
Useful filters:
   * high-risk
   * review need
   * source-linked
   * deprecated
   * privacy-sensitive
   * ecological-review-needed
   * legal-review-needed
________________


View 7 — Local / Place-Based Atlas
Purpose: support local and bioregional coordination without unsafe exposure.
Useful filters:
   * place
   * bioregion
   * local node
   * community consent status
   * location precision
   * sensitive knowledge notes
   * public/private visibility
________________


8. Minimum Viable Resource Graph
The first version should not try to model everything.
The first version should make the current inventory usable.
________________


v0.1 goal
Create a structured table that turns the existing resource inventory into a navigable, reviewable, and source-aware graph-in-progress.
________________


v0.1 success criteria
The first pass succeeds if:
   * obvious duplicates are reduced
   * source systems are separated from individual resources
   * each entry has at least one type
   * each entry has at least one domain
   * each entry has at least one function
   * important source systems are credited
   * high-risk entries are flagged
   * loose leads are separated from reviewed or candidate resources
   * at least 25–50 priority entries are structured
   * contributors can add a new resource without understanding the whole architecture
The first pass does not need to be perfect.
It needs to be usable.
________________


9. First-Pass Prioritization
Do not classify every resource equally.
Start with the resources that unlock the rest of the Toolkit.
________________


Priority 1 — Source systems
Why: they preserve lineage and prevent extractive aggregation.
Examples:
   * P2P Foundation Wiki
   * SuperBenefit Knowledge Garden
   * Bloom Wiki
   * Gitcoin Governance Forum
   * CSIS GitHub
   * OpenTEAM
   * DeSci World
   * ReFi DAO resources
________________


Priority 2 — Infrastructure candidates
Why: they shape what the Toolkit could become.
Examples:
   * GitHub
   * Markdown
   * Astro / Starlight
   * Quartz
   * Notion
   * Kumu
   * Geo / GRC-20
   * KOI-net
   * semantic search / RAG workflows
________________


Priority 3 — Public goods / impact tools
Why: they connect to funding, claims, implementation, and reporting.
Examples:
   * Allo
   * Karma GAP
   * Hypercerts
   * EAS
   * Open Source Observer
   * DAOstar OpenGrants
   * Green Goods
________________


Priority 4 — Localism and coordination tools
Why: the Toolkit is intended to support local coordination.
Examples:
   * Hylo
   * Sarafu / Grassroots Economics
   * Bloom Network
   * ReFi DAO Localism Finance
   * Open Referral
   * Decidim
   * Pol.is
________________


Priority 5 — Ecology / MRV resources
Why: ecological claims require stronger evidence discipline.
Examples:
   * OpenTEAM
   * farmOS
   * GBIF
   * iNaturalist
   * Global Forest Watch
   * Restor
   * OpenMRV
   * Plan Vivo
   * Verra
   * Gold Standard
________________


10. Contributor Workflow
A contributor should not need to understand the full Toolkit to add one useful thing.
________________


Lightweight contribution flow
   1. Add the resource.
   2. Add the link or source.
   3. Say why it matters.
   4. Choose a type.
   5. Choose a domain.
   6. Choose a function.
   7. Add related concepts if known.
   8. Mark source system if applicable.
   9. Add source lineage.
   10. Flag risks or review needs.
   11. Route it to another layer if needed.
________________


Minimum contribution form
Field
	Response
	What I am adding
	

	Link / source
	

	Why it matters
	

	Suggested type
	

	Suggested domain
	

	Suggested function
	

	Related concepts
	

	Is this a source system?
	yes / no / unsure
	Any risks or review needs?
	

	Should this route somewhere else?
	Encyclopedia / Option Library / Implementation Memory / Open Question / unsure
	________________

12. Working Priorities
Immediate priorities
   1. Convert this registry into a structured table or spreadsheet.
   2. Add links for each entry.
   3. Identify source systems vs individual resources.
   4. Add type, domain, and function to each priority entry.
   5. Add source lineage and use type.
   6. Add relationship mapping for the first 50 entries.
   7. Mark review and maturity status.
   8. Flag high-risk entries.
   9. Route entries into Encyclopedia, Option Library, Deployment, Implementation Memory, Infrastructure, or Open Questions.
   10. Create the first Source System Registry view.
   11. Create the first Ecosystem Atlas views.
________________


Suggested first sprint
Sprint goal: Create the Minimum Viable Resource Graph.
Outputs:
   * Resource Graph table v0.1
   * Source System Registry v0.1
   * 25–50 structured priority entries
   * First relationship grammar applied to priority entries
   * High-risk resource flags
   * First routing notes into other Toolkit layers
   * Contributor form for adding new resources
Success condition:
A new contributor can add a resource in less than 10 minutes without understanding the full Toolkit architecture.
________________


13. Open Questions
   * What is the minimum viable Resource Graph?
   * Which fields are essential now, and which should wait?
   * Which resources should become reviewed entries first?
   * Which are source systems rather than individual resources?
   * Which source systems need explicit permission, correction paths, or return paths?
   * Which entries should become Concept & Idea Ecology lineage notes?
   * Which entries should become Option Library entries?
   * Which entries are infrastructure candidates?
   * Which entries are implementation cases?
   * Which are failure cases?
   * Which are only loose leads?
   * Which resources should be removed?
   * What is missing from the atlas?
   * Which communities should be asked for correction or context?
   * Which resources require legal, ecological, privacy, or structural review before practical use?
   * What relationship types are useful enough to keep?
   * What relationship types are too complex for v0.1?
   * How should the Resource Graph eventually migrate into Markdown, CSV, JSON, a graph database, Geo, or another substrate?
   * How should changes to the Resource Graph be reviewed and logged?
   * Who is responsible for maintaining the Source System Registry?
   * How should source systems receive attribution, corrections, collaboration, or return value?
________________


Working Summary
The Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas is the Toolkit’s map of reality.
It helps knowledge gardeners move from scattered resources toward a structured, relational, source-aware ecosystem map.
The Resource Graph makes resources classifiable.
The Ecosystem Atlas makes relationships interpretable.
The Source System Registry makes attribution and reciprocity visible.
The Relationship Grammar turns the inventory into a graph.
The Review & Maturity system prevents raw or risky resources from becoming false authority.
The purpose is not to collect everything.
The purpose is to help the ecosystem see itself, credit itself, connect itself, and learn from itself.


🌺 Concept & Idea Ecology
Purpose
The Concept & Idea Ecology maps the ideas, frameworks, lineages, paradigms, tensions, and language patterns that shape the Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit.
The Toolkit should not only map organizations, tools, protocols, projects, and resources. It should also map the conceptual worlds those entities come from.
Ideas shape what people build.
Language shapes what people notice.
Frameworks shape what people treat as important.
Lineages shape what gets credited.
Tensions reveal where deeper thinking is needed.
This section helps the Toolkit understand the intellectual and cultural terrain it is moving through.
Core function
The Concept & Idea Ecology helps answer:
   * What ideas are shaping this field?
   * Where did these ideas come from?
   * Which concepts are shared across multiple ecosystems?
   * Which concepts are used differently by different communities?
   * Which ideas should be credited to specific lineages, thinkers, or source systems?
   * Which concepts are useful but vague?
   * Which tensions remain unresolved?
   * Which ideas should become Encyclopedia pages?
   * Which ideas should inform the Option Library?
   * Which ideas should shape Deployment and Structural Integrity?
   * Which ideas are missing from the current map?
Difference from the Resource Graph
The Resource Graph maps entities.
The Concept & Idea Ecology maps meaning.
Resource Graph
	Concept & Idea Ecology
	OpenCivics
	Open civic innovation, civic infrastructure, knowledge commoning
	Bloom Network
	Regenerative culture, local action, community weaving
	SuperBenefit
	Reimagining power, impact networks, systems transformation
	Crypto Altruists
	Web3 impact, crypto philanthropy, nonprofit adoption
	Gitcoin
	Public goods funding, quadratic funding, ecosystem allocation
	Regenesis
	Regenerative development, place-sourced design, nested wholes
	CSIS
	Structural integrity, adverse signals, obligation direction
	Hylo
	Place-based coordination, requests and offers, community discovery
	EAS
	Attestations, verifiable claims, source-linked records
	OpenTEAM
	Agroecological data, land stewardship, open ecological tooling
	The same organization, project, or tool may connect to many concepts.
The same concept may appear across many organizations, communities, and tools.
What belongs here
This section can include:
   * concepts
   * frameworks
   * intellectual lineages
   * movement language
   * paradigms
   * tensions
   * metaphors
   * design principles
   * bridge terms
   * structural terms
   * recurring questions
   * conceptual clusters
   * source lineages
   * schools of thought
   * practice traditions
   * anti-patterns
   * interpretive lenses

	Short definition
	Plain-language explanation.
	Why it matters
	Why the Toolkit should care.
	Related lineages

	Related resources
	Books, articles, orgs, tools, source systems, events, or cases.
	Related Toolkit layers
	Where this concept matters.
	Common confusions
	Terms or ideas it is often confused with.
	Tensions / critiques
	Where the concept is contested or risky.

	Maturity status
	Raw, draft, reviewed, source-linked, field-informed, open question.

1. Knowledge Commoning
Core idea
Knowledge commoning is the practice of collectively stewarding knowledge as a shared resource.
It is not only content management. It includes contribution, curation, review, attribution, maintenance, access, synthesis, memory, and adaptation.
Related concepts
   * knowledge commons
   * knowledge gardening
   * source lineage
   * attribution
   * open knowledge
   * shared memory
   * public knowledge
   * digital gardens
   * component indexes
   * resource graphs
   * living documents
   * review and maturity
   * federated knowledge systems
   * decentralized curation
   * commons stewardship
   * knowledge maintenance
   * knowledge decay
   * open questions
   * synthesis
   * pattern harvesting
Why it matters
The Toolkit is fundamentally a knowledge commoning project.
Its goal is not simply to collect information. It is to help a community steward knowledge over time: making it findable, attributable, contextual, reusable, corrigible, and capable of learning from practice.
Key tensions
   * openness vs stewardship
   * contribution ease vs metadata quality
   * broad participation vs review burden
   * AI-assisted synthesis vs source fidelity
   * curation vs gatekeeping
   * decentralization vs coherence
   * living knowledge vs stable reference
   * shared memory vs information overload
Related Toolkit sections
   * Ontology
   * Encyclopedia
   * Resource Graph
   * Provenance & Attribution
   * Review & Maturity
   * Synthesis & Sensemaking
   * Evolution
________________


2. Commons and P2P Lineages
Core idea
Commons and P2P lineages frame shared resources as collectively stewarded systems rather than purely private, state-owned, or market-managed assets.
This lineage informs how the Toolkit thinks about stewardship, participation, access, reciprocity, governance / coordination, and enclosure.
Related concepts
   * commons
   * commoning
   * peer production
   * commons transition
   * open knowledge
   * digital commons
   * platform cooperativism
   * cooperative ownership
   * mutual aid
   * enclosure
   * contribution
   * stewardship
   * polycentric governance
   * public goods
   * shared infrastructure
   * resource sovereignty
Possible lineage references
   * Elinor Ostrom
   * Michel Bauwens
   * P2P Foundation
   * Commons Transition
   * Creative Commons
   * Open Knowledge Foundation
   * platform cooperativism
   * cooperative movements
   * open-source communities
   * mutual aid networks
Why it matters
The Toolkit should not treat knowledge as content extracted from communities and reorganized into a new central platform.
Commons and P2P lineages remind the Toolkit to ask:
   * Who stewards this?
   * Who contributes?
   * Who benefits?
   * Who has access?
   * Who can correct or contest?
   * What prevents enclosure?
   * What obligations exist toward source communities?
   * What should remain distributed?
Key tensions
   * open access vs community consent
   * reuse vs extraction
   * shared stewardship vs unclear responsibility
   * commons governance vs governance theater
   * platform ownership vs commons infrastructure
   * decentralization vs abandonment
   * contribution without maintenance
Related Toolkit sections
   * Working Posture
   * Provenance & Attribution
   * Resource Graph
   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
   * Regenerative Obligation
   * Legal / Institutional Interface
   * Implementation Memory
________________


3. Regenerative Design and Development
Core idea
Regenerative design asks how systems can increase the vitality, capacity, and evolutionary potential of the living systems they are part of.
It is not only about sustainability, restoration, or impact reduction. It asks how places, communities, ecosystems, and human systems can develop in ways that support life, relationship, learning, and renewal.
Related concepts
   * regeneration
   * regenerative development
   * living systems
   * place-sourced design
   * nested wholes
   * potential
   * reciprocity
   * developmental practice
   * ecological vitality
   * social vitality
   * regenerative economics
   * whole-systems thinking

4. Public Goods and Web3 Coordination
Core idea
Public goods are resources that are non-excludable and non-rivalrous, or at least widely beneficial and difficult to fund through ordinary market mechanisms.
Web3 public goods work explores how cryptographic infrastructure, programmable coordination, funding mechanisms, attestations, and decentralized communities can support public goods creation and maintenance.
Related concepts
   * public goods
   * digital public goods
   * protocol public goods
   * open-source infrastructure
   * ecosystem funding
   * retroactive funding
   * quadratic funding
   * grants
   * attestations
   * hypercerts
   * onchain records
   * credible neutrality
   * mechanism design
   * coordination failure
   * commons infrastructure
   * contribution records

Possible lineage references

   * Gitcoin

   * Allo

   * Giveth

   * Hypercerts
   * Ethereum Attestation Service
   * Open Source Observer
   * public goods economics
   * open-source communities
   * mechanism design research
Why it matters
The Toolkit emerges partly from regenerative web3 and public goods ecosystems. These ecosystems provide many of the tools, mechanisms, and questions that shape the project.
Important questions include:
   * How should public goods be funded?
   * How should contributions be recognized?
   * How should impact be claimed?
   * How should grants be reported?
   * How can funding avoid gaming and capture?
   * How can open-source infrastructure be maintained?
   * How can local impact connect with global funding systems?
   * How can regenerative projects avoid being reduced to financial primitives?
Key tensions
   * public goods funding vs grant dependency
   * impact claims vs evidence quality
   * token incentives vs intrinsic motivation
   * retroactive rewards vs future capacity
   * open-source maintenance vs unpaid labor
   * ecosystem funding vs ecosystem capture
   * metrics vs meaning
   * local impact vs global funder expectations

5. ReFi and Regenerative Crypto
Core idea
Regenerative finance and regenerative crypto explore how financial, social, technical, and ecological systems can be designed to support regeneration rather than extraction.

6. Localism, Bioregionalism, and Place-Based Coordination
Core idea
Localism and bioregionalism emphasize that coordination should be grounded in place, ecology, community, culture, and local context.
A local node is not only a branch of a larger network. It is a situated coordination system with its own relationships, needs, assets, constraints, histories, and ecological realities.

Why it matters
The original motivation for the Toolkit includes helping local regenerative web3 communities operate more effectively.
Local groups need more than concepts. They need ways to:
   * find relevant knowledge
   * map local resources
   * coordinate people
   * discover collaborators
   * connect needs and offers
   * track projects
   * access funding
   * preserve local memory

   * represent themselves safely and accurately

7. Civic Innovation and Democratic Infrastructure
Core idea
Civic innovation explores how communities, institutions, networks, and technologies can support collective decision-making, participation, public problem-solving, and civic life.
Democratic infrastructure includes the tools, processes, norms, institutions, and data systems that enable people to deliberate, decide, coordinate, and act together.
Related concepts

   * participatory democracy

   * public interest technology
   * civic AI efforts
   * municipalist movements
Why it matters
The Toolkit overlaps strongly with civic infrastructure because it is concerned with how people discover knowledge, coordinate resources, make decisions, preserve memory, and learn together.
Civic innovation also helps keep the Toolkit from becoming overly web3-native. Many of the problems the Toolkit cares about existed before web3 and have been explored deeply by civic technologists, public sector innovators, deliberation practitioners, and democratic infrastructure builders.
Key tensions
   * participation vs participation theater
   * digital access vs digital exclusion

8. Governance, Coordination, and Structural Integrity
Core idea
Governance and coordination are often used loosely. The Toolkit should use familiar bridge language where helpful, but become more precise where structure matters.
Coordination architecture refers to the roles, decision paths, obligations, information flows, consent conditions, power/control points, review processes, and response pathways that allow people to act together.
Structural integrity asks whether those arrangements are clear enough, fair enough, responsive enough, and context-aware enough to function without hidden harm.
Related concepts
   * governance
   * coordination architecture
   * coordination instruments
   * roles
   * decision paths
   * stewardship obligation
   * obligation direction
   * information asymmetry
   * power and control
   * consent legibility
   * adverse signals
   * detection with named response
   * structural floors
   * generative capacities
   * review pathways
   * conformance
   * conflict transformation
   * cost-bearing parties
   * scale thresholds
   * trust radius
   * contributor capacity
Possible lineage references
   * CSIS
   * Metagov
   * DAOstar
   * Govbase
   * Sociocracy
   * Holacracy
   * Viable System Model
   * Ostrom
   * cybernetics
   * organizational design
   * digital governance research
   * civic deliberation
Why it matters
The Toolkit may influence funding, local coordination, knowledge representation, AI-assisted discovery, public claims, community profiles, and ecological work.
That means it needs more than vague governance language. It needs clear structures for:
   * who decides
   * who maintains
   * who reviews
   * who can contest
   * who bears costs
   * who controls infrastructure
   * who has hidden information
   * who responds to harm
   * what happens when something fails
Bridge language and structural language
Bridge language
	More precise structural language
	Governance
	Coordination architecture / coordination instrument
	Accountability
	Stewardship obligation / obligation direction
	Stakeholders
	Participants / contributors / cost-bearing parties
	Transparency
	Multi-directional visibility
	Enforcement
	Detection with named response
	Compliance
	Conformance
	Feedback
	Signal / adverse signal / learning signal
	Users
	Participants / contributors / affected parties
	Power structure
	Power, control, and obligation structure
	Rules
	Structural conditions
	Key tensions
   * governance as language vs governance as structure
   * visible rules vs hidden influence
   * consent vs passive participation
   * transparency vs information overload
   * decentralization vs accountability gaps
   * autonomy vs coherence
   * speed vs legitimacy
   * conflict avoidance vs conflict transformation
   * formal power vs informal power

   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
   * Option Library
   * Review & Maturity
   * Evolution
   * Resource Graph
   * Implementation Memory

9. AI, Collective Intelligence, and Sensemaking
Core idea
AI and collective intelligence tools can help people search, summarize, classify, map, compare, synthesize, and discover patterns across large bodies of knowledge.
But AI can also create false authority, flatten context, erase source lineage, and amplify semantic drift.
The Toolkit should use AI as an assistant to knowledge gardening, not as a replacement for stewardship.
Related concepts
   * collective intelligence
   * distributed cognition
   * sensemaking
   * AI-assisted curation
   * AI-assisted synthesis
   * semantic search
   * embeddings
   * retrieval-augmented generation
   * human-in-the-loop review
   * deliberation
   * pattern recognition
   * epistemic commons
   * model-mediated interpretation
   * automation bias
   * source fidelity
   * provenance under AI use
   * disagreement preservation
   * narrative capture
Possible lineage references
   * Collective Intelligence Project
   * Harmonica
   * Bonfires
   * Pol.is
   * Kialo
   * SenseMaker
   * OpenMined
   * civic AI projects
   * public interest AI
   * digital garden tools
   * AI safety and governance research
Why it matters
The Toolkit will likely rely on AI for some combination of:
   * resource classification
   * concept extraction
   * synthesis
   * search
   * comparison
   * relationship mapping
   * glossary support
   * draft generation
   * pattern detection
   * review assistance
   * contributor support
This can make the commons more usable, but only if source lineage, human review, and uncertainty remain visible.
Key tensions
   * speed vs trust
   * synthesis vs flattening
   * pattern detection vs hallucination
   * AI recommendations vs consent
   * semantic search vs source context
   * polished drafts vs review maturity
   * automation vs stewardship
   * model interpretation vs community interpretation
   * scale vs nuance

10. Ecology, MRV, and Evidence
Core idea
Ecological and regenerative work requires evidence, but ecological evidence is complex, context-sensitive, and often difficult to reduce to simple metrics.
MRV, monitoring, community science, remote sensing, field records, attestations, and ecological indicators can support accountability, but they can also distort reality if treated as complete representations of living systems.

Why it matters
The Toolkit may help communities make or evaluate ecological claims.
That creates a high standard of care. Environmental claims should not be treated as simple marketing, grant reporting, or tokenization material.
The Toolkit should help distinguish:
   * claim
   * evidence
   * measurement
   * observation
   * verification
   * attestation

15. Privacy, Consent, and Cypherpunk Lineages
Core idea
Privacy and consent are not optional technical features. They are structural conditions for safe participation, especially when a system maps people, communities, locations, skills, needs, offers, contribution histories, or local projects.
Cypherpunk and privacy lineages remind the Toolkit that digital coordination can become surveillance if visibility, identity, and matching are handled carelessly.
Related concepts
   * privacy
   * consent
   * encryption
   * anonymity
   * pseudonymity
   * selective disclosure
   * zero-knowledge proofs
   * identity sovereignty
   * metadata protection
   * secure communication
   * location privacy
   * opt-in discovery
   * data minimization
   * community safety
   * surveillance risk
   * privacy-preserving AI
   * privacy-preserving matching
Possible lineage references
   * Signal
   * Web3Privacy Now
   * 0xPARC
   * Nym
   * Aztec
   * OpenMined
   * cypherpunk research
   * privacy engineering
   * decentralized identity work
   * secure communication communities
Why it matters
The Toolkit’s Discovery & Affinity ambitions create privacy risks.
A system that helps people find each other may also expose:
   * sensitive locations
   * vulnerable communities
   * local organizers
   * funding needs
   * political or ecological conflicts
   * informal networks
   * contribution histories
   * personal interests
   * relationship graphs
The Toolkit should design discovery with consent from the beginning.
Key tensions
   * discoverability vs safety
   * visibility vs consent
   * identity vs pseudonymity
   * matching vs surveillance
   * open maps vs sensitive places
   * contribution history vs privacy
   * reputation vs exclusion
   * AI recommendations vs consent
   * public commons vs protected knowledge

16. Failure, Anti-Patterns, and Structural Drift
Core idea
Failure should be part of the knowledge commons.
The Toolkit should preserve not only best practices, but also recurring breakdowns, harmful patterns, weak structures, incentive failures, coordination breakdowns, and examples of drift.
Failure knowledge helps communities avoid repeating harm.
Related concepts
   * anti-patterns
   * failure modes
   * structural drift
   * governance theater
   * extractive aggregation
   * documentation decay
   * local node drift
   * grant dependency
   * contributor burnout
   * hidden power
   * capture
   * metric gaming
   * token speculation
   * ecological overclaiming
   * AI flattening
   * weak consent
   * weak source lineage
   * implementation overload
   * coordination collapse
Possible lineage references
   * failure case studies
   * DAO failures
   * open-source maintainer burnout literature
   * commons enclosure analysis
   * organizational failure cases
   * governance case forums
   * local project retrospectives
   * public goods funding postmortems
Why it matters
A good Toolkit should not only say what to do. It should help people notice when something is going wrong.
Failure and anti-pattern mapping can inform:
   * Deployment checks
   * Option Library risk notes
   * Review & Maturity states
   * Implementation retrospectives
   * Evolution updates
   * contributor care
   * structural integrity reviews
Key tensions
   * learning from failure vs blame
   * public transparency vs reputational harm
   * naming patterns vs exposing people
   * failure analysis vs cynicism
   * critique vs care
   * unresolved tension vs forced closure
Related Toolkit sections
   * Implementation Memory
   * Evolution
   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
   * Option Library
   * Review & Maturity
   * Regenerative Obligation
________________


Cross-cutting conceptual tensions
The Toolkit should preserve tensions rather than forcing premature resolution.
Open vs protected
Some knowledge should be open. Some should be contextual. Some should be protected. Some should not be mapped publicly.
Local vs global
Reusable patterns are useful, but local context matters. A pattern that works in one place may fail elsewhere.
Broad vs coherent
The Toolkit should be broad enough to hold the field, but coherent enough to navigate.
AI speed vs human stewardship
AI can accelerate curation and synthesis, but human review and source lineage remain essential.
Finance vs regeneration
Funding is necessary, but regenerative coordination should not collapse into financial mechanisms.
Evidence vs living systems
Evidence matters, but living systems cannot be fully represented by metrics.
Discovery vs surveillance
Helping people find each other is powerful, but unsafe visibility can create harm.
Structure vs emergence
Structure helps coordination. Too much structure can block emergence. Too little structure can hide power and overburden informal labor.
Commons vs enclosure
Shared knowledge can become enclosed by platforms, funders, brands, or extractive aggregators.
Participation vs capacity
Open contribution is valuable, but contribution systems must account for burnout, invisible labor, and maintenance burden.
Standardization vs plurality
Interoperability requires shared structures, but different communities need different languages, practices, and contexts.
________________

	Deployment, Review, Option Library
	Culture and narrative
	Implementation Memory, Contributor Care, Regenerative Obligation
	Open research
	Encyclopedia, Review, Research Backlog
	Privacy and consent
	Discovery, Resource Graph, Deployment
	Failure and anti-patterns
	Implementation Memory, Evolution, Option Library
	________________


Working priorities for this section
1. Turn key concepts into Encyclopedia pages
Priority candidates:
   * knowledge commons
   * knowledge gardening
   * resource graph
   * source lineage
   * regenerative web3
   * local node
   * implementation memory
   * structural integrity
   * information asymmetry
   * stewardship obligation
   * public goods funding
   * MRV
   * discovery and affinity
   * AI-assisted curation
   * extractive aggregation
2. Build lineage maps
Useful first lineage maps:
   * commons / P2P lineage
   * regenerative design lineage
   * web3 public goods lineage
   * localism / bioregionalism lineage
   * civic innovation lineage
   * AI / collective intelligence lineage
   * structural integrity / CSIS lineage
   * ReFi / climate web3 lineage
3. Add bridge-language notes
Terms that need bridge-language treatment:
   * governance
   * accountability
   * stakeholders
   * transparency
   * enforcement
   * compliance
   * impact
   * users
   * feedback
   * community
   * ecosystem
   * decentralization
   * regeneration
4. Map tensions explicitly
Some tensions should become open-question pages or anti-pattern pages.
Examples:
   * open knowledge vs protected knowledge
   * public maps vs community safety
   * AI synthesis vs source fidelity
   * regenerative finance vs financialization
   * local specificity vs reusable patterns
   * evidence quality vs participation burden
   * contribution openness vs review quality
5. Connect concepts to resources
Each major concept should eventually link to:
   * source systems
   * key thinkers
   * relevant organizations
   * related tools
   * implementation cases
   * failure cases
   * option entries
   * open questions
