Working summary
The Concept & Idea Ecology helps the Toolkit see the invisible architecture of the field: the ideas, lineages, tensions, metaphors, and assumptions that shape what people build.
A strong ecosystem map should include organizations and tools.
A deeper ecosystem map should also include concepts and lineages.
This section helps ensure that the Toolkit does not only ask:
Who is doing what?
It also asks:
What ideas are moving through the field, where did they come from, what do they make possible, and what risks do they carry?


📚 Option Library
Purpose
The Option Library organizes reusable implementation choices that communities, builders, knowledge gardeners, funders, local organizers, and ecosystem stewards can compare before moving into deployment.
It is not a resource list, tool directory, recommendation engine, or catalog of projects.
It is a structured design space for choices that can be selected, adapted, combined, tested, rejected, or refined in context.
A useful option entry should help someone understand:
   * what reusable choice the option represents;
   * what problem, gap, or tension it addresses;
   * when it may be useful;
   * when it may be risky, unnecessary, or misleading;
   * what roles, conditions, tools, and knowledge it requires;
   * what failure modes to watch for;
   * what consent, power, privacy, or regenerative obligation concerns it creates;
   * what evidence, cases, or source lineages support it;
   * how it connects to deployment and implementation learning.
The Option Library should not tell people what to choose. It should help them understand what they are choosing.
________________


Core Distinction: Option, Tool, Resource, Deployment, Implementation
The Option Library must preserve the distinction between different object types.
Object
	Meaning
	Example
	Concept
	An idea or explanatory construct.
	Source lineage, structural integrity, regenerative obligation
	Resource
	A source, reference, artifact, dataset, report, guide, or source system.
	CSIS guide, SuperBenefit Knowledge Garden, Gitcoin Governance Forum
	Tool
	A specific platform, protocol, app, template, or component.
	Karma GAP, Charmverse, EAS, Hypercerts, Kumu, Hylo
	Option
	A reusable design choice, pattern, mechanism, or pathway.
	Milestone-based funding, source lineage field, opt-in discovery profile
	Deployment
	A context-specific assembly of options, tools, roles, obligations, and operating conditions.
	A local grants round using milestone-based funding, community review, public reports, and a shared treasury
	Implementation
	What actually happens when a deployment is used in practice.
	The actual funding round, including proposals, decisions, updates, conflicts, delays, lessons, and adaptations
	A tool may support an option, but it is not itself the option.
A resource may inform an option, but it is not itself the option.
A deployment may combine several options, but it is not itself the option.
An implementation may generate learning about an option, but one case does not automatically become a reusable pattern.
________________


What Counts as an Option
An entry belongs in the Option Library when it represents a reusable design choice or pathway that could be adapted across more than one context.
An option may be:
   * a funding mechanism;
   * a decision process;
   * a documentation pattern;
   * a discovery pattern;
   * a contribution model;
   * a review process;
   * an identity or access pattern;
   * a mapping approach;
   * a governance or coordination structure;
   * an implementation learning pattern;
   * an infrastructure or publishing pattern;
   * a measurement or evidence approach;
   * a contributor care practice;
   * a legal or institutional design choice.
An entry should not remain in the Option Library if it is primarily:
If the entry is primarily...
	Move or route it to...
	a tool profile
	Resource Graph / Tool Registry
	a raw link
	Resource Graph
	a source system
	Source System Registry
	a concept explanation
	Encyclopedia or Concept & Idea Ecology
	a framework explanation
	Encyclopedia or Concept & Idea Ecology
	a specific project or example
	Implementation Memory or Case Library
	an operational checklist
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	a schema or object type
	Ontology
	a field signal
	Evolution
	an unresolved question
	Open Questions / Research Backlog
	an outdated or weak suggestion
	Archive / Deprecated References
	The Option Library can link to all of these layers, but it should not absorb their functions.
________________


Option Status Labels
Each option should have one visible status label.
Status
	Meaning
	Raw
	Named as a possible option, not yet structured.
	Candidate
	Likely useful, but needs review, source lineage, or clearer conditions.
	Draft
	Developed enough to discuss and compare.
	Reviewed
	Checked by relevant human reviewers.
	Field-informed
	Informed by one or more real implementations.
	Pattern-generating
	Has produced reusable lessons across contexts.
	High-risk
	Requires stronger review before practical use.
	Deprecated
	Preserved for memory, but no longer recommended or current.
	Open question
	Not yet stable enough to treat as an option.
	A polished option should not automatically be treated as reliable. Status should reflect evidence, review, and practical maturity.
________________


Option Entry Template
Field
	Description
	Option name
	Name of the reusable design choice.
	Short description
	What the option is.
	Option family
	Knowledge commons, mapping, governance, funding, discovery, evidence, AI, infrastructure, legal, culture, etc.
	Problem addressed
	What need, gap, or tension this responds to.
	Useful when
	Conditions where the option may fit.
	Not useful when
	Conditions where the option may fail, overcomplicate, distort, or create risk.
	Required roles
	Stewards, reviewers, facilitators, maintainers, signers, contributors, etc.
	Required infrastructure
	Tools, platforms, data structures, channels, repositories, contracts, forms, etc.
	Related tools
	Specific tools or protocols that may support the option.
	Related concepts
	Concepts needed to understand the option.
	Related resources
	Source systems, reports, guides, frameworks, or references.
	Related cases
	Implementations, examples, or failures.
	Risks / failure modes
	What can go wrong.
	Consent / privacy notes
	Representation, data, identity, location, or matching concerns.
	Power / control notes
	Who controls resources, infrastructure, decisions, data, or narratives.
	Regenerative obligation notes
	Who should be credited, protected, consulted, supported, or given a correction path.
	Evidence quality
	What supports this option and how strong the evidence is.
	Deployment requirements
	What must be made explicit before use.
	Maturity status
	Raw, candidate, draft, reviewed, field-informed, pattern-generating, high-risk, deprecated.
	Source lineage
	Where the option comes from or what source systems informed it.
	Open questions
	What remains unresolved.
	________________


Option Families
The following families preserve the conceptual range of the previous Option Library while avoiding tool/resource drift.
Each family should eventually contain a short index, several full option entries, links to supporting tools/resources, and implementation memory from real cases.
________________


1. Knowledge Commons Options
These options help a group create, maintain, review, publish, and evolve shared knowledge.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Public knowledge base
	Shared place for concepts, guides, resources, and working memory.
	Google Docs, Notion, GitHub, Astro, Starlight, Quartz
	Becomes outdated, centralized, or hard to navigate.
	Candidate
	Digital garden
	Living, linked, evolving knowledge space.
	Obsidian, Quartz, Logseq, Foam, SuperBenefit Knowledge Garden
	Too personal, weak review, hard for newcomers.
	Candidate
	Component index
	Structured catalog of reusable components, templates, and patterns.
	Knowledge Commoning Swarm, Resource Graph, DAOstar OpenGrants
	Becomes a directory without context.
	Candidate
	Source lineage field
	Metadata field preserving source origin, attribution, and use type.
	W3C PROV, Dublin Core, Markdown frontmatter
	Treated as bureaucracy instead of obligation.
	Draft
	Glossary
	Shared definitions for key terms.
	Markdown, docs, wiki, web glossary tools
	False precision; contested terms hidden.
	Candidate
	Open-question page
	Dedicated page for unresolved questions.
	Docs, GitHub issues, Notion database
	Questions pile up without synthesis.
	Candidate
	Review and maturity system
	Visible states for trust, review, and readiness.
	GitHub PRs, Notion status fields, Airtable, labels
	Creates false authority if poorly maintained.
	Draft
	Contribution guide
	Instructions for adding or improving knowledge.
	GitHub, docs, community guides
	Too complex for casual contributors.
	Candidate
	Knowledge maintenance rhythm
	Regular practice of pruning, updating, archiving, and resurfacing.
	Monthly calls, review sprints, changelogs
	Invisible labor and burnout.
	Candidate
	AI-assisted draft with human review
	Uses AI for initial synthesis while preserving human stewardship.
	LLMs, RAG workflows, GitHub review
	False authority, source loss, hallucination.
	High-risk candidate
	Pattern harvest
	Turns repeated implementation lessons into reusable patterns.
	Retrospectives, implementation notes, pattern templates
	Overgeneralizes from weak evidence.
	Candidate
	Source system registry
	Identifies living knowledge environments that require attribution and return paths.
	Wikis, forums, GitHub repos, public libraries, knowledge gardens
	Treats source systems as extractable link pools.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Source Lineage Field
Short description
A metadata pattern that records where knowledge, resources, concepts, claims, options, or implementation lessons came from and how they are being used.
Problem addressed
Knowledge commons often aggregate ideas, resources, and practices without preserving authorship, community context, source systems, or use type. This creates attribution loss, extractive aggregation, false authority, and weak trust.
Useful when
   * The Toolkit summarizes, adapts, synthesizes, or cites external work.
   * A resource comes from a living source system such as a wiki, forum, map, repository, or knowledge garden.
   * A concept, option, or case is influenced by a specific community, framework, person, event, or implementation.
Not useful when
   * A note is purely private, temporary, or not intended to enter the commons.
   * The contribution is too raw to classify. In that case, mark it Raw or To-place first.
Required roles
Source lineage steward, resource gardener, reviewer, contributor.
Required infrastructure
A source field, citation or URL field, use type field, review status field, and correction or return-path field for high-value source systems.
Related tools
GitHub, Markdown frontmatter, Zotero, Airtable, Notion, Google Sheets, W3C PROV-inspired fields, Dublin Core-inspired metadata.
Related concepts
Provenance, attribution, source system, regenerative obligation, commons stewardship, extractive aggregation, review and maturity.
Risks / failure modes
   * Source lineage becomes a checkbox instead of a practice of care.
   * Contributors avoid adding resources because the process feels too heavy.
   * Source systems are credited generically but not meaningfully linked back.
   * AI-generated summaries lose original source context.
   * Attribution privileges aggregators over original communities or knowledge holders.
Consent / privacy notes
If the source includes people, communities, exact locations, local ecological knowledge, or sensitive internal notes, source lineage should include visibility and reuse limits.
Regenerative obligation notes
Source lineage is not just citation. It is a minimal obligation toward contributors, source communities, knowledge holders, and future users.
Deployment requirements
Define required fields, optional fields, who fills them, and how corrections can be submitted.
Maturity status
Draft / Priority option
Open questions
   * What is the minimum viable source lineage field set?
   * Which entries require full source lineage before publication?
   * How should source systems receive return paths?
________________


Priority Entry: Review and Maturity System
Short description
A visible status system that helps readers understand whether content, resources, options, claims, and deployment templates are raw, draft, reviewed, field-informed, high-risk, or deprecated.
Problem addressed
Large living documents often contain uneven material. Without visible maturity states, polished drafts may look authoritative, raw notes may be mistaken for decisions, and high-risk content may be reused without review.
Useful when
   * A knowledge commons contains mixed content states.
   * AI-assisted content is present.
   * Some content involves legal, ecological, governance, funding, privacy, or public claims.
   * Contributors need to know where to improve, review, archive, or rely on material.
Not useful when
   * The system is so small and informal that maturity labels add more overhead than clarity.
   * Labels are added but never reviewed or updated.
Required roles
Reviewer, domain reviewer, source reviewer, option librarian, deployment steward, knowledge steward.
Required infrastructure
Status labels, review type labels, high-risk triggers, review queue or review notes, and optional changelog.
Related tools
GitHub PRs, Notion status fields, Airtable, Google Sheets, labels, frontmatter, review queues.
Related concepts
Review, maturity, false authority, AI-assisted content, high-risk content, source fidelity, field-informed learning.
Risks / failure modes
   * Labels become stale.
   * Labels create false hierarchy.
   * Review bottlenecks form around a few people.
   * High-risk content is labeled but not handled differently.
   * Review becomes gatekeeping rather than stewardship.
Consent / privacy notes
Community profiles, people maps, exact locations, sensitive local knowledge, and AI-assisted recommendations require privacy and consent review before public use.
Power / control notes
Review power can become hidden governance. Reviewer roles, criteria, and appeal/correction paths should be visible.
Regenerative obligation notes
Review protects contributors, source communities, affected communities, ecosystems, and future users from misuse or overclaiming.
Deployment requirements
Define maturity states, review types, high-risk triggers, reviewer roles, and review cadence.
Maturity status
Draft / Priority option
Open questions
   * Which sections require review before public release?
   * Who can assign or change maturity labels?
   * How should review labor be recognized?
________________


2. Resource Graph and Ecosystem Mapping Options
These options help a community map the field around it without collapsing into a link dump.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Resource Graph
	Structured registry of entities, resources, tools, source systems, and relationships.
	Google Sheets, Airtable, Notion, CSV, GitHub, graph databases
	Becomes a stale spreadsheet or overbuilt graph.
	Draft
	Ecosystem Atlas
	Multi-view interpretive map of actors, tools, concepts, places, and relationships.
	Kumu, Figma, Miro, graph databases
	Too broad; visually impressive but hard to use.
	Candidate
	Source system registry
	List of living knowledge systems to credit and revisit.
	Wikis, forums, maps, GitHub repos
	Treats source systems as extractable link pools.
	Candidate
	Actor map
	Map of people, orgs, communities, and networks.
	Kumu, Graph Commons, spreadsheets
	Consent and representation risks.
	High-risk candidate
	Tool map
	Map of tools by function and interoperability.
	Resource Graph, GitHub, docs
	Tool comparison without context.
	Candidate
	Concept map
	Visual map of ideas and relationships.
	Kumu, Loopy, Miro, FigJam
	Beautiful but vague.
	Candidate
	Place / bioregion map
	Map of local nodes, watersheds, communities, projects, and ecological contexts.
	Hylo, QGIS, OpenStreetMap, Restor
	Exposes sensitive locations or communities.
	High-risk candidate
	Event harvest map
	Captures resources and connections from gatherings.
	Event notes, recordings, docs
	Outputs disappear after event.
	Candidate
	Relationship grammar
	Shared vocabulary for relationships between entries.
	Ontology docs, graph schemas
	Too many relationship types.
	Candidate
	Function tagging
	Tags resources by what they help do.
	publish, map, fund, verify, coordinate, sensemake, discover
	Tags become inconsistent.
	Candidate
	To-place inbox
	Holding area for resources not yet classified.
	Docs, spreadsheets, GitHub issues
	Becomes permanent junk drawer.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Resource Graph
Short description
A structured registry that classifies resources, tools, organizations, source systems, cases, datasets, frameworks, and other entities by type, function, source lineage, review status, and relationships.
Problem addressed
Regenerative web3, public goods, knowledge commons, local coordination, and ecological stewardship resources are scattered across docs, forums, maps, repos, websites, and community memory. Without structure, the Toolkit becomes a link dump.
Useful when
   * A group needs to map what exists across a complex ecosystem.
   * Resources need source lineage, type, function, and review status.
   * Entries must route into Encyclopedia, Option Library, Implementation Memory, Deployment, or Open Questions.
   * Contributors need a practical table they can add to without understanding the whole architecture.
Not useful when
   * The group has too few resources to justify structure.
   * The graph becomes overengineered before a simple table is useful.
   * Relationships are added without enough clarity to support navigation or action.
Required roles
Resource gardener, source lineage steward, reviewer, knowledge steward.
Required infrastructure
Structured table or database; type, domain, function, source lineage, status, and notes fields; optional relationship fields; contributor workflow.
Related tools
Google Sheets, Airtable, Notion, GitHub, CSV, Kumu, Neo4j, RDF, Geo-like knowledge spaces.
Related concepts
Ontology, Resource Graph, Ecosystem Atlas, source system, source lineage, relationship grammar, review and maturity.
Risks / failure modes
   * Link dump with metadata.
   * Stale spreadsheet.
   * Duplicate entries.
   * Too many categories.
   * Source systems treated as extractable resources.
   * Sensitive people, places, or communities mapped without consent.
   * Graph theater: impressive maps that do not help action.
Consent / privacy notes
Actor maps, people profiles, community profiles, local maps, and exact locations require consent and visibility rules.
Power / control notes
Whoever controls the Resource Graph can shape discoverability, legitimacy, and attention. Contribution and correction paths should be visible.
Regenerative obligation notes
The graph should credit source systems, avoid extraction, and route value or attention back where possible.
Deployment requirements
Start with a minimum viable table before moving to graph databases or complex infrastructure.
Maturity status
Draft / Priority option
Open questions
   * What are the minimum required fields?
   * Which relationship types are useful enough for v0.1?
   * Which resources should be structured first?
________________


3. Governance and Coordination Options
These options help groups make decisions, distribute roles, coordinate work, handle tensions, and surface adverse signals.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Working group model
	Small groups responsible for specific domains.
	DAO working groups, guilds, circles
	Fragmentation, unclear authority.
	Candidate
	Council model
	Smaller body holds decision or stewardship role.
	Councils, multisigs, committees
	Capture, legitimacy gaps.
	High-risk candidate
	Consent-based decision-making
	Decisions proceed unless there is a reasoned objection.
	Sociocracy, Loomio
	Requires facilitation skill.
	Candidate
	Proposal flow
	Structured process for submitting, reviewing, and deciding proposals.
	Forums, Charmverse, Snapshot, Gardens, GitHub
	Process theater, bottlenecks.
	Candidate
	Delegated review
	Trusted reviewers assess resources, proposals, or content.
	Review committees, curators
	Reviewer capture or fatigue.
	Candidate
	Role-based stewardship
	Roles define obligations, not only permissions.
	Hats Protocol, Holacracy, Sociocracy
	Role inflation, unclear accountability.
	Candidate
	Multisig execution
	Shared wallet controlled by multiple signers.
	Safe, multisigs
	Hidden power, signer inactivity.
	High-risk candidate
	Dispute or objection path
	Process for contesting decisions or representations.
	Governance forums, arbitration, mediation
	Underused if socially unsafe.
	Candidate
	Conflict transformation pathway
	Structured way to work with conflict as signal.
	Mediation, restorative practices
	Requires skilled facilitation.
	Candidate
	Decision log
	Record of what was decided, by whom, and why.
	GitHub, docs, forums
	Not maintained.
	Candidate
	Stewardship rotation
	Rotating roles to avoid centralization and burnout.
	Role cycles, councils
	Loss of continuity.
	Candidate
	Adverse signal channel
	Place where harms, risks, or failures can be surfaced.
	Forms, ombuds, trusted stewards
	No named response.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Adverse Signal Channel
Short description
A designated pathway for surfacing harms, risks, failures, objections, weak signals, or structural concerns, paired with a named response process.
Problem addressed
Many coordination systems collect feedback but do not define where adverse signals go, who sees them, who responds, or what happens next. Detection without named response allows risks to accumulate.
Useful when
   * A deployment affects funds, people, communities, ecological claims, public maps, AI recommendations, governance decisions, or local contexts.
   * Participants need a safe way to surface concerns.
   * Structural integrity review identifies unresolved exposure.
Not useful when
   * The channel exists only symbolically.
   * No one is responsible for response.
   * Social conditions make reporting unsafe.
Required roles
Response steward, reviewer, facilitator or mediator where needed, deployment steward.
Required infrastructure
Reporting form, private channel, trusted steward, ombuds path, issue tracker, or governance forum; named response process; escalation and privacy rules; unresolved issue log.
Related tools
Forms, secure email, Signal, private channels, GitHub issues, Charmverse, forums, ombuds processes, mediation practices.
Related concepts
Structural integrity, adverse signal, named response, information asymmetry, power/control, regenerative obligation, conflict transformation.
Risks / failure modes
   * No named response.
   * Retaliation or social risk.
   * Concerns disappear into private channels.
   * Only loud or high-status participants are heard.
   * Channel becomes a complaint sink instead of a learning path.
   * Sensitive information is exposed.
Consent / privacy notes
Reports may contain sensitive personal, community, financial, ecological, or conflict-related information. Visibility levels must be defined.
Power / control notes
The party receiving adverse signals should not be the only party with power to interpret or dismiss them.
Regenerative obligation notes
A commons must create pathways for correction, contestation, and repair, especially for people or communities affected by representation, funding, mapping, or implementation decisions.
Deployment requirements
Every high-risk deployment should identify what adverse signals are expected, where they go, who responds, and what response options exist.
Maturity status
Candidate / Priority option
Open questions
   * Who can safely receive adverse signals?
   * What signals require pause or redesign?
   * How should unresolved signals update the Toolkit?
________________


4. Funding, Allocation, and Resource Flow Options
These options help communities allocate money, grants, matching funds, rewards, local vouchers, mutual credit, and other resources.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Direct grants
	Funds allocated directly by a person or committee.
	Gitcoin, foundations, councils
	Centralization, bias.
	High-risk candidate
	Quadratic funding
	Matching funds amplify broad support.
	Gitcoin, Allo, QF paper
	Sybil attacks, popularity bias.
	High-risk candidate
	Retroactive funding
	Rewards work after impact is observed.
	Optimism Retro Funding, Hypercerts
	Hard to assess impact fairly.
	High-risk candidate
	Milestone-based funding
	Payments tied to progress milestones.
	Karma GAP, grant agreements
	Over-administration, milestone gaming.
	Draft
	Conviction voting
	Preference accumulates over time.
	Commons Stack, Gardens-like systems
	Capture by patient capital or insiders.
	High-risk candidate
	Participatory budgeting
	Community allocates a budget collectively.
	Decidim, civic PB systems
	Participation fatigue.
	Candidate
	Matching pool
	Funds match donations, votes, or other signals.
	Gitcoin, Allo
	Gaming and eligibility disputes.
	High-risk candidate
	Streaming payments
	Continuous funds flow over time.
	Superfluid
	Harder to stop or review.
	Candidate
	Donation pool
	Shared pool for donors to support projects.
	Giveth, Open Collective
	Weak accountability if not designed.
	Candidate
	Local vouchers
	Locally redeemable promises for goods or services.
	Sarafu-like systems, Citizen Wallet
	Legal/tax complexity, liquidity issues.
	High-risk candidate
	Community currency
	Local exchange medium or mutual credit system.
	Grassroots Economics, Circles, GoodDollar
	Trust, compliance, adoption burden.
	High-risk candidate
	Impact attestations
	Structured records of claims or contributions.
	EAS, Hypercerts, Green Goods
	Overclaiming, weak evidence.
	High-risk candidate
	Public goods reporting
	Transparent updates on use of funds and progress.
	Karma GAP, OpenGrants, OSO
	Reporting burden, metric theater.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Milestone-Based Funding
Short description
A funding pattern where disbursement, review, or accountability is tied to defined project milestones, deliverables, or progress updates.
Problem addressed
Public goods and regenerative projects often receive funds without clear progress structure, reporting cadence, evidence expectations, or learning loops. This can create weak accountability, funder uncertainty, and poor implementation memory.
Useful when
   * Projects can define meaningful milestones without over-bureaucratizing the work.
   * Funders need progress visibility.
   * Project teams need staged support.
   * Milestones are used for learning and coordination, not only compliance.
Not useful when
   * Work is exploratory and cannot be meaningfully divided into milestones.
   * Milestones incentivize performative deliverables over real learning or impact.
   * Small grassroots groups lack reporting capacity.
   * Funders use milestones to impose extractive oversight.
Required roles
Project team, funding steward, reviewer, milestone approver, optional community reviewer.
Required infrastructure
Project profile, milestone template, reporting channel, evidence submission process, review process, funding disbursement process.
Related tools
Karma GAP, Charmverse, GitHub, Notion, Google Drive, OpenGrants, public reports, multisig processes, EAS, Hypercerts.
Related concepts
Public goods funding, accountability, stewardship obligation, source lineage, evidence quality, signal vs conclusion, regenerative obligation.
Risks / failure modes
   * Milestone gaming.
   * Metric theater.
   * Administrative overload.
   * Reporting replaces implementation.
   * Weak signals treated as conclusions.
   * Funders ignore qualitative context.
   * Reporting requirements exclude smaller or less formal groups.
Consent / privacy notes
Milestone reports may include people, places, community activities, funding details, or ecological sites. Visibility and consent should be defined.
Power / control notes
Milestone approval gives reviewers or funders significant power. Criteria, appeal paths, and disbursement control should be visible.
Regenerative obligation notes
Funded projects owe some form of reporting to funders, collaborators, affected communities, and future learners, but reporting should not become extractive bureaucracy.
Deployment requirements
Before use, define milestone criteria, review roles, evidence standards, payment triggers, appeal path, and reporting burden limits.
Maturity status
Draft / Field-informed
Open questions
   * What kinds of milestones are appropriate for local regenerative work?
   * How can reporting remain lightweight and useful?
   * When should milestone funding be avoided?
________________


5. Discovery, Affinity, and Participation Options
These options help people find topics, resources, communities, collaborators, requests, offers, and contribution pathways.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Role-based entry points
	Navigation by user role or intention.
	Docs, websites, onboarding flows
	Over-segmentation.
	Candidate
	Interest profile
	Person or group indicates interests.
	Forms, profiles, Hylo-like systems
	Privacy and inference risk.
	High-risk candidate
	Skill map
	Map of skills people offer.
	Directories, profiles, community platforms
	Outdated, status games.
	High-risk candidate
	Needs / offers board
	Public or community board of requests and offers.
	Hylo, forums, Airtable, Notion
	Noise, unclear fulfillment.
	Candidate
	Local discovery map
	Map of nearby communities or projects.
	Hylo Map, OpenStreetMap, QGIS
	Location sensitivity.
	High-risk candidate
	Opt-in matchable profile
	Profile can be used for recommendations or matching.
	Consent-based systems
	Consent ambiguity.
	High-risk candidate
	Contributor pathway
	Steps for moving from interest to contribution.
	Onboarding docs, bounties, GitHub issues
	Too rigid or gamified.
	Candidate
	Learning path
	Curated sequence for a topic or role.
	Docs, courses, playlists
	Becomes outdated.
	Candidate
	Opportunity board
	List of tasks, grants, events, roles, or calls.
	Charmverse, GitHub issues, Dework-like tools
	Maintenance burden.
	Candidate
	Mentor / peer matching
	Matches people for learning or support.
	Forms, community platforms
	Emotional labor, privacy.
	High-risk candidate
	AI-assisted recommendation with consent
	Uses AI to suggest resources or connections under explicit consent rules.
	RAG, embeddings, recommender systems
	Surveillance, bias, false matches.
	High-risk candidate
	Priority Entry: Opt-In Discovery Profile
Short description
A consent-based profile that allows a person, group, community, project, or local node to be discoverable under defined visibility, contact, matching, and data-use conditions.
Problem addressed
Communities need ways to find relevant people, projects, skills, needs, offers, and local nodes. But discovery systems can easily become surveillance systems if visibility, contact, location, identity, and AI use are not consent-based.
Useful when
   * People or communities want to be discoverable.
   * Discovery requires more than a public directory.
   * Matching depends on interests, skills, needs, offers, location, or contribution history.
   * The system can support clear consent and correction paths.
Not useful when
   * Consent is vague or one-time.
   * Users cannot control visibility.
   * Profiles expose sensitive community or location information.
   * AI recommendations use profile data without explicit permission.
Required roles
Profile owner, discovery steward, privacy reviewer, technical maintainer, community reviewer where communities are represented.
Required infrastructure
Profile form, visibility settings, consent fields, contact preference fields, correction or withdrawal process, optional matching engine or directory view.
Related tools
Hylo, forms, Airtable, Notion, community directories, Solid-like data systems, decentralized identity tools, consent-based matching tools.
Related concepts
Discovery and affinity, privacy, consent, identity, local coordination, community representation, data minimization, regenerative obligation.
Risks / failure modes
   * Public visibility without real consent.
   * Outdated profiles.
   * Unwanted contact.
   * Location exposure.
   * AI inference beyond consent.
   * Social graph exposure.
   * Contributor histories used as reputation without context.
Consent / privacy notes
Consent should be granular: listed, described, contacted, recommended, matched, location used, contribution history used, AI-assisted routing used.
Power / control notes
The profile owner should control visibility and updates. The system should not force discoverability as a condition of participation.
Regenerative obligation notes
Discovery should create useful connection without extracting identity, availability, social networks, or local knowledge.
Deployment requirements
Define profile fields, visibility levels, consent categories, location precision, update cadence, correction path, and AI-use restrictions.
Maturity status
Candidate / High-risk / Priority option
Open questions
   * What is the minimum useful profile?
   * How should local/community profiles differ from individual profiles?
   * What should never be used for AI-assisted matching?
________________

These options help communities represent claims, evidence, monitoring, outcomes, verification, and learning.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Claim-evidence record
	Separates claims from supporting evidence.
	EAS, Hypercerts, docs, databases
	Evidence quality varies.
	Candidate
	Impact report
	Narrative and data report of outcomes.
	Karma GAP, OpenGrants, public docs
	Marketing over learning.
	Candidate
	MRV workflow
	Monitoring, reporting, and verification process.
	OpenMRV, Verra, Plan Vivo, Gold Standard
	High burden, false precision.
	High-risk candidate
	Community science observation
	Local observations contribute evidence.
	iNaturalist, GBIF, farmOS
	Data quality and consent issues.
	High-risk candidate
	Field record system
	Structured logs from ecological or local work.
	farmOS, spreadsheets, mobile forms
	Maintenance burden.
	Candidate
	Attested contribution
	Onchain or signed record of contribution.
	EAS, Hypercerts
	Privacy, overclaiming.
	High-risk candidate
	Biodiversity evidence checklist
	Checklist for biodiversity-related claims.
	GBIF, iNaturalist, ecological review
	Incomplete indicators.
	High-risk candidate
	Restoration monitoring map
	Map and monitor restoration sites.
	Restor, Global Forest Watch, QGIS
	Remote sensing mismatch.

	Failure / adverse outcome report
	Records what did not work or caused harm.
	Retrospectives, incident reports

	Candidate
	Evidence quality label
	Marks confidence or review level.
	Maturity badges, review workflows
	Can appear more precise than it is.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Claim-Evidence Record
Short description
A structured record that separates a claim from the evidence, signals, sources, uncertainty, and review status supporting or challenging it.
Problem addressed
Impact reports, grant updates, ecological claims, and public goods narratives often blur the difference between activity, output, outcome, impact, signal, evidence, and conclusion.
Useful when
   * A project makes public claims about impact, progress, ecological outcomes, governance outcomes, or funding use.
   * Attestations, hypercerts, reports, or dashboards are used.
   * Reviewers need to distinguish evidence from interpretation.
   * The Toolkit needs to avoid treating detection as proof.
Not useful when
   * The claim is low-stakes and informal.
   * Evidence standards are too heavy for early learning notes.
   * The record creates false precision around complex social or ecological outcomes.
Required roles
Claim author, evidence curator, reviewer, domain reviewer where needed, community or ecological reviewer for high-risk claims.
Required infrastructure
Claim field, evidence field, source lineage field, evidence type field, review status field, uncertainty field, correction path.
Related tools
EAS, Hypercerts, Karma GAP, OpenGrants, GitHub, Google Docs, databases, dashboards, spreadsheets.
Related concepts
Signal vs conclusion, evidence quality, source lineage, MRV, impact integrity, regenerative obligation, overclaiming.
Risks / failure modes
   * Weak evidence presented as strong evidence.
   * Activity metrics treated as impact.
   * Onchain records treated as truth.
   * Ecological claims made without ecological review.
   * Community experience contradicts public reporting.
   * Evidence is cherry-picked.
Consent / privacy notes
Evidence may include photos, locations, personal data, community stories, field observations, or ecological site data. Visibility and reuse permissions are required.
Power / control notes
The party making the claim should not be the only party assessing its evidence quality.
Regenerative obligation notes
Claims should be proportional, contextual, correctable, and accountable to affected communities and ecosystems.
Deployment requirements
Define claim categories, evidence types, review requirements, uncertainty labels, and correction path.
Maturity status
Candidate / Priority option
Open questions
   * What is the minimum viable claim-evidence template?
   * Which claims require ecological, legal, community, or structural review?
   * How should uncertainty be represented without weakening useful learning?
________________

	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks

	AI-assisted classification
	AI suggests tags, types, or relationships.
	LLMs, embeddings, RAG
	Semantic drift, hallucination.
	High-risk candidate
	AI-assisted summary
	AI summarizes notes, sources, or events.
	LLMs
	Source loss, flattening.
	High-risk candidate
	Human-in-the-loop review
	Human review required before publication.
	GitHub PRs, review queues
	Bottleneck.
	Candidate
	Semantic search
	Search by meaning, not only keywords.
	Embeddings, vector databases
	Poor explainability.
	Candidate
	RAG knowledge assistant
	AI answers from curated sources.
	RAG pipelines
	Overconfidence, retrieval gaps.
	High-risk candidate
	Deliberation synthesis
	Summarizes group discussion and disagreement.
	Harmonica, Pol.is, Kialo
	False consensus.
	High-risk candidate
	Signal clustering
	Groups repeated issues, questions, or feedback.
	AI tools, spreadsheets, sensemaking tools

	Unresolved tension log
	Keeps tensions visible rather than forcing closure.
	Docs, issue trackers
	Becomes ignored.

	Pattern extraction
	Turns repeated observations into candidate patterns.
	AI + human synthesis
	Overgeneralization.
	High-risk candidate
	Model use disclosure
	Labels AI-assisted content or recommendations.
	Metadata fields
	Not consistently applied.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: AI-Assisted Draft with Human Review
Short description
A workflow where AI supports drafting, summarizing, classifying, or synthesizing, but human stewards review source fidelity, context, uncertainty, and publication readiness before content enters the commons as knowledge.
Problem addressed
AI can accelerate knowledge gardening but can also create false authority, hallucinated connections, source loss, flattened context, and overconfident synthesis.
Useful when
   * The source material is large, fragmented, or difficult to organize manually.
   * AI output is treated as draft, not knowledge.
   * Source lineage and human review are required.
   * The workflow has clear labels for AI-assisted content.
Not useful when
   * No human reviewer is available.
   * Sources are sensitive, private, or consent-limited.
   * The output will influence high-risk deployment without review.
   * The task requires strong factual precision without source verification.
Required roles
AI operator, human reviewer, source lineage steward, domain reviewer where needed.
Required infrastructure
AI-use label, source list, review checklist, version history, correction path.
Related tools
LLMs, RAG workflows, semantic search, GitHub review, Google Docs suggestions, knowledge graph tools.
Related concepts
AI-assisted curation, false authority, source fidelity, human-in-the-loop review, semantic drift, synthesis, review and maturity.
Risks / failure modes
   * Hallucinated facts or relationships.
   * Lost source context.
   * Flattened community nuance.
   * Polished draft mistaken for reviewed knowledge.
   * AI recommendations violate consent.
   * Review labor becomes invisible.
Consent / privacy notes
Do not use sensitive people, community, location, identity, contribution history, or private governance data in AI workflows without explicit permission.
Power / control notes
AI workflows can centralize interpretation in whoever prompts, selects, and edits outputs. Review and source visibility are necessary.
Regenerative obligation notes
AI should support human knowledge stewardship, not erase contributors, sources, local context, or dissent.
Deployment requirements
Define AI-use labels, what content AI can process, who reviews outputs, what cannot be published without review, and how corrections are handled.
Maturity status
Candidate / High-risk
Open questions
   * Which AI uses should be prohibited or require stronger review?
   * How should AI-assisted drafts be labeled in the public Toolkit?
   * What is the minimum source fidelity checklist?
________________

	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Modular Document Set
Short description
A publishing and stewardship pattern that decomposes a large working document into separate but connected artifacts.
Problem addressed
A single large living document can become difficult to read, maintain, review, or use. Sections accumulate without clear stewardship, and contributors struggle to tell what is normative, descriptive, or provisional.
Useful when
   * The document is too large for most contributors to read end-to-end.
   * Different sections need different review cadences.
   * Registries, architecture, ontology, and working notes are mixed together.
   * The team needs clearer contribution paths.
Not useful when
   * The project is still too early to justify multiple artifacts.
   * Fragmentation would make navigation worse.
   * There is no steward responsible for keeping modules linked.
Required roles
Knowledge steward, document maintainer, ontology steward, option librarian, resource graph steward.
Required infrastructure
Clear navigation, stable filenames/tabs, cross-links, changelog, contribution guide, and status labels.
Related tools
Google Docs tabs, GitHub, Markdown, Notion, Obsidian, static docs sites.
Related concepts
Entropy management, normative vs descriptive vs provisional content, minimum ontology, review and maturity, knowledge stewardship.
Risks / failure modes
   * Modules drift apart.
   * Contributors do not know where to add content.
   * Working notes still become hidden governance.
   * Splitting becomes busywork instead of stewardship.
   * The architecture becomes harder, not easier, to navigate.
Deployment requirements
Start with five artifacts: Architecture Specification, Minimum Ontology, Option Library, Resource Registry, and Working Notes. Keep a clear index that shows how they connect.
Maturity status
Draft / Priority option
Open questions
   * Which sections should be split first?
   * What remains in the master document?
   * Who maintains cross-links and version history?
________________

	Example tools / references

	Steward ownership
	Ownership designed around mission stewardship.
	Purpose Foundation
	Legal complexity.

	DAO legal wrapper
	Legal form around DAO operations.

	Open license
	Defines reuse rights for knowledge.
	Creative Commons
	Wrong license choice.

	Capture or bureaucracy.

	Policy template

	Crypto Altruists nonprofit policy resources
	Requires legal review.
	High-risk candidate
	Priority Entry: Open License
Short description
A clearly stated reuse license for Toolkit content, resources, templates, or published materials.
Problem addressed
Knowledge commons need reuse rights to be legible. Without licensing clarity, contributors and users may not know whether content can be copied, adapted, translated, remixed, or published elsewhere.
Useful when
   * The Toolkit publishes public material.
   * Templates or guides are meant to be reused.
   * Contributors need clarity about how their work can circulate.
   * Source systems with their own licenses are referenced or adapted.
Not useful when
   * Content includes sensitive, private, consent-limited, or community-restricted knowledge.
   * The Toolkit has not clarified what it owns, adapts, or merely references.
   * Legal review is needed but unavailable.
Required roles
Knowledge steward, legal/institutional reviewer where needed, contributor steward.
Required infrastructure
License notice, contribution expectations, source lineage fields, and restrictions for content that is not openly reusable.
Related tools
Creative Commons licenses, open-source licenses, contributor agreements, source lineage metadata.
Related concepts
Open knowledge, commons stewardship, attribution, source lineage, consent, non-extractive reuse.
Risks / failure modes
   * Applying an open license to content the Toolkit does not own.
   * Reusing source-system material outside its license or community norms.
   * Treating open licensing as permission to extract.
   * Choosing a license that blocks intended reuse.
   * Publishing sensitive community knowledge under an overly permissive license.
Deployment requirements
Clarify which parts of the Toolkit are original, adapted, cited, private, public, or restricted. Do not apply one license blindly to all material.
Maturity status
Candidate / Requires legal review
Open questions
   * What license fits the Toolkit’s public materials?
   * How should adapted source-system material be handled?
   * What content should never be openly licensed?
________________


10. Culture, Care, and Social Practice Options
These options support the relational infrastructure of the commons.
Key Options
Option
	Short description
	Example tools / references
	Key risks
	Status
	Onboarding ritual
	Welcomes people into shared context.
	Community calls, docs, workshops
	Performative if not supported.
	Candidate
	Community call rhythm
	Regular gathering for alignment and updates.
	Zoom, Discord, Hylo, forums
	Meeting fatigue.
	Candidate
	Recognition practice
	Names and appreciates contributions.
	POAPs, shoutouts, attestations
	Status games.
	Candidate
	Contributor care check
	Regular check on capacity and burnout.
	Surveys, retrospectives
	Ignored if inconvenient.
	Candidate
	Role offboarding
	Clear way to leave or transfer roles.
	Role docs, handoff templates
	Often forgotten.
	Candidate
	Retrospective ritual
	Structured reflection after work.
	After-action reviews
	Turns into vague discussion.
	Candidate
	Repair pathway
	Process for addressing harm or conflict.
	Mediation, restorative practice
	Needs trust and skill.
	High-risk candidate
	Movement glossary
	Shared language and translation layer.
	Docs, glossary pages
	Insider jargon.
	Candidate
	Story archive
	Preserves narratives, origin stories, and lessons.
	Blogs, podcasts, videos
	Mythologizes instead of learns.
	Candidate
	Seasonal planning rhythm
	Periodic planning and reflection cycle.
	Quarterly cycles, sprints
	Can become bureaucratic.
	Candidate
	Priority Entry: Implementation Retrospective
Short description
A structured reflection after a deployment, project, event, campaign, funding round, sprint, or experiment that captures what happened, what worked, what failed, what signals emerged, and what should update the Toolkit.
Problem addressed
Projects often produce outputs but lose the learning. Without retrospectives, the Toolkit becomes theoretical and communities repeat the same failures.
Useful when
   * A deployment has completed a phase.
   * A funding round, event, sprint, local campaign, or tool experiment has generated signals.
   * Something failed or created friction.
   * The Toolkit needs to harvest patterns without overgeneralizing.
Not useful when
   * Participants are too exhausted and the process would become extractive.
   * The retrospective has no route into Implementation Memory or Evolution.
   * The group cannot create enough safety to discuss failure honestly.
Required roles
Facilitator, participants, note-taker, implementation memory steward, optional reviewer.
Required infrastructure
Retrospective template, safe discussion space, notes or recording rules, source lineage and consent notes, routing path into Implementation Memory and Evolution.
Related tools
Google Docs, Miro, FigJam, Charmverse, GitHub issues, forms, meeting notes, implementation case templates.
Related concepts
Implementation Memory, Evolution, pattern harvest, unresolved tension, contributor care, regenerative obligation, source lineage.
Risks / failure modes
   * Vague discussion with no usable outputs.
   * Failure is hidden due to reputational fear.
   * Hidden labor is not named.
   * Lessons are captured but never routed into the Toolkit.
   * Participants are asked to reflect without care or reciprocity.
Consent / privacy notes
Retrospectives may include conflict, failure, private community context, financial issues, or sensitive local information. Decide what can be public, anonymized, internal, or omitted.
Power / control notes
Facilitators and editors shape the memory of what happened. Participants should be able to correct or contest summaries.
Regenerative obligation notes
Implementation learning should credit participants, protect sensitive context, and support future users without extracting from the people who did the work.
Deployment requirements
Define when retrospectives happen, who participates, where outputs go, how privacy is handled, and what updates the Toolkit.
Maturity status
Draft / Priority option
Open questions
   * What is the minimum useful retrospective?
   * How should failed experiments be represented without blame?
   * How does a retrospective become a pattern harvest?
________________

Structural Review Notes for Options
Every full option entry should eventually be reviewed through the following lenses.
1. Conditions
What must be true for this option to work?
Examples:
   * sufficient shared context;
   * clear participants;
   * stable source of truth;
   * review capacity;
   * facilitation capacity;
   * technical maintenance;
   * legal understanding;
   * community legitimacy;
   * evidence standards;
   * privacy boundaries.
2. Dependencies
What does the option depend on?
Examples:
   * a tool;
   * a role;
   * a funder;
   * a dataset;
   * a protocol;
   * a shared process;
   * a social norm;
   * a legal entity;
   * an API;
   * a review committee;
   * a local steward.
3. Failure Modes
What can go wrong?
Examples:
   * capture;
   * gaming;
   * burnout;
   * overcomplexity;
   * underuse;
   * poor evidence;
   * weak consent;
   * maintenance decay;
   * hidden power;
   * false legitimacy;
   * platform lock-in;
   * financialization;
   * metric theater;
   * social exclusion.
4. Power and Control
Who controls:
   * funds;
   * infrastructure;
   * admin access;
   * metadata;
   * moderation;
   * review;
   * publication;
   * identity;
   * treasury;
   * decision paths;
   * data exports;
   * public narrative?
5. Consent and Privacy
Does this option involve:
   * people profiles;
   * locations;
   * community representation;
   * contribution histories;
   * sensitive local knowledge;
   * contact preferences;
   * identity data;
   * recommendation systems;
   * AI-assisted matching;
   * ecological site data?
6. Regenerative Obligation
Who should be credited, consulted, protected, compensated, or given a correction path?
Examples:
   * source creators;
   * local communities;
   * knowledge holders;
   * contributors;
   * reviewers;
   * maintainers;
   * affected ecosystems;
   * future users;
   * implementation participants.
7. Evidence Quality
What supports this option?
Examples:
   * theory only;
   * source-linked reference;
   * implementation case;
   * repeated use;
   * field-informed learning;
   * reviewed pattern;
   * mixed evidence;
   * contested evidence;
   * high-risk or weak evidence.
________________


Option Library Governance Rules
Rule 1: Every option must have the primary ontology type “Option”
Tools, resources, source systems, concepts, deployments, and cases should be linked inside option entries, not treated as standalone options.
Rule 2: Options must be reusable
A one-off deployment is not an option. A specific case may be included as an example, but the option should describe a pathway that others could adapt.
Rule 3: Options must name conditions
An option should make clear when it is useful and when it is not useful.
Rule 4: Options must name risks and failure modes
An option is incomplete if it only describes benefits.
Rule 5: Options must distinguish tools from design choices
Karma GAP is a tool. Milestone-Based Funding or Public Goods Reporting are options.
EAS is a protocol. Impact Attestation or Claim-Evidence Record are options.
Hylo is a tool. Local Discovery Map or Needs / Offers Board are options.
Rule 6: Options must distinguish signals from conclusions
An option may identify useful signals, but signals do not automatically prove impact, success, or suitability.
Rule 7: High-risk options require stronger review before deployment
High-risk options include those involving:
   * funds or resource allocation;
   * ecological, carbon, biodiversity, or restoration claims;
   * people or community profiles;
   * local maps or exact locations;
   * AI-assisted recommendations;
   * governance / coordination recommendations;
   * legal, tax, or institutional implications;
   * identity, reputation, or contribution histories;
   * public claims about impact.
Rule 8: Options should evolve through implementation memory
When an option is used in practice, implementation learning should update the option’s risks, conditions, examples, evidence quality, or maturity status.
________________

Concept Preservation Note
This updated Option Library does not attempt to preserve every old phrase or every old table row.
It does preserve the conceptual range of the previous Option Library by carrying forward the main option families:
   * knowledge commons;
   * resource graph and ecosystem mapping;
   * governance and coordination;
   * funding and allocation;
   * discovery, affinity, and participation;
   * impact, evidence, and MRV;
   * AI and sensemaking;
   * infrastructure and publishing;
   * legal, institutional, and stewardship choices;
   * culture, care, and social practice.
Specific tools, projects, source systems, and references should be routed to the Resource Graph, Source System Registry, Concept & Idea Ecology, or Implementation Memory rather than treated as options.
The intent is not deletion. The intent is classification.
________________

When replacing the old Option Library, use this rule:
Previous entry type
	New destination
	Reusable design choice
	Keep in Option Library

	Resource Graph / Tool Registry
	Source system
	Source System Registry
	Concept or framework
	Encyclopedia / Concept & Idea Ecology

	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Evidence from practice

	Unresolved issue
	Open Questions / Research Backlog

	Deprecated or obsolete item
	Archive / Deprecated References
	Examples:
Item
	Classification
	Destination
	Notes
	Karma GAP
	Tool
	Resource Graph / Tool Registry
	Supports Milestone-Based Funding and Public Goods Reporting.
	EAS
	Tool / protocol

	Framework / source reference
	Encyclopedia / Concept & Idea Ecology / Deployment
	Informs Structural Integrity Review; do not claim conformance by default.
	Gitcoin / Allo / Octant / Giveth / Open Collective

	Support funding and allocation options.
	SuperBenefit Knowledge Garden / Bloom Wiki / P2P Foundation

	Green Goods

Open Questions
   * Which options are most useful for knowledge gardeners right now?
   * Which options are most useful for local regenerative communities?
   * Which options require structural review before being recommended?
   * Which named entries are really tools, not options?
   * Which tools support multiple options?
   * Which options are too heavy for early-stage communities?
   * Which options create privacy, consent, or location risks?
   * Which options risk over-financializing regenerative work?
   * Which options are already well documented by other toolkits?
   * Which options should reference Crypto Altruists, OpenCivics, Bloom, SuperBenefit, P2P Foundation, Metagov, ReFi DAO, GreenPill, CSIS, or other source systems?
   * Which options should become templates?
   * Which options should remain conceptual for now?
   * What option entries need field-informed examples before they are treated as mature?
________________


Working Summary
The Option Library helps communities move from vague intention to visible design space.
It preserves options as reusable design choices, while routing tools, resources, source systems, concepts, deployments, cases, and signals to their proper homes.
A strong Option Library makes it easier to compare approaches, see dependencies, avoid failure modes, preserve source lineage, identify structural risks, and move toward deployment with care.
The next step is not to add more options. The next step is to develop the highest-priority options into full entries, route tool/resource entries into the Resource Graph, and connect field-tested deployments back into Implementation Memory.


🧰 Deployment & Structural Integrity
Purpose
Deployment is where knowledge, resources, concepts, and options become operational.
The Deployment & Structural Integrity section helps communities make structure explicit before moving from ideas into action.
It is especially important when the Toolkit informs:
   * local nodes
   * funding rounds
   * knowledge commons assemblies
   * public resource maps
   * community directories
   * governance / coordination systems
   * ecological impact claims
   * AI-assisted discovery
   * public goods programs
   * local regenerative projects
   * research sprints
   * ecosystem mapping initiatives
   * implementation cases
Deployment asks:
What must be visible, agreed, reviewed, or protected before this becomes active in the world?
Core distinction
An option is not a deployment.
Object
	Meaning
	Option
	A reusable design choice.
	Tool
	A specific platform, protocol, or component.
	Deployment
	A context-specific assembly of options, tools, roles, obligations, boundaries, and review pathways.
	Implementation
	What actually happens when the deployment is used in practice.
	Example:
   * Option: milestone-based funding
   * Tool: Karma GAP
   * Deployment: a local regenerative grants process using milestone-based funding, a review committee, a shared treasury, public reporting, and escalation paths
   * Implementation: the actual funding round, including proposals, decisions, updates, conflicts, delays, lessons, and adaptations
Deployment is the bridge between design space and reality.
What deployment should make explicit
A deployment should clarify:
   * purpose
   * scope
   * participants
   * roles
   * decision paths
   * coordination structure
   * source of truth
   * information requirements
   * consent conditions
   * power and control points
   * stewardship obligations
   * review pathways
   * adverse signal channels
   * escalation paths
   * implementation boundaries
   * maintenance responsibilities
   * evidence expectations
   * privacy boundaries
   * source lineage
   * unresolved questions
   * structural risks
   * regenerative obligations
The goal is not bureaucracy.
The goal is legibility.
Deployment entry template
Field
	Description
	Deployment name
	Name of the project, process, local node, round, map, or system.
	Purpose
	Why this exists.
	Context
	Local, community, ecosystem, technical, or institutional context.
	Scope
	What is included and excluded.
	Participants
	Who is involved.
	Cost-bearing parties
	Who may be affected or carry risk.
	Selected options
	Reusable patterns being used.
	Tools / components
	Specific platforms, protocols, or resources.
	Roles
	Stewards, reviewers, contributors, signers, facilitators, maintainers, participants.
	Decision paths
	How decisions are made and recorded.
	Source of truth
	Where reliable information lives.
	Information requirements
	What people need to know to participate meaningfully.
	Information asymmetries
	Who knows what others do not.
	Consent requirements
	What permissions or agreements are needed.
	Power / control points
	Who controls funds, infrastructure, data, moderation, or narrative.
	Adverse signal channels
	Where concerns, harms, failures, or objections go.
	Named response
	What happens when a concern is raised.
	Review cadence
	When and how the deployment is reviewed.
	Maintenance responsibilities
	Who keeps it working.
	Evidence / reporting
	What should be captured.
	Maturity status
	Draft, candidate, active, reviewed, field-informed, deprecated.
	Open questions
	What is unresolved.
	Lightweight deployment template
For early use:
Question
	Response
	What are we trying to do?
	

	Who is involved?
	

	Who could be affected?
	

	What options or tools are we using?
	

	Who decides what?
	

	Where is the source of truth?
	

	What consent is needed?
	

	Who controls funds, data, infrastructure, or publication?
	

	What can go wrong?
	

	Where do concerns go?
	

	Who responds?
	

	What should we learn from this?
	

	________________


Structural Integrity
Core idea
Structural integrity asks whether the system’s basic conditions are visible enough to function without hidden harm.
A deployment may have good intentions and still be structurally weak.
Structural weakness can appear as:
   * vague roles
   * unclear decision paths
   * hidden power
   * missing consent
   * weak review
   * unclear ownership
   * unclear maintenance
   * information asymmetry
   * invisible labor
   * no adverse signal path
   * unclear funding control
   * overclaiming
   * poor source lineage
   * weak evidence
   * no way to update after failure
The Toolkit should help detect these weaknesses early.
CSIS-informed posture
The Toolkit should be informed by CSIS and adjacent structural integrity work.
The current posture is:
CSIS-informed, not CSIS-conformant by default.
That means CSIS can inform vocabulary, checks, audits, and structural design, but the Toolkit should not claim formal conformance unless specific criteria, review process, and results are documented.
The useful distinction:
CSIS helps detect structural exposure.
The Toolkit defines how to respond.
Detection is not enough. If something is found to be structurally weak, the deployment needs a named response.
Structural floors and generative capacities
Deployment should hold two kinds of requirements.
Structural floors
Structural floors are minimum conditions that reduce confusion, exploitation, hidden power, and coordination failure.
Examples:
   * clear purpose
   * clear roles
   * source of truth
   * visible decision paths
   * consent boundaries
   * power/control visibility
   * source lineage
   * review pathways
   * adverse signal channels
   * maintenance responsibilities
   * fixed / configurable / experimental boundaries
Generative capacities
Generative capacities allow the system to learn, adapt, repair, and remain alive.
Examples:
   * sensemaking
   * trust-building
   * conflict transformation
   * contributor care
   * local adaptation
   * pattern harvesting
   * learning from implementation
   * memory
   * regenerative obligation
   * unresolved tension tracking
A deployment with only structural floors may become rigid.
A deployment with only generative capacity may become vague and dependent on informal labor.
Good deployment needs both.
________________


Deployment Checklist
1. Purpose and scope
Clarify what is being deployed.
Questions:
   * What is the purpose?
   * What problem, opportunity, or need does this address?
   * What is included?
   * What is excluded?
   * What assumptions are being made?
   * What would make this successful?
   * What would make this harmful or unnecessary?
   * What should not be overclaimed?
Output:
   * purpose statement
   * scope boundary
   * explicit exclusions
   * success / failure criteria
   * open assumptions
2. Context
Clarify the setting.
Questions:
   * Is this local, digital, bioregional, organizational, public, private, or hybrid?
   * What community or ecosystem is involved?
   * What local context matters?
   * What cultural context matters?
   * What legal or institutional context matters?
   * What ecological context matters?
   * What prior work does this build on?
   * What relationships already exist?
   * What tensions or histories should be known?
Output:
   * context note
   * relevant history
   * local / cultural / ecological considerations
   * related source systems or prior work
3. Participants and cost-bearing parties
Clarify who is involved and who may be affected.
Questions:
   * Who is participating?
   * Who is contributing?
   * Who is deciding?
   * Who is reviewing?
   * Who is maintaining?
   * Who is represented?
   * Who may be affected?
   * Who may bear costs without direct control?
   * Who should be consulted?
   * Who should be able to contest representation?
Output:
   * participant map
   * role list
   * cost-bearing party note
   * consultation requirements
4. Roles and obligations
Clarify who does what and where obligations run.
Questions:
   * What roles are required?
   * What does each role control?
   * What is each role responsible for?
   * Who does each role hold obligation toward?
   * How are roles assigned?
   * How are roles reviewed?
   * How can someone leave a role?
   * What happens if a role becomes inactive?
   * What roles are overloaded?
Output:
   * role map
   * obligation direction notes
   * role handoff process
   * capacity risks
5. Decision paths
Clarify how decisions happen.
Questions:
   * What decisions need to be made?
   * Who can make each decision?
   * What requires consent, vote, review, or delegation?
   * What decisions are reversible?
   * What decisions are high-impact?
   * Where are decisions recorded?
   * How can decisions be challenged?
   * What happens in emergencies?
Output:
   * decision map
   * decision log
   * escalation path
   * emergency process
6. Information requirements and asymmetries
Clarify what information is needed and who has it.
Questions:
   * What information do participants need to participate meaningfully?
   * Who has context others lack?
   * Who understands the technical system?
   * Who understands the local context?
   * Who controls documentation?
   * What information is hidden, implicit, or hard to interpret?
   * What information asymmetries could create unfairness or harm?
   * What must be explained before participation?
Output:
   * minimum context note
   * information asymmetry map
   * onboarding requirements
   * documentation needs
7. Consent and representation
Clarify permissions and visibility.
Questions:
   * Who or what is being represented?
   * Has consent been given?
   * Consent for what specific use?
   * Is consent ongoing or one-time?
   * Can consent be withdrawn?
   * Are people, communities, places, or local knowledge being mapped?
   * What should remain private?
   * What should be anonymized or aggregated?
   * Is AI being used on personal, community, or location data?
Output:
   * consent checklist
   * visibility settings
   * withdrawal process
   * representation notes
8. Power and control
Clarify where control lives.
Questions:
   * Who controls funds?
   * Who controls infrastructure?
   * Who controls admin access?
   * Who controls data?
   * Who controls publishing?
   * Who controls moderation?
   * Who controls final interpretation?
   * Who controls public narrative?
   * Who can pause, revise, or shut down the deployment?
   * What power is informal but real?
Output:
   * power/control map
   * admin/access list
   * treasury control note
   * infrastructure dependency note
   * informal power risks
9. Source lineage and attribution
Clarify where knowledge came from.
Questions:
   * What sources are being used?
   * Who created them?
   * Are we quoting, summarizing, adapting, or synthesizing?
   * What source systems should be credited?
   * What communities should be acknowledged?
   * Is any knowledge being extracted from a local or community context?
   * What return path exists?
   * What correction path exists?
Output:
   * source lineage notes
   * attribution list
   * use type labels
   * correction path
   * return path where appropriate
10. Evidence and claims
Clarify what is being claimed.
Questions:
   * What claims are being made?
   * Are these claims conceptual, social, ecological, financial, operational, or technical?
   * What evidence supports them?
   * What evidence is missing?
   * Who reviewed the evidence?
   * What uncertainty remains?
   * What should not be claimed publicly?
   * Are metrics being overused or misrepresented?
Output:
   * claim-evidence table
   * evidence quality note
   * review status
   * uncertainty note
11. Adverse signals and named response
Clarify what happens when something goes wrong.
Questions:
   * What adverse signals should be watched?
   * Where can concerns be raised?
   * Who receives them?
   * Who is obligated to respond?
   * What response options exist?
   * What happens if the issue is ignored?
   * What gets documented?
   * What remains private?
   * What requires escalation?
   * What requires pause or redesign?
Output:
   * adverse signal channel
   * response steward
   * response options
   * escalation path
   * unresolved issue log
12. Review and update cadence
Clarify how the deployment learns.
Questions:
   * When is the deployment reviewed?
   * Who reviews it?
   * What evidence is reviewed?
   * How are signals interpreted?
   * How are updates made?
   * What becomes implementation memory?
   * What gets routed back into the Toolkit?
   * What should be deprecated, revised, or repeated?
Output:
   * review cadence
   * retrospective template
   * update process
   * implementation memory routing
________________


Structural Exposure Card
A Structural Exposure Card is a short summary of unresolved risk.
It should be used when something proceeds even though a structural gap, uncertainty, or risk remains.
Template
Field
	Response
	Deployment / object
	What is being assessed?
	Exposure identified
	What is structurally weak, unclear, or unresolved?
	Affected parties
	Who may be affected or bear costs?
	Severity
	Low, medium, high, unknown.
	Why proceeding may still make sense
	Context or rationale.
	Required disclosure
	What should be visible to participants or users?
	Named steward
	Who is responsible for monitoring or response?
	Review date
	When this must be revisited.
	Possible response
	Revise, pause, narrow scope, add consent, add review, archive, continue with disclosure.
	Example exposures
   * resource map includes communities without confirmed consent
   * AI-assisted recommendations are active before bias review
   * funding round has unclear appeal path
   * ecological impact claims lack sufficient evidence
   * public directory has no correction path
   * deployment depends on one overloaded maintainer
   * source system is heavily used without clear attribution
   * local project is represented by outsiders
   * governance process names participation but decision power remains elsewhere
________________


High-Risk Deployment Triggers
Some deployments require stronger review.
High-risk conditions
Use stronger review when the deployment involves:
   * funds or resource allocation
   * public claims about impact
   * ecological, carbon, biodiversity, or restoration claims
   * personal profiles
   * community profiles
   * location data
   * vulnerable communities
   * AI-assisted matching or recommendations
   * governance / coordination decisions
   * public representation of local groups
   * legal, tax, or institutional claims
   * sensitive cultural or local knowledge
   * identity, reputation, or contribution history
   * public rankings or scoring
   * conflict mediation
   * external funder reporting
Required review types
Depending on context, require:
   * source review
   * community review
   * privacy review
   * legal / tax review
   * ecological review
   * structural integrity review
   * AI review
   * implementation review
   * place-based review
   * conflict / facilitation review
________________


Regenerative Obligation in Deployment
Deployment should ask not only “Does this work?” but also “Toward whom do we hold obligation?”
Possible obligation directions
A deployment may hold obligation toward:
   * contributors
   * reviewers
   * local communities
   * source communities
   * people represented in maps
   * funders
   * participants
   * maintainers
   * future users
   * affected ecosystems
   * knowledge holders
   * public institutions
   * cost-bearing parties
   * places and bioregions
Regenerative obligation questions
   * Who made this possible?
   * Who should be credited?
   * Who should be asked before publication?
   * Who may bear costs?
   * Who could be misrepresented?
   * Who should have a correction path?
   * What local context must be preserved?
   * What should not be extracted?
   * What return path is possible?
   * What would make this reciprocal rather than extractive?
________________


Place Profile Template
Use this when a deployment is local, regional, ecological, or bioregional.
Field
	Description
	Place name
	City, region, watershed, bioregion, territory, or community context.
	Ecological context
	Ecosystem, watershed, species, climate, restoration context, land use.
	Cultural / historical context
	Local history, community context, relevant tensions.
	Current stewards
	People, communities, organizations, or institutions active in the place.
	Existing projects
	Local initiatives, maps, field work, events, campaigns.
	Local knowledge holders
	People or groups whose knowledge matters.
	Sensitive knowledge
	What should not be public or should require consent.
	Local assets
	Spaces, tools, skills, institutions, community resources.
	Local needs
	Requests, gaps, vulnerabilities, opportunities.
	Consent requirements
	What representation requires local agreement.
	Risks
	Ecological, political, social, financial, or privacy risks.
	Related resources
	Maps, datasets, reports, community docs, source systems.
	Review needs
	Ecological, community, legal, cultural, or structural review.
	________________


Deployment Maturity Ladder
Level
	State
	Meaning
	0
	Informal
	Intent exists, but key structures are implicit.
	1
	Minimally legible
	Purpose, participants, source of truth, and basic roles are visible.
	2
	Operationally structured
	Decision paths, consent, review, power/control, and maintenance are defined.
	3
	Structurally reviewed
	Reviewed against structural integrity criteria or equivalent.
	4
	Field-tested
	Used in practice and generated implementation signals.
	5
	Pattern-generating
	Produced reusable lessons, templates, failure notes, or patterns for the commons.
	This maturity ladder is not a ranking of virtue.
It helps people know how much trust to place in a deployment.
________________
