7. Near-Term Recommendation
For the next working version, the Toolkit should use a simple but portable stack while testing advanced infrastructure in bounded experiments.
Recommended v0.1 stack
Function
	Recommended substrate
	Working drafts
	Google Docs or Fileverse
	Resource Graph
	Google Sheets / CSV
	Source System Registry
	Spreadsheet tab or Markdown table
	Content source
	GitHub + Markdown
	Public site
	Astro / Starlight
	Metadata
	Markdown frontmatter
	Source lineage
	sources_used field
	Review states
	Frontmatter + registry fields
	Explorer views
	Current Explorer / Tag Explorer
	Relationship mapping
	Start in table; later test Kumu / Graph Commons
	Sensemaking
	Manual synthesis first; test Harmonica for group deliberation
	Long-document retrieval
	Test PageIndex on the master doc and source-system exports
	GraphRAG / context graph
	Keep TrustGraph.ai as a to-review candidate
	Knowledge graph substrate
	Keep Geo / GRC-20 and KOI as candidates after schema v0.1
	Trust / ranking
	Keep OpenRank and TrustGraph.network as high-risk candidates
	Archive
	GitHub exports + periodic ZIP / CSV / Markdown backup
	This stack is enough to support a Minimum Viable Commons.
It does not prevent future migration into Geo, KOI, JSON-LD, RDF, Wikibase, PageIndex, TrustGraph, OpenRank, or more advanced graph infrastructure.
________________


8. Bounded Infrastructure Experiments
Advanced infrastructure should be tested through bounded experiments, not adopted as the default system.
Experiment 1: PageIndex for Long-Document Retrieval
Purpose
Test whether PageIndex helps contributors query the master document, CSIS references, implementation reports, or source-system exports without losing section context.
Success criteria
   * Retrieves relevant sections more accurately than ordinary search or vector-only RAG.
   * Preserves section hierarchy.
   * Produces reviewable retrieval traces.
   * Helps distinguish concepts, tools, options, deployments, evidence, and signals.
   * Does not create false authority.
Review needs
AI review, source lineage review, human reviewer workflow.
________________


Experiment 2: Geo / GRC-20 for Governed Knowledge Graphs
Purpose
Test whether Toolkit entries can be represented as composable graph objects with relationships, edits, and community curation.
Success criteria
   * Represents Toolkit object types clearly.
   * Preserves source lineage.
   * Supports reusable relationship types.
   * Enables export or interoperability.
   * Does not require premature full ontology formalization.
   * Supports meaningful curation rather than just graph complexity.
Review needs
Ontology review, governance review, export review, contributor accessibility review.
________________


Experiment 3: OpenRank for Contextual Trust-Weighted Discovery
Purpose
Test whether ranking or reputation computation can help discover resources, contributors, reviewers, or implementation cases without becoming hidden governance.
Success criteria
   * Rankings are scoped to a clear purpose.
   * Trust seed and graph inputs are explicit.
   * Outputs are explainable.
   * Rankings are treated as signals, not conclusions.
   * People and communities are not ranked without consent.
   * Results can be contested or corrected.
Review needs
Privacy review, consent review, governance review, structural integrity review.
________________


Experiment 4: TrustGraph.network for Attestation-Based Trust Relationships
Purpose
Explore whether attested trust relationships or endorsement graphs can help represent contextual trust, review relationships, or source-system credibility.
Success criteria
   * Trust relationships are explicit and contextual.
   * Attestations preserve source, timestamp, and issuer.
   * Trust does not collapse into universal reputation.
   * Sensitive social relationships are not exposed.
   * Trust claims can be corrected, expired, or contested.
Review needs
Consent review, identity review, trust/reputation review, governance review.
________________


Experiment 5: TrustGraph.ai for GraphRAG / Context Graph Retrieval
Purpose
Test whether graph-native AI retrieval can help search, enrich, and retrieve structured knowledge across Toolkit documents and registries.
Success criteria
   * Supports structured knowledge retrieval.
   * Preserves context and relationships.
   * Helps reduce hallucination compared with unstructured retrieval.
   * Provides useful APIs or export paths.
   * Does not replace human review.
Review needs
AI review, data model review, source lineage review, export review.
________________


9. Open Questions
   * What should remain in Google Docs or Fileverse?
   * What should move into Markdown?
   * What should live in the Resource Graph table?
   * What should become graph data?
   * What should be published publicly?
   * What should remain internal or restricted?
   * Which metadata fields are essential for v0.1?
   * What export formats should be supported first?
   * How should source lineage travel across tools?
   * How should review and maturity status travel across tools?
   * How should consent boundaries travel across tools?
   * How should implementation learning update infrastructure choices?
   * What should be indexed for AI retrieval?
   * What should not be indexed for AI retrieval?
   * How should PageIndex, GraphRAG, or semantic search preserve section context?
   * When is a knowledge graph useful, and when is it graph theater?
   * When are rankings useful, and when are they reputation theater?
   * Should OpenRank ever be used for people or contributor reputation?
   * Should trust graphs be opt-in only?
   * How should trust or ranking signals be contested?
   * Could Geo / GRC-20 support public knowledge curation without overcomplicating the Toolkit?
   * Could TrustGraph.network or OpenRank support review signals without becoming hidden governance?
   * Which infrastructure experiments should happen only after the Minimum Ontology and Resource Graph v0.1 are stable?
________________


Working Summary
Infrastructure should help the Toolkit become more legible, portable, reviewable, and useful.
It should not define the commons too early.
The current posture is:
Simple first.
Structured next.
Graph later.
AI carefully.
Ranking cautiously.
Consent always.
The near-term priority is not to choose a final platform.
The near-term priority is to preserve optionality while building the minimum viable structures that any future substrate will need:
   * stable object types;
   * source lineage;
   * review and maturity status;
   * consent and visibility fields;
   * exportable resource entries;
   * relationship grammar;
   * implementation memory;
   * correction paths;
   * clear stewardship roles.
Once those are stable, the Toolkit can test more advanced infrastructure such as Geo / GRC-20, KOI, PageIndex, TrustGraph, OpenRank, Wikibase, RDF, JSON-LD, or GraphRAG without losing the core commons logic.
The infrastructure should serve the knowledge commons.
The knowledge commons should not become subordinate to the infrastructure.
🐝 Knowledge Commoning Swarm
Note on relationship to the rest of the master doc: This section reflects the Regen Toolkit’s current relationship to the Knowledge Commoning Swarm. It frames the Toolkit as a contribution artifact: something that may be reused, remixed, simplified, absorbed into shared Swarm outputs, or partially composted. Some terminology in this section, such as Map, Component Index, Assembly Wizard, Store, Contribute, Find, Govern, Connect, Evolve, Scout, Garden, Compose, and Harvest, comes from the Swarm context and may not yet be fully harmonized with the rest of the master doc. Future passes may align related sections such as Resource Graph, Infrastructure & Interoperability, Contributor Workflow, Option Library, Implementation Memory, Provenance & Attribution, and Review & Maturity. For now, this section should be read as a bridge between the Regen Toolkit and the broader knowledge commoning effort, not as a replacement for the Toolkit’s existing architecture.
Purpose
The Knowledge Commoning Swarm is a live collaboration context for assembling an open Knowledge Commons Toolkit.
The Swarm is not about building one platform, picking one architecture, or asking everyone to abandon what they have already built.
It is about pooling what exists, making it legible, mapping how components connect, and packaging the result so communities can compose their own knowledge commons from open, modular parts.
For the Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit, the Swarm should be treated as a learning and contribution context, not as a validation test.
The Regen Toolkit may turn out to be highly useful.
It may also be partially absorbed, renamed, simplified, broken apart, or scrapped.
That is acceptable.
The goal is not to defend the master doc as a final product.
The goal is to contribute useful distinctions, resources, patterns, warnings, and regenerative context to the shared knowledge commons effort.
________________


Current posture
The Regen Toolkit should enter the Swarm as a contribution artifact, not as an architecture seeking adoption.
Its value may lie in specific pieces:
   * resource maps
   * source-system thinking
   * structural integrity questions
   * implementation memory
   * regenerative and place-based context
   * public goods funding knowledge
   * local coordination patterns
   * ecological MRV caution
   * review and maturity language
   * anti-extraction principles
   * distinctions between tools, options, patterns, protocols, and deployments
The Toolkit does not need to survive intact to be useful.
If parts of it help the Swarm clarify the shared Knowledge Commons Toolkit, then the work has already contributed.
________________


1. What the Swarm is building
The Swarm is organized around four shared outputs.
These outputs are useful reference points for deciding which parts of the Regen Toolkit might be worth contributing, translating, or decomposing.
Swarm output
	Description
	Regen Toolkit connection
	Map
	A living canvas of components organized by function, with connections between them
	Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
	Assembly Wizard
	A guided tool for composing a knowledge commons in a specific context
	Option Library; Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Instructional Guide
	The conceptual entry point explaining what a knowledge commons is and how the toolkit works
	Start Here; Encyclopedia; Contributor Guide
	Component Index
	A structured, searchable, relational archive of components with metadata
	Resource Graph; Source System Registry; Infrastructure & Interoperability
	The Swarm’s shared output is not just a document.
It is a package of maps, indexes, guides, and assembly paths that communities can reuse.
________________


2. The six functional affordances
The Swarm frames knowledge commons components through six functional questions.
This is useful because it describes components by what they help communities do, rather than by brand, ideology, or platform.
Function
	Guiding question
	Related Toolkit areas
	Store
	Where does the knowledge live?
	Infrastructure, Resource Graph, publishing stack, archival systems
	Contribute
	How does knowledge get in?
	Contributor pathways, source intake, annotations, review requests
	Find
	How do people discover what is there?
	Explorer, Tag Explorer, Resource Graph views, learning paths
	Govern
	Who decides what and how?
	Review & Maturity, stewardship, permissions, attribution, provenance
	Connect
	How does it talk to other systems?
	Interoperability, exports, federation, APIs, source-system relationships
	Evolve
	How does it learn, version, and improve?
	Implementation Memory, changelogs, decay/renewal, pattern harvesting
	The Regen Toolkit should use these functions as a translation layer.
Instead of asking others to adopt Regen Toolkit terminology first, we can ask:
   * What does this help people store?
   * What does this help people contribute?
   * What does this help people find?
   * What does this help people govern?
   * What does this help people connect?
   * What does this help people evolve?
This makes the Toolkit more legible to the broader Swarm.
________________


3. What the Regen Toolkit can contribute
The Regen Toolkit should not be presented as one large object.
It should be decomposed into smaller contributions that can plug into the Swarm.
3.1 Domain-specific atlas
The Toolkit can contribute a mapped view of resources from:
   * regenerative web3
   * ReFi
   * local coordination
   * public goods funding
   * bioregionalism
   * localism
   * ecological MRV
   * community science
   * regenerative design
   * web3 impact tools
   * civic infrastructure
   * knowledge gardening
   * implementation learning
This can support the Swarm’s Map and Component Index.
________________


3.2 Source-system and provenance discipline
The Toolkit can contribute the idea that source systems are not just links.
A source system is a living knowledge environment, such as:
   * a wiki
   * a repo
   * a map
   * a forum
   * a directory
   * a resource library
   * a knowledge garden
   * a community archive
   * a research database
Source systems should be credited as stewarded contexts, not mined as isolated resources.
This can support the Swarm’s Govern, Connect, and Evolve functions.
________________


3.3 Structural integrity lens
The Toolkit can contribute questions that help prevent knowledge commons from becoming extractive, unsafe, or falsely authoritative.
Useful questions include:
   * Who is represented?
   * Who is absent?
   * Who can correct this?
   * Who maintains it?
   * Who benefits from visibility?
   * Who may be exposed by visibility?
   * What claims need review?
   * What source lineage is missing?
   * What risks travel with this component?
   * What happens if this gets reused in another context?
This can support the Swarm’s Govern and Assembly Wizard work.
________________


3.4 Implementation Memory
The Toolkit can contribute Implementation Memory as a pattern.
Knowledge commons often preserve resources, frameworks, and tools, but lose the lessons from actual use.
Implementation Memory asks:
   * What was attempted?
   * Who used it?
   * In what context?
   * What worked?
   * What failed?
   * What surprised people?
   * What risks appeared?
   * What should be updated?
   * What should not be generalized?
   * What pattern, if any, emerged?
This can support the Swarm’s Evolve function.
________________


3.5 Option vs tool vs deployment distinction
The Toolkit can contribute a useful distinction:
Term
	Meaning
	Tool
	A specific software, platform, protocol, method, or resource
	Option
	A reusable design choice that may use one or more tools
	Pattern
	A recurring configuration that solves a common problem
	Protocol
	A shared agreement for enacting a pattern
	Deployment
	A situated implementation in a real context
	Case
	A documented example of what happened in practice
	This distinction can help the Swarm avoid confusing component lists with actual assembly guidance.
________________


3.6 Anti-patterns and cautions
The Toolkit can contribute warnings that may be useful across knowledge commons contexts.
Important anti-patterns include:
   * aggregation without attribution
   * visibility without consent
   * AI synthesis without source lineage
   * mapping without stewardship
   * platform adoption without export paths
   * openness without care
   * metrics without meaning
   * funding claims without evidence
   * tool-first thinking
   * local context erased by global patterns
   * polished content mistaken for reviewed knowledge
These can support the Swarm’s Govern, Connect, and Evolve functions.
________________


4. What the Regen Toolkit should learn from the Swarm
The Swarm can help the Toolkit become less heavy, more modular, and more useful.
4.1 Learn what others are gravitating toward
The Toolkit should pay attention to what other participants repeatedly bring up.
Useful signals include:
   * which functions attract the most energy
   * which components appear repeatedly
   * which terms people naturally use
   * which tools people are already building with
   * which gaps multiple people identify
   * which governance questions keep returning
   * which infrastructure choices feel practical
   * which outputs people actually want to use
   * which parts of the Toolkit feel too heavy
   * which Toolkit distinctions help others think more clearly
The goal is not to prove the master doc is right.
The goal is to notice where collective understanding is forming.
________________


4.2 Translate before proposing
Instead of leading with internal Toolkit categories, translate them into Swarm language.
Regen Toolkit language
	Swarm translation
	Resource Graph
	Component Index / Map
	Source System Registry
	Govern / Connect / Evolve support
	Option Library
	Assembly Wizard input
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Assembly guardrails
	Implementation Memory
	Evolve pattern
	Review & Maturity
	Govern pattern
	Provenance & Attribution
	Govern / Connect requirement
	Interface & Navigation
	Find layer
	Infrastructure & Interoperability
	Store / Connect layer
	Contributor workflow
	Scout / Garden / Connect / Compose / Harvest
	This makes the Toolkit easier for others to use, critique, and remix.
________________


4.3 Accept composting
Some parts of the Toolkit may not be useful to the Swarm.
That is not failure.
A commons grows through composting:
   * ideas get simplified
   * sections get renamed
   * patterns get extracted
   * terms get replaced
   * resources get merged
   * concepts get absorbed elsewhere
   * architecture gets reduced to a few durable moves
The master doc does not need to be preserved as a complete object.
The useful pieces should travel.
________________


5. Recommended Swarm contribution bundle
Instead of sharing the whole master doc first, the Regen Toolkit should prepare a small contribution bundle.
Bundle 1 — Regen domain atlas
A curated set of resources related to:
   * ReFi
   * public goods funding
   * local coordination
   * ecological MRV
   * regenerative design
   * web3 impact tools
   * localism and bioregionalism
   * knowledge commons infrastructure
Purpose:
Contribute domain-specific components to the Swarm Map and Component Index.
________________


Bundle 2 — Implementation Memory pattern
A short pattern entry:
Field
	Draft
	Name
	Implementation Memory
	Function
	Evolve
	Problem
	Commons often preserve resources and tools but lose the lessons from actual use
	Use when
	A community runs pilots, events, grants, maps, deployments, or experiments
	Helps with
	Retrospectives, pattern harvesting, failure learning, updating the commons
	Watch out for
	Success theater, exposing sensitive conflict, overgeneralizing from one case
	Related functions
	Govern, Contribute, Find
	Related outputs
	Instructional Guide, Component Index, Assembly Wizard
	________________


Bundle 3 — Extractive Aggregation anti-pattern
A short anti-pattern entry:
Field
	Draft
	Name
	Extractive Aggregation
	Function
	Govern / Connect
	Problem
	A toolkit collects resources from other communities without preserving attribution, context, consent, or return paths
	Signal
	Resources become discoverable, but source communities do not receive credit, corrections, traffic, value, or relationship
	Repair
	Source System Registry, use-type labels, attribution notes, return paths, review status
	Watch out for
	Treating open resources as ownerless resources
	________________


Bundle 4 — Structural Exposure Card
A lightweight review pattern:
Field
	Draft
	Name
	Structural Exposure Card
	Function
	Govern
	Purpose
	Make visible the risks, obligations, and unresolved issues attached to a component or deployment
	Use when
	A component involves people, place, funding, governance, ecological claims, identity, AI, or sensitive knowledge
	Fields
	Exposure, affected parties, severity, mitigation, steward, review date, disclosure needed, proceed / pause recommendation
	Related outputs
	Assembly Wizard, Component Index, Review & Maturity
	________________


Bundle 5 — Functional mapping of Regen Toolkit pieces
A simple translation into Swarm functions:
Swarm function
	Regen Toolkit contribution
	Store
	Markdown, Astro/Starlight, GitHub, Fileverse, Resource Graph table
	Contribute
	Contributor guide, source intake, annotation, review requests
	Find
	Explorer, Tag Explorer, learning paths, Resource Graph views
	Govern
	Review & Maturity, Source System Registry, structural integrity checks
	Connect
	Source lineage, sources_used, interoperability fields, source relationships
	Evolve
	Implementation Memory, pattern harvesting, signal logs, changelog, deprecation status
	________________


6. Listening protocol for the Swarm
The Regen Toolkit should use the Swarm to learn, not only to present.
During the Swarm, track:
What people are building
   * tools
   * maps
   * schemas
   * guides
   * workflows
   * wizards
   * indexes
   * protocols
   * pattern libraries
   * review processes
   * contributor systems
What people already have
   * existing repositories
   * existing maps
   * existing knowledge gardens
   * existing open-source tools
   * existing governance processes
   * existing community archives
   * existing resource indexes
   * existing templates
   * existing data models
What people are gravitating toward
   * functions
   * repeated language
   * shared pain points
   * common gaps
   * practical substrates
   * open standards
   * social processes
   * mapping formats
   * governance models
   * interface patterns
What people are avoiding
   * overly heavy architecture
   * premature platform choices
   * insider language
   * complex schemas
   * extractive contribution models
   * weak maintenance plans
   * unclear ownership
   * AI-generated authority
   * non-exportable systems
What needs translation
   * terms that overlap but differ
   * similar patterns with different names
   * tools used for different functions
   * governance assumptions
   * definitions of “component”
   * assumptions about openness
   * assumptions about review
   * assumptions about stewardship
________________


7. Questions to bring into the Swarm
The Regen Toolkit can bring questions rather than conclusions.
Useful questions:
   * What makes a component useful across contexts?
   * What metadata is essential for a Component Index?
   * How do we distinguish tool, pattern, protocol, playbook, and deployment?
   * How should source systems be credited?
   * How do we avoid extractive aggregation?
   * What should the Assembly Wizard warn people about?
   * How do we represent review and maturity without creating false authority?
   * How should a commons remember implementation learning?
   * How do we track decay, staleness, and renewal?
   * What should be public, private, or restricted?
   * What should happen when a component has risks?
   * How do communities fork or adapt a knowledge commons without losing lineage?
   * What are people already using for Store, Contribute, Find, Govern, Connect, and Evolve?
   * What parts of the Regen Toolkit are useful to others?
   * What parts should be composted?
________________


8. How to decide what survives
After the Swarm, do not ask only:
Did people like the Regen Toolkit?
Ask:
   * Which Toolkit concepts got reused?
   * Which distinctions helped others?
   * Which terms confused people?
   * Which patterns traveled well?
   * Which resources filled gaps?
   * Which sections felt too heavy?
   * Which pieces became components in the Swarm index?
   * Which ideas should be renamed using Swarm language?
   * Which parts should become standalone templates?
   * Which parts should be removed from the master doc?
   * Which parts should be contributed upstream to the shared Knowledge Commons Toolkit?
The Toolkit’s success should be measured by contribution, not adoption.
________________


9. Relationship to the master doc
The master doc should remain a working base, not a fixed artifact.
The Swarm may reveal that the Toolkit should become:
   * a domain atlas
   * a resource graph
   * a pattern library
   * a contribution bundle
   * a set of review cards
   * an implementation memory template
   * an Assembly Wizard path
   * a source-system registry
   * a public site
   * a smaller guide
   * or simply compost for the shared Knowledge Commons Toolkit
Any of these outcomes can be valuable.
The master doc should stay flexible enough to absorb what emerges.
________________


10. Working stance
The Regen Toolkit should show up to the Swarm with humility and specificity.
A useful stance:
We have been developing a Regen Knowledge Commons Toolkit for regenerative web3, local coordination, public goods, and ecological stewardship. It may or may not survive as a standalone toolkit. We want to contribute what is useful, learn what others are building, and help identify the components, patterns, and functions the wider knowledge commons field is gravitating toward.
This keeps the work open.
It also makes the contribution stronger.
________________


11. Open Questions
   * Who else is involved in the Swarm?
   * What have they already built?
   * What are they currently building?
   * Which functions are attracting the most energy?
   * Which components appear repeatedly?
   * Which open standards or tools are becoming shared references?
   * What does the Swarm mean by “component” in practice?
   * What counts as enough metadata for a useful Component Index?
   * How should source systems be credited?
   * How should components carry limitations and risks?
   * How should the Assembly Wizard handle context-specific warnings?
   * What parts of the Regen Toolkit should be contributed as components?
   * What parts should become patterns?
   * What parts should become anti-patterns?
   * What parts should be left behind?
   * How can the Toolkit support collective understanding without becoming a competing architecture?
   * What would make the Toolkit useful even if it is never adopted as a standalone project?
________________


Working Summary
The Knowledge Commoning Swarm is a chance to contribute to a shared understanding of how communities can organize, govern, connect, and evolve knowledge commons.
The Regen Toolkit should not enter as a finished architecture.
It should enter as a living contribution.
Its value may be in the pieces that travel:
   * regenerative resource maps
   * source-system thinking
   * structural integrity questions
   * implementation memory
   * review and maturity language
   * anti-extraction patterns
   * public goods and local coordination knowledge
   * ecological claim caution
   * place-based context
The Toolkit may be gold.
It may be compost.
Either outcome can serve the commons if the work helps others see more clearly, connect what already exists, and build knowledge commons with more care.


❓ Open Questions & Research Backlog
Purpose
This section preserves questions, uncertainties, research leads, unresolved tensions, and areas that need deeper investigation.
The Toolkit should not force premature answers.
Some questions need research.
Some need field testing.
Some need community input.
Some need technical exploration.
Some need legal, ecological, or structural review.
Some should remain open until real implementation generates better signals.
The Open Questions & Research Backlog helps the Toolkit remain honest about what is not yet known.
How to use this section
This section can be used to:
   * capture unresolved questions
   * route questions to the right Toolkit layer
   * identify research priorities
   * preserve tensions
   * avoid premature conclusions
   * assign follow-up work
   * track questions from events or working sessions
   * convert repeated questions into Encyclopedia pages
   * convert research findings into Resource Graph entries, Option Library entries, or Deployment checks
Not every question needs to be answered immediately.
A question may remain useful simply by staying visible.
________________


1. Question Types
Conceptual questions
Questions about meaning, definitions, distinctions, and frameworks.
Examples:
   * What is the difference between a knowledge commons and a knowledge base?
   * What is the difference between a tool, option, component, deployment, and implementation?
   * What does regenerative web3 mean beyond ReFi?
   * What is structural integrity in a knowledge commons context?
   * What is a local node?
   * What counts as implementation evidence?
   * What makes a pattern transferable?
Ecosystem mapping questions
Questions about people, projects, organizations, tools, networks, events, and source systems.
Examples:
   * Which ecosystem maps already exist?
   * Which organizations are stewarding relevant knowledge?
   * Which communities should be represented?
   * Which source systems should be credited?
   * Which tools are most relevant to local coordination?
   * Which people are important bridge-builders across ecosystems?
   * Which events have generated important resources?
   * What is missing from the current Resource Graph?
Infrastructure questions
Questions about technical substrate, interoperability, schemas, publishing, AI, and graph structure.
Examples:
   * What should remain in Google Docs?
   * What should become a table?
   * What should become Markdown?
   * What should become graph data?
   * What metadata fields are essential?
   * What relationships should be modeled?
   * What infrastructure is too early?
   * How should AI-assisted workflows preserve source lineage?
Deployment questions
Questions about using the Toolkit in real contexts.
Examples:
   * What must be explicit before a local group uses a funding mechanism?
   * When does an ecosystem map require consent review?
   * What should a deployment checklist include?
   * When is a structural exposure card required?
   * How should adverse signals be received and acted on?
   * What makes a public resource map safe enough to publish?
   * What should not be deployed yet?
Regenerative design questions
Questions about reciprocity, place, source communities, ecological claims, and non-extractive knowledge stewardship.
Examples:
   * What does regenerative knowledge stewardship require?
   * How do we avoid extractive aggregation?
   * What obligations exist toward source communities?
   * What obligations exist toward places and ecosystems?
   * How should local knowledge be represented?
   * What should not be mapped publicly?
   * How do we prevent regenerative language from becoming branding?
Review and maturity questions
Questions about trust, review, expertise, evidence, and publication state.
Examples:
   * What review states are useful without becoming bureaucratic?
   * Which content requires expert review?
   * How should AI-assisted drafts be labeled?
   * How should ecological claims be reviewed?
   * How should legal or tax guidance be handled?
   * What makes content field-informed?
   * When should something be deprecated?
Implementation learning questions
Questions about how practice updates the commons.
Examples:
   * What should every implementation case capture?
   * How do we learn from failure without blame?
   * When does one implementation become evidence?
   * When does evidence become a pattern?
   * When does a pattern become a warning?
   * How do implementation signals update the Toolkit?
   * How should unresolved tensions be preserved?
________________


2. Research Backlog Template
Use this format for research questions that need deeper work.
Field
	Description
	Question
	What needs to be investigated?
	Why it matters
	Why the Toolkit needs this answered.
	Related layer
	Ontology, Resource Graph, Option Library, Deployment, etc.
	Related concepts
	Concepts connected to the question.
	Potential sources
	People, orgs, resources, papers, events, or source systems to check.
	Suggested method
	Desk research, interview, ecosystem scan, case review, tool test, field experiment, etc.
	Output
	What should this produce?
	Priority
	Low, medium, high, urgent.
	Status
	Open, in progress, partially answered, routed, archived.
	Owner / steward
	Person or group responsible, if any.
	Notes
	Caveats, tensions, or related leads.
	Lightweight question format
For fast capture:
Question
	Why it matters
	Possible route
	

	

	

	________________


3. Priority Research Questions
1. What is the minimum viable Resource Graph?
Why it matters:
The Resource Graph can become too complex too quickly. The Toolkit needs to know the minimum fields and workflows that make resource mapping useful without overburdening contributors.
Possible outputs:
   * minimum entry template
   * recommended metadata fields
   * first-pass contribution workflow
   * review and maturity states
   * spreadsheet/table structure
Related sections:
   * Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
   * Ontology
   * Standards & Interoperability
   * Provenance & Attribution
2. What is the right relationship grammar?
Why it matters:
The Resource Graph becomes much more useful when relationships are explicit. But too many relationship types will create confusion.
Possible outputs:
   * core relationship list
   * optional advanced relationship list
   * examples by domain
   * relationship-to-layer mapping
   * relationship governance notes
Related sections:
   * Ontology
   * Resource Graph
   * Concept & Idea Ecology
   * Standards & Interoperability
3. How should source systems be represented?
Why it matters:
Many important references are not individual links. They are living knowledge environments: wikis, maps, forums, repositories, directories, knowledge gardens, event archives, and community libraries.
Possible outputs:
   * source system entry template
   * source lineage fields
   * attribution guidelines
   * correction and return path practices
   * source-system maturity notes
Related sections:
   * Provenance & Attribution
   * Resource Graph
   * Regenerative Obligation
   * Review & Maturity
4. How should concepts and resources connect?
Why it matters:
The Toolkit maps both entities and ideas. The relationship between Resource Graph and Concept & Idea Ecology needs to be usable.
Possible outputs:
   * concept tagging method
   * concept entry template
   * resource-to-concept relationship types
   * example concept clusters
   * routing guide for concept pages vs resource entries
Related sections:
   * Concept & Idea Ecology
   * Resource Graph
   * Encyclopedia
   * Ontology
5. What should count as an Option Library entry?
Why it matters:
The Option Library should not become another resource list. It needs a clear threshold for when a resource, tool, concept, or practice becomes a reusable option.
Working distinction:
   * A resource is an entity or artifact.
   * A concept explains meaning.
   * A tool performs a function.
   * An option is a reusable design choice.
   * A deployment is a context-specific assembly.
   * An implementation is what happens in practice.
Possible outputs:
   * option criteria
   * option template
   * example entries
   * non-example entries
   * tool-to-option mapping
Related sections:
   * Option Library
   * Deployment
   * Resource Graph
   * Concept & Idea Ecology
6. What makes a deployment high-risk?
Why it matters:
Some deployments can affect people, communities, funds, claims, local contexts, or ecosystems. These need stronger review.
Possible outputs:
   * high-risk trigger list
   * review requirements
   * structural exposure card
   * lightweight risk score
   * examples
Related sections:
   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
   * Privacy, Consent & Identity
   * Review & Maturity
   * Regenerative Obligation
7. How should implementation evidence be evaluated?
Why it matters:
The Toolkit should learn from practice, but not all evidence is equally strong or transferable.
Possible outputs:
   * evidence quality scale
   * claim-evidence template
   * implementation case template
   * signal classification
   * pattern-harvesting criteria
Related sections:
   * Implementation Memory
   * Evolution
   * Review & Maturity
   * Ecology / MRV
8. How should AI be used safely in knowledge gardening?
Why it matters:
AI will likely be useful for synthesis, classification, search, and drafting. But it can also create false authority, source loss, hallucination, and flattening.
Possible outputs:
   * AI-use policy
   * AI-assisted content labels
   * source fidelity checklist
   * human review requirements
   * privacy restrictions
   * prompt / workflow examples
Related sections:
   * Synthesis & Sensemaking
   * Review & Maturity
   * Provenance & Attribution
   * Infrastructure & Interoperability
   * Privacy, Consent & Identity
9. What should be mapped publicly?
Why it matters:
Ecosystem maps can help people discover each other, but public mapping can create privacy and representation risks.
Possible outputs:
   * public / private visibility guidelines
   * consent fields
   * community representation checklist
   * location precision policy
   * correction path template
Related sections:
   * Resource Graph
   * Discovery & Affinity
   * Privacy, Consent & Identity
   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
10. How should regenerative obligation be operationalized?
Why it matters:
Regenerative obligation should not remain abstract. It needs practical fields, review questions, and deployment checks.
Possible outputs:
   * obligation matrix
   * regenerative obligation checklist
   * source community return-path practices
   * affected-party mapping
   * local knowledge guidance
Related sections:
   * Regenerative Obligation
   * Deployment
   * Provenance
   * Implementation Memory
________________


4. Ecosystem Mapping Backlog
Knowledge commons and open knowledge
Research questions:
   * Which knowledge commons toolkits already exist?
   * What does the Open Knowledge Foundation offer that should inform this Toolkit?
   * What can be learned from library science, archival practice, and metadata communities?
   * What can be learned from personal knowledge management and digital gardening communities?
   * How do knowledge gardens preserve versioning, review, and source lineage?
   * Which commons resources from P2P Foundation and Commons Transition should become core references?
   * Which SuperBenefit Knowledge Garden patterns should inform the Toolkit?
Possible outputs:
   * source system entries
   * concept pages
   * source lineage guide
   * knowledge garden pattern list
   * glossary terms
Civic innovation and democratic infrastructure
Research questions:
   * Which civic tech tools are most relevant to local regenerative coordination?
   * What should be learned from Decidim, Pol.is, Consul, Loomio, and vTaiwan?
   * How do public participation systems handle legitimacy, moderation, and institutional interface?
   * What civic data standards are relevant to community resource maps?
   * What does OpenCivics’ field of thinkers and resources help us understand?
   * How should digital public infrastructure relate to web3 public goods?
Possible outputs:
   * Resource Graph entries
   * Option Library entries
   * Deployment checklist additions
   * civic infrastructure concept map
Regenerative networks and local coordination
Research questions:
   * How do Bloom, GreenPill, ReFi DAO, Hylo, bioregional networks, and local chapters organize local action?
   * What local node patterns recur?
   * What are the common failure modes of local chapters?
   * How should requests/offers be represented?
   * What should be learned from mutual aid and solidarity economy networks?
   * What should be learned from community currency experiments?
Possible outputs:
   * local node pattern candidates
   * place profile template improvements
   * implementation case entries
   * local coordination option entries
ReFi, climate, and ecological coordination
Research questions:
   * Which ReFi ecosystem maps already exist?
   * Which climate web3 tools are still active and useful?
   * Which MRV systems are relevant to community-scale regenerative work?
   * How should ecological claims be reviewed?
   * What should be learned from OpenTEAM, farmOS, Restor, GBIF, iNaturalist, Plan Vivo, Verra, and Gold Standard?
   * How can ecological data be used without reducing regeneration to carbon metrics?
Possible outputs:
   * MRV concept pages
   * ecological evidence checklist
   * Resource Graph entries
   * high-risk claim review process
   * ecology / MRV option entries
Web3 public goods and allocation
Research questions:
   * Which funding mechanisms should be represented first?
   * What should be learned from Gitcoin, Allo, Octant, Giveth, Open Collective, Karma GAP, Hypercerts, EAS, and Open Source Observer?
   * Which funding mechanisms are useful for local regenerative work?
   * How should grant reporting become implementation memory?
   * What are the failure modes of web3 funding rounds?
   * How can public goods funding avoid metric theater?
Possible outputs:
   * funding option entries
   * deployment templates for rounds
   * claim-evidence templates
   * failure mode pages
   * implementation case entries
AI, collective intelligence, and sensemaking
Research questions:
   * Which AI tools are useful for knowledge gardening?
   * Which collective intelligence tools support deliberation and synthesis?
   * How should Harmonica, Bonfires, Pol.is, Kialo, SenseMaker, and similar tools be classified?
   * What are safe patterns for AI-assisted curation?
   * How should disagreement be preserved in AI-assisted synthesis?
   * How should AI outputs be reviewed before becoming knowledge?
Possible outputs:
   * AI workflow options
   * review guidelines
   * infrastructure comparison
   * sensemaking tool entries
   * AI anti-patterns
Legal, institutional, and policy interface
Research questions:
   * What nonprofit, cooperative, fiscal sponsorship, and DAO-wrapper references matter?
   * How should Crypto Altruists’ nonprofit policy resources inform deployment checks?
   * What should the Toolkit say about legal/tax topics without giving legal advice?
   * How should licensing be handled for shared knowledge?
   * What institutional forms support regenerative knowledge commons?
   * What should be learned from Open Collective, Creative Commons, Purpose Foundation, Platform Cooperativism, LexDAO, and computable law work?
Possible outputs:
   * legal / institutional concept pages
   * review trigger list
   * license guidance
   * Resource Graph entries
   * deployment caution notes

7. Open Tensions
These tensions should remain visible.
Open knowledge vs protected knowledge
The Toolkit should promote open knowledge while respecting privacy, consent, local knowledge, and sensitive community information.
Ecosystem mapping vs exposure
Mapping helps people discover each other, but can expose people, places, relationships, vulnerabilities, and informal networks.
AI assistance vs source fidelity
AI can accelerate synthesis, but can also erase source lineage, flatten disagreement, or produce polished falsehoods.
Web3 public goods vs regenerative practice
Web3 funding and coordination tools can help regenerative work, but can also over-financialize it.
Local specificity vs reusable pattern
The Toolkit wants reusable options, but regenerative work is context-sensitive.
Structure vs emergence
Too little structure hides power and overburdens informal labor. Too much structure can block participation and adaptation.
Review quality vs contributor ease
Strong review improves trust, but heavy review can block contribution.
Metrics vs meaning
Evidence is necessary, but metrics can distort social and ecological realities.
Public visibility vs community safety
Public directories, maps, and case studies can be useful, but not everything should be public.
Infrastructure sophistication vs maintainability
Advanced graph, AI, or web3 systems may be powerful, but can create maintenance burdens and exclusion.
________________


8. Candidate Research Outputs
This backlog can generate many kinds of outputs.
Short-form outputs
   * glossary entry
   * resource entry
   * source system card
   * open-question note
   * comparison table
   * tool profile
   * concept stub
   * risk note
   * failure mode
   * relationship map
Medium-form outputs
   * Encyclopedia page
   * option entry
   * deployment checklist
   * implementation case
   * ecosystem map section
   * tool comparison
   * concept lineage map
   * evidence review note
Long-form outputs
   * research memo
   * field guide
   * source system review
   * infrastructure evaluation
   * implementation retrospective
   * pattern library
   * ecosystem atlas
   * deployment playbook
   * regenerative knowledge stewardship guide
________________


9. Prioritization Method
Use a simple prioritization method.
High priority
Questions that affect:
   * current document structure
   * Resource Graph design
   * source lineage
   * deployment risk
   * consent and privacy
   * ecological or legal claims
   * Swarm participation
   * immediate contributor workflows
   * high-value missing ecosystem maps
Medium priority
Questions that affect:
   * future infrastructure
   * advanced graph design
   * AI workflows
   * deep concept lineage
   * tool comparisons
   * broader ecosystem expansion
Low priority
Questions that are interesting but not blocking current collaboration.
Priority questions
Ask:
   * Does this block the next iteration?
   * Does this affect safety or trust?
   * Does this improve contributor experience?
   * Does this prevent duplicated work?
   * Does this help people act?
   * Does this improve source lineage?
   * Does this reveal important ecosystem context?
   * Does this help the Toolkit learn from implementation?
________________


10. Research Workflow
Step 1 — Capture
Add the question without trying to solve it immediately.
Step 2 — Route
Identify the relevant Toolkit layer.
Step 3 — Source
List possible source systems, people, communities, or resources.
Step 4 — Investigate
Use appropriate method:
   * desk research
   * source review
   * interviews
   * tool test
   * case review
   * workshop
   * ecosystem scan
   * implementation experiment
Step 5 — Synthesize
Produce an output that can update the Toolkit.
Step 6 — Update
Route the synthesis into:
   * Encyclopedia
   * Resource Graph
   * Concept & Idea Ecology
   * Option Library
   * Deployment
   * Implementation Memory
   * Infrastructure
   * Review & Maturity
Step 7 — Preserve uncertainty
If unresolved, keep the question visible.
________________


Working Summary
The Open Questions & Research Backlog protects the Toolkit from false closure.
It gives the commons a place to hold uncertainty, curiosity, tensions, missing research, and future work.
A strong backlog does not mean the Toolkit is incomplete in a bad way.
It means the Toolkit is alive enough to know what it does not yet know.


📑 Appendices & Templates
Purpose
This section collects reusable templates, field lists, relationship vocabularies, routing rules, and lightweight working structures used across the Toolkit.
The appendices should make the rest of the document easier to use.
They are not meant to be perfect or final. They are working materials that can be copied, adapted, simplified, or replaced as the Toolkit evolves.
________________


1. Core Object Types
The Toolkit should preserve a small set of core object types.
Object type
	Meaning
	Primary home
	Concept
	An idea, term, or meaning structure.
	Encyclopedia / Concept & Idea Ecology
	Framework
	A structured model for interpretation or action.
	Encyclopedia / Concept & Idea Ecology
	Resource
	A link, article, book, paper, dataset, video, guide, or artifact.
	Resource Graph
	Source System
	A living external knowledge environment, map, wiki, directory, forum, repo, or library.
	Resource Graph
	Organization
	A formal or semi-formal group.
	Resource Graph
	Network
	A distributed ecosystem or community field.
	Resource Graph
	Person
	A thinker, builder, steward, researcher, organizer, maintainer, or contributor.
	Resource Graph
	Tool
	A platform, app, protocol, library, or technical component.
	Resource Graph / Infrastructure
	Protocol
	A formal system, standard, or technical coordination layer.
	Resource Graph / Infrastructure
	Standard
	A shared specification, schema, or rule set.
	Ontology / Interoperability
	Option
	A reusable design choice or pattern.
	Option Library
	Deployment
	A context-specific assembly of options, tools, roles, boundaries, and operating structure.
	Deployment & Structural Integrity
	Implementation
	What happens when a deployment is used in practice.
	Implementation Memory

	An observation from use, review, conflict, feedback, or implementation.

	Material that supports, challenges, or contextualizes a claim.
	Implementation Memory / Review

	A repeated or transferable lesson.
	Option Library / Implementation Memory

	A recurring design that tends to create harm, fragility, confusion, or hidden cost.
	Implementation Memory / Evolution
	Open Question
	A question intentionally preserved for research or future sensemaking.
	Open Questions & Research Backlog
	Place
	A city, region, watershed, bioregion, territory, site, or local context.
	Resource Graph / Deployment
	Claim
	Something asserted about impact, function, evidence, status, or outcome.

2. Routing Rules
Use these rules to decide where something belongs.
If it explains meaning
Route to:
   * Encyclopedia
   * Concept & Idea Ecology
   * Glossary
Examples:
   * What is source lineage?
   * What is regenerative web3?
   * What is a local node?
   * What is structural integrity?
If it is a real thing in the world
Route to:
   * Resource Graph & Ecosystem Atlas
Examples:
   * OpenCivics
   * Bloom Network
   * Karma GAP
   * Hypercerts
   * Decidim
   * farmOS
   * a paper
   * an event
   * a dataset
   * a person
If it is a reusable design choice
Route to:
   * Option Library
Examples:
   * review queue
   * source lineage field
   * milestone-based funding
   * adverse signal channel
   * local discovery map
   * claim-evidence record
If it is an operational use case
Route to:
   * Deployment & Structural Integrity
Examples:
   * a specific funding round design
   * a local node setup
   * a public resource map launch
   * an AI-assisted discovery workflow
   * a community directory
If it already happened
Route to:
   * Implementation Memory
Examples:
   * a completed event
   * a grant round
   * a workshop
   * a local campaign
   * a failed governance process
   * a tool experiment
If it is a lesson from practice
Route to:
   * Implementation Memory
   * Evolution
   * Option Library
   * Anti-patterns
Examples:
   * a repeated failure mode
   * a useful adaptation
   * a pattern from multiple cases
   * a deployment risk discovered through use
If it is unresolved
Route to:
   * Open Questions & Research Backlog
   * Unresolved Tension Log
Examples:
   * Should public maps include local communities?
   * What is enough evidence for an ecological claim?
   * How should AI-assisted recommendations be reviewed?
________________

	Source system questions
   * Is this a living knowledge system or a single resource?
   * Who maintains it?
   * What worldview, field, or community does it represent?
   * What should be credited if we use it?
   * Are we citing, summarizing, adapting, or synthesizing from it?
   * Does it contain sensitive or community-specific knowledge?
   * Should we ask for context before using it heavily?
   * How can value return to the source system?

8. Structural Exposure Card
Use this when a deployment proceeds despite unresolved structural risk.
Field
	Response
	Deployment / object
	

	Exposure identified
	

	Affected parties
	

	Severity
	Low, medium, high, unknown.
	Why proceeding may still make sense
	

	Required disclosure
	

	Named steward
	

	Review date
	

	Possible response
	Revise, pause, narrow scope, add consent, add review, archive, continue with disclosure.
	Example exposures
   * resource map includes communities without confirmed consent
   * AI-assisted recommendations are active before review
   * funding round has unclear appeal path
   * ecological claims lack sufficient evidence
   * public directory has no correction path
   * deployment depends on one overloaded maintainer
   * source system is used without clear attribution
   * local project is represented by outsiders
   * governance process names participation but decision power remains elsewhere
________________

16. Review & Maturity States
Content maturity
State
	Meaning
	Raw
	Added for visibility; not yet organized.
	To-place
	Useful but not yet placed in the right section.
	Stub
	Minimal entry; needs expansion.
	Draft
	Developed enough to discuss; not fully reviewed.
	AI-assisted draft
	Created or substantially shaped with AI; needs human review.
	Source-linked
	Has visible references or source lineage.
	Candidate
	Likely useful; needs validation or review.
	Reviewed
	Checked by relevant human reviewers.
	Field-informed
	Informed by real implementation or practice.
	Pattern-generating
	Has produced reusable learning across contexts.
	Open question
	Intentionally unresolved.
	Deprecated
	Retained for memory but no longer current or recommended.
	Review types
Review type
	Useful for
	Source review
	Citations, links, provenance.
	Domain review
	Accuracy within a field.
	Structural review
	Roles, obligations, risks, coordination assumptions.
	Community review
	Representation of a group or community.
	Place-based review
	Local, cultural, ecological, or bioregional context.
	Legal / tax review
	Jurisdiction-sensitive claims.
	Ecological review
	Environmental, MRV, biodiversity, or restoration claims.
	AI review
	AI-assisted synthesis, classification, or recommendations.
	Implementation review
	Practice-generated lessons.
	________________


17. Relationship Grammar
Basic relationships
   * created by
   * maintained by
   * stewarded by
   * funded by
   * hosted by
   * contributed to
   * cited by
   * adapted from
   * inspired by
   * used by
   * partnered with
   * active in
   * located in
   * part of
   * related to
Functional relationships
   * supports
   * enables
   * provides infrastructure for
   * helps map
   * helps publish
   * helps coordinate
   * helps fund
   * helps verify
   * helps sensemake
   * helps discover
   * helps review
   * helps implement
   * helps preserve memory
Knowledge relationships
   * defines
   * explains
   * compares
   * challenges
   * documents
   * synthesizes
   * preserves lineage of
   * provides evidence for
   * raises question about
   * contradicts
   * extends
   * translates
   * contextualizes
Design relationships
   * instantiates option
   * supports option
   * depends on
   * requires
   * constrains
   * complements
   * conflicts with
   * replaces
   * can be composed with
   * incompatible with
   * safer with
   * risky without
Implementation relationships
   * implemented by
   * tested in
   * generated signal for
   * produced evidence for
   * failed under
   * adapted through
   * created pattern for
   * revealed risk in
   * generated open question for
Stewardship relationships
   * obligated toward
   * accountable to
   * reviewed by
   * consented by
   * represented by
   * can be corrected by
   * should credit
   * should consult
   * should not expose
   * requires local review
________________


18. Function Tags
Use function tags to describe what something helps people do.
Function
	Meaning
	Publish
	Make knowledge or resources visible.
	Store
	Hold data, records, files, or knowledge.
	Curate
	Select, organize, classify, or maintain knowledge.
	Search
	Help people find information.
	Map
	Visualize relationships, places, systems, or ecosystems.
	Explain
	Clarify concepts or frameworks.
	Convene
	Bring people together.
	Coordinate
	Organize action, roles, or work.
	Decide
	Support decision-making or governance / coordination.
	Fund
	Allocate money or resources.
	Verify
	Support claims, evidence, attestations, or review.
	Measure
	Support metrics, MRV, monitoring, or evaluation.
	Sensemake
	Interpret complexity, disagreement, or feedback.
	Discover
	Find resources, communities, collaborators, or opportunities.
	Match
	Connect needs, offers, skills, projects, or people.
	Protect
	Support privacy, security, consent, or safety.
	Learn
	Support education, onboarding, or learning paths.
	Remember
	Preserve history, lessons, and implementation memory.
	Evolve
	Support updates, adaptation, and learning loops.
	________________


19. Bridge Language Table
Some terms are useful for communication but should be clarified when structure matters.
Bridge language
	More precise structural language
	Governance
	Coordination architecture / coordination instrument
	Accountability
	Stewardship obligation / obligation direction
	Stakeholders
	Participants / contributors / affected parties / cost-bearing parties
	Transparency
	Multi-directional visibility
	Enforcement
	Detection with named response
	Compliance
	Conformance
	Feedback
	Signal / adverse signal / learning signal
	Users
	Participants / contributors / affected parties
	Power structure
	Power, control, and obligation structure
	Rules
	Structural conditions
	Impact
	Claim + evidence + context + uncertainty
	Community
	Specific group with boundaries, context, and representation questions
	Ecosystem
	Set of related actors, resources, concepts, tools, and environments
	Decentralization
	Specific distribution of control, authority, infrastructure, or risk
	Regeneration
	Contextual practice that should specify living system, place, relationship, and obligation
	________________

22. First-Pass Contributor Guide
Ways to contribute
A useful contribution can be small.
Examples:
   * add a resource
   * add a source system
   * add a concept
   * improve a definition
   * add a relationship
   * add source lineage
   * flag a weak claim
   * add an open question
   * add a failure mode
   * suggest a tool
   * add a case
   * improve a template
   * identify duplication
   * mark a risk
   * suggest a better category
Contribution questions
Before adding something, ask:
   * What is this?
   * Why does it matter?
   * Where does it belong?
   * What source should be credited?
   * What concepts does it connect to?
   * What function does it serve?
   * Does it involve people, communities, places, claims, or sensitive information?
   * Does it require review?
   * Is it a resource, concept, option, deployment, implementation, or open question?
Minimum contribution format
Field
	Response
	What I am adding
	

	Link / source
	

	Why it matters
	

	Suggested section
	

	Related concepts
	

	Any risks or review needs
	

	________________


23. One-Page Summary Template
Use this when a section, source system, option, or implementation needs a concise overview.
Field
	Response
	Name
	

	What it is
	

	Why it matters
	

	Key concepts
	

	Relevant resources
	

	Toolkit relevance
	

	Risks / tensions
	

	Open questions
	

	Next steps
	

	________________


24. Minimum Viable Commons Checklist
A lightweight knowledge commons should have:
   * clear purpose
   * clear audience
   * source of truth
   * contribution path
   * source lineage field
   * resource entry template
   * concept entry template
   * review / maturity labels
   * open-question area
   * maintenance rhythm
   * change log
   * export or backup path
   * named stewards
   * unresolved tension log
   * pathway for implementation learning
Optional but useful:
   * Resource Graph table
   * glossary
   * relationship grammar
   * Option Library
   * deployment checklist
   * implementation case template
   * AI-use notes
   * privacy and consent guidance
________________


25. Final Working Summary
These appendices are not separate from the Toolkit. They are reusable working instruments.
They help contributors:
   * classify entries
   * route content
   * preserve source lineage
   * compare options
   * prepare deployments
   * capture implementation memory
   * harvest patterns
   * document failure
   * update the commons
   * keep uncertainty visible
The templates should remain lightweight enough to use and structured enough to support future migration into tables, repositories, graphs, or other infrastructure.
